Detailed Reviews

Paper Reviews (A-E)

Reviewer A
Tech: 7.0 Nov: 4.0 Clar: 9.0 Sig: 7.0 Overall: 6.8
Technical Quality (7.0/10)
The paper is technically organized and more careful than many empirical submissions: the judge model, selection procedures, budgets, and several hyperparameters are explicitly specified, and the authors do include useful ablations on fallback handling, bias level, and descriptor capacity. Assumptions are stated candidly, especially the synthetic nature of the judge and the unequal information content between triplet and pairwise supervision. However, from a rigor-first perspective, the core empirical comparison still has material methodological weaknesses. The comparison is not fully fair because DIRECT consumes continuous pairwise distances while DISTILLED receives only triplet supervision, and the paper itself acknowledges this but does not resolve it. The central explanatory claim about compression of bias is plausible yet not formally established; the argument is mostly intuition plus synthetic trends. There is also an internal inconsistency between the full-coverage DIRECT headline in Table 1 (9.11) and the fallback table full-coverage entries (9.05 for every policy), which weakens confidence in result bookkeeping. Complexity is discussed at the level relevant to the contribution, but the evaluation remains limited to a narrow synthetic benchmark rather than a robust technical validation of the claim.
Novelty (4.0/10)
The paper's main novelty is limited to a targeted synthetic comparison between two already-motivated paradigms—distilled learned descriptors versus direct AI-judge querying—in the small-candidate FoO regime. That is a worthwhile empirical question, but it is closer to a conditional benchmarking study than a fundamentally new method or theory. The positioning against QDHF and QDAIF is understandable, but related-work coverage feels incomplete for metric learning, subset selection, and noisy-judge comparison methodologies, especially given that fairness of supervision channels is central here. Because the experimental comparison uses different supervision modalities and does not include stronger learned-metric alternatives beyond modest capacity ablations, the claim to advancement beyond incremental variation is weak. The novelty claims are, to the authors' credit, mostly restrained rather than exaggerated.
Clarity (9.0/10)
The paper is clearly written, well structured, and easy to follow. The introduction states the problem, contrast between distillation and direct judging, and the conditional nature of the claim with unusual directness. Methodological sections are concise but generally sufficient for an expert reader to reconstruct the setup, and the tables are integrated into the narrative with sensible interpretation rather than overclaiming. Notation is mostly consistent and definitions are introduced before use. The main clarity weakness is that there are no actual figures and the tabular presentation alone does not fully illuminate mechanisms such as pairwise coverage saturation or the geometry of descriptor compression; additionally, a few result discrepancies require the reader to pause and reconcile numbers. Still, overall exposition is strong.
Significance (7.0/10)
The question addressed is relevant: in practice, whether one should amortize judge feedback into a descriptor or query a judge directly is an important systems and methodology choice for quality-diversity style selection. The paper identifies a concrete regime where direct feedback may be preferable, and that could motivate useful follow-up work on judge calibration, richer distillation, and cost-aware selection. That said, the current evidence is synthetic and narrow, so the work does not yet demonstrate a substantial shift in practice or a large enough improvement to justify adoption broadly. Its significance is therefore more as a hypothesis-generating result and experimental design prompt than as a definitive advance. The topic is timely and likely to interest researchers working on LLM-mediated evaluation and diversity selection.
Overall Verdict (6.8/10)
This paper is a careful and readable synthetic study of a meaningful deployment question, and I appreciate the authors' explicit caveats and targeted ablations. However, under a stringent rigor standard, the central comparison is not methodologically clean enough to support a strong conclusion: the two paradigms are given different supervision granularity, the validation is entirely synthetic, and there is at least one numerical inconsistency across reported tables. I therefore view the main claim as plausible but not convincingly established. My recommendation is borderline negative in its current form, with the strongest path to acceptance being a tighter apples-to-apples comparison and more rigorous validation of the proposed mechanism.
Reviewer B
Tech: 7.0 Nov: 4.0 Clar: 9.0 Sig: 7.0 Overall: 6.8
Technical Quality (7.0/10)
The paper is technically organized and substantially reproducible for a synthetic study: the biased-judge model is explicit, the two selection paradigms are described clearly, and key hyperparameters, budgets, and ablations are reported. I also appreciate that the paper states its assumptions and foregrounds several limitations, especially the synthetic nature of the judge and the sensitivity of mid-budget DIRECT to missing-distance handling. However, the methodological comparison is not fully fair because the two paradigms are given different supervision modalities and information content (triplet labels for distillation versus continuous pairwise distances for direct judging), which weakens causal interpretation of the claimed advantage. In addition, the paper's central mechanism is argued plausibly but not demonstrated with sufficient rigor to exclude alternative explanations, so I do not view the work as a strong technical advance beyond a carefully constructed empirical benchmark.
Novelty (4.0/10)
From a novelty and literature-positioning perspective, I am not convinced this reaches a high bar. The core paper idea is essentially a synthetic comparison between two already articulated paradigms—distill the judge versus query the judge directly—within a FoO-sized candidate regime, under a particular biased-judge model. That is a useful empirical framing, but it feels more like a targeted deployment study or design note than a fundamentally new method or concept. The related-work coverage is serviceable but not exhaustive for the claims being made: the paper invokes metric learning, novelty/diversity selection, DPPs, and submodularity, yet does not deeply situate its greedy direct selector relative to the broader literature on budgeted set selection, active preference/query design, or alternative nonparametric ways to preserve pairwise structure without low-dimensional distillation. Comparisons are also not fully fair because the supervision channels differ, so the novelty claims should be read cautiously. To the authors' credit, the paper generally avoids the most extreme overclaiming and gives proper local credit to QDHF/QDAIF/FoO.
Clarity (9.0/10)
This is a clear and well-structured paper. The introduction states the problem, the competing design choices, and the conditional claim effectively, and the methods/results sections are easy to follow. Notation is mostly consistent and the tables are interpretable and well integrated into the narrative. My only clarity reservation is that there are no richer visualizations to help readers build intuition about the biased metric, the crossover regime, or the geometry of compression loss; for a paper centered on representational effects, such figures would have materially improved understanding.
Significance (7.0/10)
The problem is timely and relevant: if LLM judges are biased and candidate pools are modest, deciding whether to amortize or query online is practically important. The paper could influence how researchers think about AI-feedback diversity selection in small-pool settings, and it opens follow-up directions around judge calibration, richer descriptors, and interface-matched comparisons. That said, the demonstrated impact is limited by the synthetic setup and by the fact that the main positive result appears in a fairly specific regime—full or near-full pairwise saturation under a particular bias model. I therefore see the work as potentially useful and hypothesis-generating, but not yet as evidence for a broadly field-changing shift in practice.
Overall Verdict (6.8/10)
This paper is clear, disciplined, and asks a worthwhile question, but from a demanding novelty-and-literature standpoint I find the contribution narrower than the presentation sometimes suggests. The main result is a synthetic, regime-dependent comparison of two existing paradigms under an intentionally biased judge model, and the unequal information content of the queried signals makes the comparison less definitive than it first appears. Still, the paper usefully identifies a plausible deployment regime where direct AI feedback may outperform descriptor distillation, and it states its limitations more honestly than many papers do. My recommendation is borderline: interesting empirical note with practical relevance, but not a strong novelty contribution in its current form.
Reviewer C
Tech: 8.0 Nov: 5.0 Clar: 9.0 Sig: 7.0 Overall: 7.2
Technical Quality (8.0/10)
The paper is technically stronger than many short empirical studies: the biased-judge model is explicitly defined, assumptions are mostly surfaced, and the experimental setup includes enough concrete hyperparameters and budget accounting to make the study reasonably reproducible. I also appreciate the targeted ablations on fallback policy, judge bias, and descriptor capacity, which directly probe the central mechanism rather than serving as decorative experiments. However, I cannot call the methodology fully airtight. The comparison intentionally uses different supervision interfaces for the two paradigms, which weakens fairness; the study is synthetic and narrow; and the claim that distillation uniquely 'compresses bias' is plausible but not rigorously isolated from alternative explanations such as interface richness, training objective mismatch, or limited model class. So the technical story is solid and well argued, but not at the absolute highest standard.
Novelty (5.0/10)
The paper offers an interesting empirical angle: under small FoO candidate pools and biased judges, direct querying can outperform metric distillation at pairwise-saturated budgets. That conditional crossover is a useful finding. Still, as a novelty claim, this feels moderate rather than strong. The work mainly contrasts two already-motivated paradigms from prior literature in a synthetic benchmark tailored to the stated mechanism, rather than introducing a fundamentally new algorithmic framework. Related work coverage is adequate but not comprehensive enough for a top-tier novelty standard, especially around fair comparison between query modalities and broader diversity-selection literature. The novelty is real as an experimental observation, but it is closer to a focused study than a major conceptual advance.
Clarity (9.0/10)
This is the paper's strongest aspect. The problem framing is crisp, the conditional claim is stated early and repeated consistently, and the organization from setup to results to limitations is easy to follow. The prose is unusually disciplined: caveats are clearly marked, design choices are justified in context, and the tables are integrated into the narrative rather than dropped in without interpretation. Notation is mostly clean and lightweight, which helps readability. My main clarity reservation is presentation breadth: there are tables but no real figures or schematics to help readers build intuition for the geometry, budget regimes, or selection process, and some claims would benefit from visualization rather than verbal explanation alone.
Significance (7.0/10)
The topic is timely and relevant because LLM-based judging, diversity selection, and budget-aware deployment choices are all active concerns. The main practical message—do not automatically distill AI feedback when option pools are small and pairwise coverage is affordable—is useful and could influence how researchers design FoO-style systems or similar candidate-selection pipelines. That said, the significance is bounded by the paper's synthetic scope and by the fact that the strongest advantage appears in a fairly specific regime. The study opens worthwhile follow-up directions, particularly real-judge calibration and richer distillation baselines, but it does not yet establish a broadly transformative shift in practice.
Overall Verdict (7.2/10)
This is a clear, well-structured, and intellectually honest paper with a focused empirical contribution. Its strongest merit is communication quality: the conditional claim, the experimental logic, and the limitations are all presented unusually well. Technically, the study is careful and informative, but the evidence remains constrained by synthetic judges, unequal query interfaces, and somewhat limited fairness of the baseline comparison. My recommendation is mildly positive: the paper is worth considering for its clarity and its concrete design insight, but it does not yet reach the strongest bar for technical conclusiveness or novelty.
Reviewer D
Tech: 7.0 Nov: 4.0 Clar: 9.0 Sig: 7.0 Overall: 6.8
Technical Quality (7.0/10)
The paper is technically organized and unusually explicit about assumptions, budgets, and ablations. The synthetic judge model, budget accounting, fallback policies, and descriptor-capacity stress checks are all described clearly enough that an expert could reproduce the core study. I also appreciate that the authors explicitly narrow the claim to a synthetic regime and acknowledge unequal information content between triplets and pairwise distances. From a feasibility standpoint, the most useful part is the identification of a pairwise-saturation regime where direct judging may be operationally plausible for small FoO candidate pools. That said, I am not convinced the methodology fully supports the stronger practical implications. The central comparison is not fully fair because the two paradigms receive different query types and different effective information per call, while the distilled baseline is intentionally limited to relatively simple descriptor classes. The full-coverage DIRECT result is the strongest evidence, but it remains evidence in a stylized simulator whose deployment relevance is asserted rather than demonstrated. There is also a notable mismatch between the paper's feasibility framing and the lack of a more concrete resource analysis beyond query counts, such as latency, batching assumptions, or calibration cost in realistic judge pipelines. Overall, the work is careful and reasonably rigorous for a synthetic benchmark, but not strong enough to clear the highest bar for methodological soundness and practical implementation realism.
Novelty (4.0/10)
The paper's main contribution is a synthetic empirical comparison between direct AI-feedback selection and descriptor distillation under a biased-judge model, framed around FoO-scale budgets. That is a useful angle, but in novelty terms it feels more like a carefully targeted recombination of existing ideas from FoO, QDHF, and QDAIF than a fundamentally new method. The strongest contribution is the cost-bias crossover observation, not a new algorithmic advance. I also do not think the related-work positioning is fully comprehensive for a claim centered on practical diversity selection under noisy or biased similarity signals. The paper cites several relevant lines, but the comparison space remains somewhat selective, and fairness concerns in the baseline setup weaken the strength of any novelty claim derived from the empirical outcome. The authors avoid major overclaiming, which helps, but the paper still reads as an insightful ablation study more than a clearly state-of-the-art conceptual advance.
Clarity (9.0/10)
The paper is clearly written, well structured, and easy to follow. The introduction states the problem, the competing paradigms, and the conditional thesis very directly. Methods are laid out in a disciplined way, and the discussion repeatedly distinguishes what is demonstrated from what is not. This is especially important given the synthetic nature of the setup, and the authors generally handle that responsibly. Tables are readable and integrated into the narrative, and the interpretation of results is appropriately cautious. The only notable weakness is that there are no richer visualizations to help readers build intuition for the geometry of the bias/compression tradeoff; everything is table-driven. That is acceptable, but it slightly limits accessibility for adjacent audiences trying to understand why DIRECT benefits under certain regimes.
Significance (7.0/10)
This paper addresses a relevant question: when should one amortize diversity judgments into a learned metric versus query an AI judge directly at selection time? That design choice matters for systems that rely on small candidate sets and expensive feedback, and the paper contributes a concrete regime map that practitioners could use as a decision heuristic. The work is timely given current interest in LLM-as-a-judge and feedback-efficient diversity selection. However, the practical significance is constrained by the synthetic setup and the absence of an end-to-end deployment study. The reported gains are meaningful within the benchmark, but they are not yet enough to justify broad adoption because the real bottlenecks in implementation—judge calibration, latency, batching, failure modes, and drift—are not evaluated in the paper body. I do think the paper can stimulate useful follow-up work, especially real-judge calibration studies and more realistic cost models, but its immediate field-changing impact is limited.
Overall Verdict (6.8/10)
This is a careful and clearly written synthetic study with a practically relevant question and a credible conditional finding: for small FoO option pools and sufficiently high pairwise-query budgets, direct AI-feedback selection can outperform low-to-moderate-capacity distillation under biased judges. I appreciate the authors' restraint in limiting claims and their inclusion of missing-distance and capacity ablations. My main concern, from a feasibility and implementation perspective, is that the result is still too simulator-dependent and the baseline comparison too uneven to support a strong practical recommendation without real judge calibration and richer resource analysis. I would view this as a worthwhile but not yet definitive paper: promising evidence for a deployment-relevant regime, but not strong enough to endorse broad conclusions.
Reviewer E
Tech: 7.0 Nov: 5.0 Clar: 8.0 Sig: 5.0 Overall: 6.2
Technical Quality (7.0/10)
The paper is technically organized and substantially more careful than many short empirical notes. The judge model is explicit, assumptions are stated, budgets are quantified, and the ablations on missing distances, bias, and descriptor capacity are useful. Reproducibility is reasonably strong from the paper body because the setup, hyperparameters, and evaluation protocol are largely specified. However, I do not find the methodology fully free of major concerns: the comparison intentionally uses unequal information per query, the evaluation remains synthetic, and the selector is not a full quality-aware QD optimizer. Those choices do not invalidate the study, but they materially limit how strongly one can interpret the claimed advantage. Overall, this is competent empirical work with clear caveats, but not a technically definitive demonstration.
Novelty (5.0/10)
The work offers a new empirical comparison in a specific regime rather than a fundamentally new method. Its most interesting contribution is the conditional finding that direct AI judging can outperform distilled metrics in small-pool FoO-style selection under biased judges and saturated pairwise budgets. That is a useful framing, but it is still close to a design-study or stress-test built from existing ingredients rather than a major conceptual advance. I also do not think the related-work positioning is fully comprehensive enough to support strong novelty claims, especially around broader diversity-selection and metric-learning alternatives. The novelty is real at the level of the reported crossover result, but modest overall.
Clarity (8.0/10)
The manuscript is easy to follow and communicates its central question effectively. The introduction motivates the design choice clearly, the methods section defines the two paradigms crisply, and the results are interpreted with appropriate caution. Notation is mostly consistent and the writing is concise without becoming cryptic. The main clarity weakness is presentation: there are tables but no actual figures or richer visual diagnostics, and some claims about mechanisms would benefit from more direct visual evidence. Still, for an expert reader, the paper is generally clear and well structured.
Significance (5.0/10)
From an impact standpoint, the problem is timely and relevant: choosing between distilling feedback and querying judges directly is an important systems question for AI-assisted diversity selection. The paper also identifies a concrete regime that could motivate follow-up work on calibration, budget allocation, and biased-judge modeling. That said, I am not convinced the present study by itself has broad field-shaping significance. The evidence is synthetic, tied to a narrow FoO option-layer setting, and the practical adoption case remains weak until validated with real LLM judges and more realistic task distributions. As a result, the paper is better viewed as a useful hypothesis-generating study than as a result likely to change practice on its own.
Overall Verdict (6.2/10)
This paper presents a clear and reasonably rigorous synthetic study of when direct AI-feedback querying may outperform descriptor distillation in Flow-of-Options diversity selection. Its strongest contribution is not a new algorithm but a conditional deployment insight: when candidate pools are small, judge bias is present, and pairwise saturation is affordable, direct querying can be competitive or better. I appreciate the explicit limitations and the relevant ablations, but the significance is constrained by the synthetic judge model, unequal query information, and narrow task framing. My recommendation is borderline positive as an exploratory empirical paper, but not strong acceptance if the venue prioritizes broad impact and externally validated conclusions.

Code Review (F)

Reviewer F
Tech: 5.0 Repro: 7.0 Correct: 7.0 Align: 10.0 Overall: 7.2
Code Technical Quality (5.0/10)
The artifact is thoughtfully structured for a research code submission: concerns are separated into data generation, judge modeling, selection methods, reporting, and tests, with strong use of dataclasses, type hints, and explanatory docstrings. The bundle is unusually self-contained and the CLI entry points are clear. However, it does not meet stricter production-quality expectations: style compliance is not demonstrated with linting, dependencies are not pinned, some performance choices are quadratic and recompute distances repeatedly, and the runtime file-writing bundle approach is harder to maintain than a normal package layout. Input validation and logging are reasonably good, but I would not call the implementation bug-free or enterprise-maintainable.
Code Reproducibility (7.0/10)
Reproducibility is a strong point. The submission provides a single-file bundle, deterministic NumPy seeding, self-tests, machine-readable JSON reports with environment metadata and stable digests, and commands for the main ablations. For a synthetic benchmark, the needed resources are effectively included, and the outputs appear intended to be replayable. The main gaps are environment specification and configuration packaging: there is no container/lockfile, no exact dependency pinning, and no separate machine-readable config files beyond hard-coded dataclasses and CLI defaults. Still, for a lightweight research artifact, this is substantially better than average.
Code Correctness (7.0/10)
The implementation is generally careful and internally consistent. Function signatures are coherent, validations cover many bad inputs, numerical guards are present for normalization and degenerate correlations, and the included tests target key invariants such as budget accounting and determinism. That said, I cannot mark correctness as complete: there are some logic choices that weaken confidence, notably the use of two noisy pairwise calls for a single triplet comparison, the possible mismatch between claimed full-coverage call accounting and actual greedy query behavior, and the lack of guarantees against all runtime/pathological cases. The code looks solid for a simulation study, but not proven correct for all inputs.
Code-Paper Alignment (10.0/10)
The code aligns very well with the claimed contribution. It directly implements the two compared paradigms, the biased judge model, equal-budget sweeps, fallback-policy ablations, capacity ablations, and the stated evaluation metrics. Terminology in code closely matches the paper, and the CLI entry points map naturally onto the reported experiments. As a result, the artifact is genuinely useful for validating the paper’s core synthetic claims.
Overall Verdict (7.2/10)
This is a strong research artifact for a synthetic code paper: it is self-contained, reproducible, well documented, and closely aligned with the claimed experiments. Its main strengths are deterministic execution, explicit ablation support, and a clear mapping from paper concepts to implementation. The main weaknesses are lack of pinned/containerized environment specification, some maintainability compromises from the bundle format, and a few correctness/performance issues that keep it below production-grade. Recommendation: accept on code grounds as a solid reproducible research implementation, with minor concerns about rigor at the software-engineering level.

Extension Review (G)

Reviewer G
Ext: 9.0 Overall: 9.0
Extension Quality (9.0/10)
The paper is a strong and clearly articulated extension of Flow-of-Options. It explicitly situates itself as addressing a specific unresolved issue in FoO-style diversity handling: structural branching creates options but does not itself verify meaningful behavioral diversity. The proposed contribution preserves continuity with the reference paper by staying inside the FoO option-selection setting, while extending it with a cost-aware comparison between two ways of operationalizing diversity feedback: distilled descriptors versus direct judge-time pairwise querying. This is more than an application tweak; it reframes a key downstream design choice for FoO layers and provides a concrete representational argument for when distillation helps versus when it can entrench judge bias. The synthetic judge model, budget accounting, fallback-policy ablations, bias sweeps, and descriptor-capacity checks all add substantive evidence beyond the reference work. The only notable limitation as an extension is that it does not expand FoO into a genuinely new task domain or real deployed benchmark; instead, it deepens one component of FoO within a controlled simulation. Even so, for readers interested in how FoO should actually select diverse options under realistic cost and bias constraints, this is a valuable and conceptually coherent next step.
Overall Verdict (9.0/10)
This is a substantive and well-targeted extension of Flow-of-Options. Its main strength is continuity: it focuses on a real open design question left by FoO’s structural notion of diversity and develops a principled comparison grounded in cost, bias, and representational compression. While the evidence is synthetic rather than end-to-end on real FoO tasks or real LLM judges, the contribution is still meaningful because it sharpens when direct AI feedback may outperform learned diversity metrics in FoO option layers. I would regard it as a valuable follow-on paper with strong extension quality, though its claims should remain framed as conditional until validated in real FoO deployments.

Detailed Reviews

Paper Reviews (A-E)

Reviewer A
Tech: 7.0 Nov: 5.0 Clar: 10.0 Sig: 4.0 Overall: 6.5
Technical Quality (7.0/10)
The paper is unusually explicit for a small empirical study: candidate laws, posterior surrogate, selection rules, metrics, noise levels, seed counts, and the misspecification setting are all written down clearly. The protocol is reproducible at the level of the paper body, and the ablation between predictive-variance, gate-only, and mixed scoring is useful. However, from a rigor-first perspective there are important methodological weaknesses. The claimed Bayesian/active-learning connections are only heuristic because the maintained weights are a temperature-softmax over cumulative squared residuals rather than a properly derived posterior under the stated Gaussian noise model; no justification is given for tau=0.32 relative to sigma, and this matters because policy behavior depends directly on these weights. The paired-difference inference is also not fully sound as presented: the text calls the z-test conservative relative to a paired t-test at n=10, which is generally backwards if population variance is unknown and estimated from the same small sample. In addition, there is no complexity discussion despite policies having different scoring burdens over the control pool and committee. Thus the paper is technically decent but not at the standard of airtight correctness.
Novelty (5.0/10)
The paper is forthright that its contribution is narrow and primarily empirical, which is good. In substance, however, the methods are mostly straightforward instantiations or recombinations of standard ideas: random sampling, greedy confirmation, committee disagreement, top-versus-runner-up discrimination, and a fixed convex combination thereof. The main finding is that several disagreement-aware variants behave similarly on a toy finite-hypothesis benchmark and fail under misspecification. That is a useful empirical observation, but it is not a major methodological advance. I am also not convinced the related-work coverage is comprehensive enough for such claims about BALD/MacKay equivalence and discriminating-experiment selection; several adjacent active experimental design and Bayesian model discrimination traditions seem underconnected.
Clarity (10.0/10)
The paper is very clearly written. The problem statement, benchmark, five policies, and evaluation setup are easy to follow, and the tables align well with the textual claims. Notation is mostly consistent and every policy objective is stated explicitly. The limitations under misspecification are also communicated candidly and prominently, which improves interpretability. Even though I have technical reservations about some modeling choices, the exposition itself is strong and accessible to an expert reader.
Significance (4.0/10)
The study addresses a relevant question for branching scientific agents—how to allocate measurements rather than merely expand hypotheses—but the demonstrated impact is limited by the synthetic, very small, closed-world benchmark. The strongest practical takeaway is the misspecification caveat, which is valuable, but the rest of the contribution mainly confirms that disagreement-based selection beats confirmation-style probing when the truth is in a tiny candidate set. Because the setup is highly stylized and no broader theoretical guarantee is provided, I do not see this as a substantial advance that would likely change practice on its own. It may inspire follow-up work on robust experiment selection under misspecification, but its direct significance is modest.
Overall Verdict (6.5/10)
This is a clear and well-scoped empirical note with useful ablations and an honest misspecification warning, but it falls short of high rigor. The central technical concern is that the paper treats a temperature-softmax over residual losses as a posterior-like object without deriving or justifying it from the stated observation model, while downstream policy conclusions depend on that choice. The statistical testing language is also not fully careful for such a small number of seeds. I would view this as a competent exploratory benchmark paper rather than a technically definitive study; borderline at best from a rigor-auditing standpoint.
Reviewer B
Tech: 6.0 Nov: 3.0 Clar: 10.0 Sig: 4.0 Overall: 5.8
Technical Quality (6.0/10)
The paper is generally well specified at the level of equations, setup, and protocol: the candidate laws, posterior surrogate, and five acquisition rules are stated clearly, and the experimental setup is detailed enough to reproduce the benchmark. I also credit the authors for including an explicit misspecification experiment and for reporting uncertainty summaries. However, I do not find the technical standard fully convincing. The posterior is not a probabilistically justified posterior under the stated Gaussian noise model, but rather a temperature-softmax over squared residuals with an unexplained tau lacking derivation or sensitivity analysis. Statistical testing is also weaker than it should be: a z-test with n=10 seeds is not standard here, and the analysis is conducted only on per-seed aggregates from a highly synthetic setting. The baseline set is narrow relative to the active-learning and sequential-design literature invoked, with no comparison to expected information gain, entropy reduction, Thompson-style finite-hypothesis sampling, or optimal finite-hypothesis policies suggested by the cited theory. There is a small ablation in gate-only versus variance-only versus combined, but there is no validation of the fixed 0.25 mixture weight or of tau. Overall, this is competent empirical benchmarking on a toy environment, but not a technically strong advance.
Novelty (3.0/10)
From a novelty and literature-positioning perspective, this paper is modest at best. The paper itself admits that option-flow is essentially a finite-hypothesis predictive-variance/disagreement criterion in the spirit of MacKay and BALD, and that the falsification gate is simply the top-versus-runner-up disagreement heuristic. The principal empirical conclusion is that three closely related disagreement-aware heuristics are nearly interchangeable on this benchmark, while a confirmatory greedy rule is worse when the true law is in-class and better under misspecification. That is a useful sanity check, but not a genuinely new algorithmic contribution. My larger concern is literature coverage and positioning. The citations are respectable but incomplete for the exact problem framing: finite-hypothesis experiment selection, query-by-committee/disagreement-based active learning, Bayesian experimental design, and misspecification-robust design all have richer literatures than acknowledged. The paper does not compare against several natural baselines strongly suggested by its own related-work section, which weakens both novelty claims and positioning against prior art. The novelty claims are somewhat restrained in tone, but the title and framing around 'Falsification-Gated Flow-of-Options' still overpackage what is effectively a small synthetic ablation study of known ideas.
Clarity (10.0/10)
The paper is clearly written and unusually easy to follow. The introduction states the thesis, the policies are written as explicit equations, the experimental protocol is concrete, and the results are summarized in a straightforward way. Tables are interpretable, the figures are referenced appropriately, and the discussion of the central caveat under misspecification is candid. Notation is mostly consistent and the organization from framing to setup to results is logical. Even though I am critical of the paper's novelty and breadth, I have few complaints about presentation quality.
Significance (4.0/10)
The paper addresses a relevant problem—measurement allocation under multi-hypothesis agentic reasoning—and the negative misspecification result is arguably the most valuable part. However, the significance is limited by the extremely narrow synthetic setting: only five hand-designed candidate laws, a small fixed grid, eight total observations per trial, and a softmax scoring rule that is not tied to a principled Bayesian model. Because the study is so stylized and because the main positive result is that several standard disagreement heuristics behave similarly, I do not see substantial evidence that this work would materially shift practice or theory. The misspecification caveat may inspire follow-up work on robustness, but the present paper does not itself deliver a broadly influential method or a strong new empirical phenomenon.
Overall Verdict (5.8/10)
This is a cleanly written and candid empirical note, but it is not, in my view, a strong novelty contribution. The study repackages well-known disagreement-based and confirmation-style selection heuristics in a tiny finite-hypothesis synthetic benchmark and finds that several of them perform similarly in-class while degrading under misspecification. That negative caveat is worthwhile, yet the literature coverage is not sufficiently comprehensive, the baseline set is too limited relative to the cited prior art, and the algorithmic advance is minimal. I would therefore lean reject for a venue expecting meaningful methodological novelty, while noting that the paper could become a useful workshop-style ablation or robustness note if better positioned and more broadly benchmarked.
Reviewer C
Tech: 7.0 Nov: 4.0 Clar: 9.0 Sig: 7.0 Overall: 6.8
Technical Quality (7.0/10)
The paper is unusually explicit for a short empirical study: the candidate laws, posterior surrogate, five policies, protocol, and statistics are all written down clearly, and the misspecification condition is a useful limitation check. Reproducibility of the body-level method description is strong, and the ablation separating variance, gate-only, and mixture is appropriate. However, I do not think the technical standard reaches the highest bar. The posterior is a temperature-softmax over cumulative squared residuals without a probabilistic derivation or calibration argument, the paired z-test with n=10 is only loosely justified, and complexity/scaling considerations are absent despite the paper being about experiment-selection policies. More importantly, the claimed contribution is modest and mostly benchmark-specific: the study convincingly shows that disagreement helps on this toy finite-hypothesis setup, but it does not establish that the proposed gated flow rule meaningfully advances beyond existing committee/disagreement heuristics.
Novelty (4.0/10)
The paper is honest that its contribution is empirical and narrow, which I view positively. Still, under a strict novelty standard, this is limited. The main policies are close to familiar query-by-committee/predictive-disagreement ideas, and the proposed falsification gate is essentially a top-versus-runner-up disagreement heuristic with a fixed convex combination. The empirical finding that gate-only, predictive variance, and their mixture behave similarly on this benchmark is useful, but it is more a calibration/ablation result than a distinctly new method. I also do not think the related-work positioning is fully comprehensive at the absolute highest level for sequential design under model misspecification, and the fairness claims relative to broader active-learning/state-of-the-art practice remain somewhat narrow because all comparisons occur within a synthetic five-law environment designed by the authors.
Clarity (9.0/10)
This is the strongest aspect of the paper. The introduction states the problem, setup, findings, and caveat very clearly; the methods section is well organized and uses consistent notation; and the results are easy to follow. I especially appreciated the direct equations for every policy and the candid statement that the empirical contribution is narrow. Tables are readable and the prose does a good job connecting claims to numbers. My main clarity reservation is the figure quality/effectiveness: the figures are referenced and interpretable in principle, but the presentation is not at the highest standard because the embedded images are visually cluttered, one figure is effectively a per-seed heatmap without much explanatory value, and the paper relies more on tables than on genuinely illuminating visualization design.
Significance (7.0/10)
The topic is relevant: experiment selection for branching scientific agents and finite-hypothesis falsification is timely, and the misspecification reversal is a practically important warning. The observed gains over greedy/random are large within the in-class synthetic setup, so the empirical message is not trivial. That said, significance is constrained by the benchmark design. The candidate set is tiny and hand-crafted, the control pool is only 75 points, and the conclusions are explicitly contingent on the true law being in the hypothesis class. As a result, I see this as a useful cautionary empirical note and a clean pedagogical benchmark result, but not yet a substantial field-shaping advance with broad demonstrated applicability.
Overall Verdict (6.8/10)
This is a clearly written and admirably candid empirical paper. Its main strengths are communication quality, explicit formalization of the benchmark/policies, and the useful demonstration that disagreement-based selection helps in-class but can hurt under misspecification. My main reservations are that the technical framing relies on a lightly justified posterior surrogate, the study is benchmark-bound, and the novelty of the proposed gated policy over known disagreement heuristics is limited. I would view this as a solid, readable workshop-style empirical study rather than a strong full-paper technical advance.
Reviewer D
Tech: 6.0 Nov: 4.0 Clar: 10.0 Sig: 4.0 Overall: 6.0
Technical Quality (6.0/10)
The paper is formalized clearly and is unusually explicit about policy equations, posterior weighting, dataset construction, and evaluation protocol. From a reproducibility standpoint, the synthetic setup is specified in enough detail to rerun the study. The strongest technical asset is candor: the paper openly reports a misspecification failure mode and includes a gate-only ablation that usefully sharpens the main empirical claim. However, applying a stricter implementation- and feasibility-oriented standard, I do not find the methodology fully robust. The posterior is a softmax over cumulative squared residuals with a fixed temperature but without a principled probabilistic derivation or sensitivity analysis for that temperature, and the fixed 0.25 mixture weight in the gated-flow policy is not justified beyond implementation convenience. More importantly, the statistical treatment is weaker than claimed: a z-test with n=10 seed means is not especially compelling, and there is no discussion of power or sensitivity to the seed-level aggregation choice. The baseline set is also narrow for an active-experiment-selection paper; in particular, there is no comparison to information gain, entropy, BALD-style approximations, uncertainty sampling variants, or cost-aware acquisition rules, so it is hard to judge whether the reported gains are meaningful outside this toy benchmark. Finally, there is essentially no complexity or deployment analysis, despite the paper’s framing around agentic scientific experimentation. Because the contribution is purely empirical on a tiny discrete pool with five hand-coded hypotheses, I view the technical advancement as limited.
Novelty (4.0/10)
Novelty is modest. The paper itself admits that the connection to predictive disagreement, query-by-committee, MacKay-style variance selection, and a top-vs-runner-up falsification gate is narrow and empirical. I agree with that self-assessment. The proposed policies are simple recombinations of familiar selection heuristics in a very small finite-hypothesis setting, and the main result is essentially a benchmark observation that variance and runner-up disagreement behave similarly here. That is a useful empirical note, but not a substantial methodological innovation. I also find the related-work positioning incomplete for the strength of the novelty framing: there is discussion of classic active learning and sequential testing, but the comparison set does not engage deeply with modern Bayesian experimental design or more practically relevant adaptive design literature. So while the paper gives appropriate credit in several places and does not wildly overclaim, the actual novelty remains limited and somewhat under-supported by the comparison scope.
Clarity (10.0/10)
The paper is very clearly written. The problem statement, policies, data-generating laws, metrics, and main conclusions are easy to follow, and the narrative structure is coherent from introduction through methods to caveats. Notation is consistent and lightweight, which is appropriate for this setting. The tables are readable and the text directly interprets them rather than merely restating numbers. The central claim—that disagreement-aware selection dominates greedy confirmation when the true law is in-class but loses under misspecification—is conveyed plainly and repeatedly. Even readers outside the exact subarea should be able to understand what was tested and what was found.
Significance (4.0/10)
The problem area—how to allocate experiments among competing hypotheses—is certainly relevant. However, the practical significance of this study is constrained by the benchmark’s extreme simplicity: five known candidate laws, a fixed pool of only 75 controls, eight total measurements per trial, and a synthetic misspecification case that is still quite stylized. From a feasibility and implementation perspective, this leaves a substantial gap between the paper’s framing around agentic scientific experimentation and what is actually demonstrated. The main positive result is large on this benchmark, but adoption implications are weak because the method fails under class misspecification and no realistic monitoring, recovery, or robustness mechanism is provided beyond a verbal warning. I do think the misspecification finding is valuable and could motivate follow-up research on robust acquisition under model-class error, but the present study by itself is unlikely to change practice or deployment decisions.
Overall Verdict (6.0/10)
This is a clear, honest, and competently executed synthetic study, but it remains a limited empirical note rather than a strong advance. The main finding is easy to understand and the explicit misspecification caveat is a real strength, yet the benchmark is too small and stylized to support broad conclusions about practical experiment selection in agentic science. From a feasibility-oriented perspective, the absence of stronger baselines, robustness analysis, sensitivity analysis, and deployment-relevant complexity discussion materially weakens the paper. I would view this as potentially publishable only in a venue that values concise empirical clarifications, not as a high-impact methodological contribution.
Reviewer E
Tech: 6.0 Nov: 4.0 Clar: 10.0 Sig: 4.0 Overall: 6.0
Technical Quality (6.0/10)
The paper is unusually explicit in formalization: candidate laws, posterior weights, and all five policies are stated cleanly, and the experimental protocol is detailed enough to reproduce the simulator. The empirical study is internally coherent and the paper commendably discusses a key caveat—misspecification—rather than hiding it. However, I cannot rate the technical quality highly. Novelty (4.0/10)
The paper's most plausible novelty lies in the specific empirical comparison and the observation that, on this benchmark, committee predictive variance and top-vs-runner-up disagreement behave nearly interchangeably while both fail under misspecification. That said, the underlying policies are not new, the falsification-gated combination is a lightweight heuristic, and the paper itself repeatedly frames the contribution as narrow and empirical. Overall Verdict (6.0/10)

But the contribution is narrow: the benchmark is highly synthetic, the baselines are limited, and the central method appears more heuristic than genuinely new. My recommendation is weak: useful as a compact calibration study, but not yet convincing as a broadly significant advance.

Code Review (F)

Reviewer F
Tech: 3.0 Repro: 5.0 Correct: 7.0 Align: 10.0 Overall: 6.2
Code Technical Quality (3.0/10)
The code is reasonably well-structured for a compact research simulator: policies, losses, posterior updates, trial execution, aggregation, and reporting are cleanly separated, names are mostly clear, and the implementation is readable. It also has useful docstrings and avoids unnecessary dependencies. However, it is not production-grade: there are no type annotations, minimal validation, limited exception handling, no tests or CI, and documentation does not include complexity, edge-case discussion, or usage examples. Style appears mostly good, but there is no evidence of linting or stronger maintainability infrastructure.
Code Reproducibility (5.0/10)
Reproducibility is one of the stronger aspects. The code is self-contained, standard-library only, and runnable as a single Python script. Seeds are explicitly fixed, synthetic data generation is embedded, and outputs are machine-readable JSON, which makes the main numerical claims plausibly reproducible from the provided code alone. Still, the environment is not fully specified, there is no container or lockfile, no config file externalizing parameters, and no validation/checksum tooling for outputs. So this is good practical reproducibility, but not fully specified archival reproducibility.
Code Correctness (7.0/10)
The implementation appears broadly correct and consistent with the described simulator. The candidate laws, posterior weighting, policy scoring rules, adaptive loop, metrics, bootstrap intervals, paired tests, and misspecification setup all line up coherently. Numerical choices are sensible, including a stabilized softmax. I did notice some weaker areas: lack of type annotations, little explicit handling of unusual/degenerate inputs, and no formal validation that every possible input configuration is safe. Within the intended problem domain, though, the code is likely to run correctly and produce the reported tables.
Code-Paper Alignment (10.0/10)
The code aligns very well with the paper’s experimental claims. The five candidate laws, the five policies, the posterior definition, the control pool, the observation budget, the noise sweep, the misspecification experiment, the metrics, and the paired statistical comparisons are all present and directly mapped to the paper terminology. Variable and function names mirror the paper closely, and the JSON output structure is clearly designed to support the reported quantitative results. As a paper-code match, this is strong.
Overall Verdict (6.2/10)
The submission provides a compact, readable, and largely self-contained simulator that appears to implement the paper’s main empirical study faithfully. Its strongest aspect is paper-code alignment and practical reproducibility: a reviewer can plausibly rerun the study from the provided script and inspect the exact policy logic. The main weaknesses are software-engineering depth rather than research fidelity—there are no tests, no environment pinning, limited defensive programming, and only moderate documentation. Overall, this is good research code for a synthetic study, though not a fully hardened reproducibility artifact.

Extension Review (G)

Reviewer G
Ext: 9.0 Overall: 9.0
Extension Quality (9.0/10)
This submission is a strong and coherent extension of Flow-of-Options. It explicitly positions itself as addressing an underspecified part of the reference framework: once multiple option branches exist, how should experimental budget be allocated among candidate discriminating tests? That is a real continuity point rather than a superficial citation. The paper preserves the core FoO intuition of maintaining competing hypotheses/options, but narrows the focus to experiment selection and compares concrete policies derived from disagreement, top-vs-runner-up falsification, and confirmatory single-path probing. This is a meaningful methodological extension because it operationalizes a missing decision rule inside the FoO paradigm and evaluates it systematically. The work also contributes new empirical evidence through controlled seed-averaged experiments, bootstrap intervals, paired tests, ablations, and a misspecification stress test. Its most valuable contribution is not just a positive result, but a calibrated negative one: disagreement-aware policies help substantially in the closed-world setting, yet lose their advantage under misspecification. That caveat directly sharpens the applicability boundaries of FoO-style exploration. The main weakness from an extension standpoint is that the theoretical framework is not substantially advanced beyond a finite-hypothesis reinterpretation of variance/disagreement criteria; the paper is primarily empirical rather than theoretical. Still, as a continuity-preserving, targeted extension of FoO's option-selection problem, it is substantive and likely useful to readers of the original paper.
Overall Verdict (9.0/10)
This paper is a valuable next-step extension of Flow-of-Options. It identifies a genuine gap in the original framework—how to choose the next experiment among multiple active options—and studies that question with clear policies, ablations, and careful statistical reporting. I find the continuity with the reference paper strong and the novelty real, especially in the misspecification result that qualifies when disagreement-driven selection is beneficial. My only reservation is that the advance is mainly empirical and algorithmic rather than theoretical, but within that scope it is a meaningful and worthwhile extension.

Detailed Reviews

Paper Reviews (A-E)

Reviewer A
Tech: 5.0 Nov: 2.0 Clar: 8.0 Sig: 7.0 Overall: 5.5
Technical Quality (5.0/10)
The paper is stronger on formal setup than on rigor of validation. The synthetic benchmark is clearly defined, the search scoring rule is stated cleanly, and key hyperparameters are reported. However, several central theoretical claims are not actually proved: propositions and corollaries are mostly post hoc interpretations of a stylized Bernoulli model rather than formal consequences of the algorithm. I also see important methodological weaknesses. The synthetic task is highly constructed in a way that favors the proposed lookahead mechanism, and the claim that the recorded path is the unique ground-truth retrosynthetic path is not established from the grammar definition alone. In Part II, the evidence base is very small (five targets), the reward definition is indirect, and comparisons across FoO-LLM, CoT-LLM, and Random-LLM are not fully fair in search budget or objective alignment. Complexity is discussed only through node counts, without substantive analysis. Overall, the paper is technically interesting but falls short of the highest standards of correctness and methodological soundness.
Novelty (2.0/10)
The application framing is somewhat fresh, especially the decoupled comparison between exact-option synthetic search and LLM-generated chemistry options. That said, the core method is largely a straightforward beam-search/lookahead instantiation of an existing Flow-of-Options idea, and the paper does not convincingly establish a substantial methodological advance beyond adapting that perspective to retrosynthesis. Related-work positioning is limited: foundational retrosynthesis and reasoning papers are cited, but the discussion omits broader search/planning baselines and more direct retrosynthesis planning comparisons that would be needed to substantiate novelty. The novelty claims are also somewhat overstated relative to the actual evidence, especially when the main empirical conclusion is that option quality dominates search behavior.
Clarity (8.0/10)
The manuscript is generally well written and easy to follow. The introduction states the problem, the two-part experimental design, and the intended takeaway clearly. Notation in the formalized benchmark is mostly consistent, and the progression from algorithm to synthetic study to chemistry study is logically organized. The results tables are interpretable and the narrative around them is readable. My main clarity reservations are presentation-related: the paper references propositions, corollaries, and a proposition P1 without proofs or explicit theorem statements, and it mentions figures/visual signatures while providing only tables in the supplied text. Thus, while the exposition is strong, some claimed analytical structure is not fully documented in the body.
Significance (7.0/10)
The problem is important: understanding whether search or option generation is the main bottleneck in LLM-guided retrosynthesis is timely and relevant. The controlled synthetic benchmark could be useful as a diagnostic framework, and the negative result in real chemistry is potentially valuable because it isolates a failure mode that many systems likely face. However, the demonstrated practical impact is limited by the small-scale chemistry evaluation and by the fact that the real-world setup does not yet show a compelling improvement over simpler alternatives. I therefore view the work as potentially useful for follow-up research, but not yet as a substantial advance likely to change practice on its current evidence.
Overall Verdict (5.5/10)
This paper presents a clear and potentially useful decomposition of search quality versus option-generator quality in retrosynthesis, and the controlled synthetic benchmark is the strongest part of the submission. However, from a rigor standpoint, the theoretical propositions are not proved, several claims are stronger than the evidence supports, and the real-chemistry evaluation is too limited and methodologically uneven to justify broad conclusions. I find the core intuition plausible, but the current paper does not yet meet a high standard for technical validation. My recommendation is borderline reject in its current form, with encouragement to strengthen proofs, fairness of baselines, and scale and rigor of chemical evaluation.
Reviewer B
Tech: 6.0 Nov: 0.0 Clar: 8.0 Sig: 7.0 Overall: 5.2
Technical Quality (6.0/10)
The paper is reasonably formalized and reproducible at the level of algorithm description, synthetic benchmark construction, and reported hyperparameters. The Part I setup is especially clean and the beam/lookahead ablations are useful. However, I do not find the technical case fully convincing. Several theoretical statements are presented as propositions/corollaries but are not actually proved; they rest on a stylized Bernoulli independence model that is only loosely connected to the search process. More importantly, the chemistry evaluation is methodologically weak: only five targets are used, the reward is similarity to known precursors rather than route correctness, and the baselines are not especially fair or comprehensive. There are also logical jumps from FoO-Pool vs FoO-LLM differences to strong claims that the search is definitively sound and that generation is the sole bottleneck. Thus the paper is competent in setup, but not free of major methodological concerns.
Novelty (0.0/10)
From a novelty and literature-positioning perspective, this paper falls well short of the highest standard. The central search idea is explicitly inherited from Flow-of-Options, and much of the contribution is an application/diagnostic study in retrosynthesis rather than a genuinely new method. The literature coverage is thin for both search-based LLM reasoning and retrosynthesis/planning: key adjacent areas such as MCTS-style search, best-first/A*-like planning perspectives, template-based and template-free retrosynthesis benchmarking, self-consistency/diverse decoding, and broader LLM-for-chemistry work are not meaningfully discussed. Comparisons to prior art are therefore neither comprehensive nor well calibrated. Novelty claims such as 'architecture-agnostic,' 'sound search algorithm,' and 'the bottleneck is therefore not the search' are substantially overstated relative to the evidence provided. Proper attribution is especially weak because the paper foregrounds conclusions that depend heavily on prior FoO ideas while not adequately situating its incremental contribution.

The manuscript is clearly written and easy to follow. The introduction states the question, the two-part design, and the intended takeaway effectively. Notation is mostly consistent, and the separation between the controlled grammar experiment and the chemistry case study helps readability. Results are presented in a straightforward way with interpretable tables and sensible narrative commentary. That said, the paper refers to figures/visual diagnostics that are absent, and some naming is mildly confusing across sections (for example, switching between B/K/D terminology). Overall, the presentation quality is strong, but the absence of actual figures prevents full marks.
Significance (7.0/10)
The problem is important: understanding whether search or option generation limits LLM-guided retrosynthesis is timely and relevant. The controlled synthetic benchmark is potentially useful as a diagnostic scaffold, and the paper could stimulate follow-up work on validity-gated search in chemistry. Still, the demonstrated practical impact is limited by the narrow chemistry evaluation, weak external baselines, and the fact that the strongest empirical result is largely a negative finding about online LLM options on five molecules. I do see value in the decomposition of search quality versus generator quality, but the evidence is not yet strong enough to support claims of substantial field-level advancement or a change in standard practice.
Overall Verdict (5.2/10)
This is a clearly written and thoughtfully structured paper with a useful controlled experiment, but it overclaims both novelty and conclusion strength. The strongest contribution is diagnostic: under a synthetic oracle-like option set, lookahead search helps; under a weak LLM option generator, it does not. However, the literature positioning is insufficient, attribution to prior FoO/search ideas is incomplete, and the chemistry evaluation is too limited to justify broad claims that the search is 'sound' or that generator validity is the sole bottleneck. My recommendation is reject in its current form, with encouragement to resubmit after substantially improving related-work coverage, tempering novelty claims, and adding stronger chemistry baselines and evaluation.
Reviewer C
Tech: 7.0 Nov: 4.0 Clar: 7.0 Sig: 7.0 Overall: 6.2
Technical Quality (7.0/10)
The paper is technically ambitious and substantially more formalized than many empirical retrosynthesis submissions. The synthetic benchmark is clearly specified, the search objective is written down, and the ablations over beam width and lookahead are useful. The central empirical observation—that search quality and option quality can be decoupled—is plausible and supported within the controlled grammar setting. However, I cannot rate the technical quality higher because the chemistry-side evaluation is quite weak: only five targets are used, the reward is a similarity proxy rather than route correctness, and the baseline comparisons are not fully fair in terms of budget and protocol. Some theoretical claims are framed more strongly than the evidence warrants; the Bernoulli bridge is heuristic rather than a proof, and propositions/corollaries are treated almost as confirmed theory from limited experiments. There are also methodological tensions, such as calling the setting one-step retrosynthesis while optimizing over a four-depth decomposition, and introducing max-updated path values without enough justification about what objective this preserves.
Novelty (4.0/10)
The controlled decoupling of search quality from option-generator quality is the most original aspect of the submission, and the synthetic grammar benchmark gives that question a sharper form than is typical. The application of a Flow-of-Options style search lens to retrosynthesis is interesting. Still, the novelty case is not fully convincing. The paper builds directly on FoO and combines it with familiar beam-search and LLM-option-generation ideas; the chemistry experiment feels more like a diagnostic adaptation than a fundamentally new method. Related-work positioning is also too thin for the claims being made: the paper cites some standard retrosynthesis and reasoning papers, but does not adequately situate itself relative to search-based synthesis planning, template/template-free retrosynthesis benchmarks, or prior analyses of invalid proposal bottlenecks. Because of this, several novelty and positioning claims feel overstated.
Clarity (7.0/10)
This is a generally well-written and easy-to-follow paper. The introduction states the question clearly, the two-part structure is intuitive, and the narrative arc from controlled benchmark to real chemistry is effective. The tables are integrated into the prose well enough that a reader can follow the main findings without effort. That said, from a communication standpoint the presentation falls short of the highest standard. Notation is somewhat overloaded and shifts across sections (for example B/b/K/D/L and the use of 'one-step' versus multi-depth decomposition), which creates avoidable friction. The paper also refers to propositions, corollaries, and 'P1' in ways that sound formally established, but the support is partly heuristic; clearer signaling would improve intellectual honesty and reader trust. Finally, there are no actual figures, and the visualization criterion is not really satisfied by text tables alone.
Significance (7.0/10)
The problem is important, timely, and relevant: understanding whether failures come from search or generation is valuable for LLM-guided scientific reasoning and retrosynthesis planning alike. The paper offers a useful conceptual takeaway—improving search is insufficient if the option generator collapses validity and diversity—which could inform future system design. The controlled benchmark may also be a helpful diagnostic tool for follow-up work. However, the practical significance is limited by the small-scale chemistry evaluation and the weakness of the downstream metric. The results do not yet demonstrate a substantial advance over existing retrosynthesis practice, nor do they justify immediate adoption of the proposed pipeline in realistic settings. I see the work as more significant as a diagnostic framing than as a ready technical breakthrough.
Overall Verdict (6.2/10)
This paper is clearly written, well organized, and strongest as a communication piece that isolates an important systems question: when does better search actually matter in retrosynthesis? The synthetic benchmark is the main strength and provides credible evidence that lookahead search can help when the option set is exact. However, the real-chemistry study is too small and methodologically limited to support several of the stronger conclusions, and the related-work positioning is not yet mature enough for the novelty claims. My recommendation is borderline negative in its current form: promising framing and presentation, but the technical validation on realistic chemistry needs to be substantially strengthened.
Reviewer D
Tech: 7.0 Nov: 4.0 Clar: 8.0 Sig: 7.0 Overall: 6.5
Technical Quality (7.0/10)
The paper formalizes the synthetic benchmark clearly and does a good job separating search from option generation, which is valuable. Reproducibility is relatively strong at the level of paper description: grammar construction, scoring rules, hyperparameters, and ablations are stated. However, from a feasibility and implementation standpoint, the technical case is not fully convincing. The main theoretical propositions are not proved, and the bridge model is a heuristic fit rather than a rigorous analysis. More importantly, the chemistry study is too small and too weakly controlled to support strong causal conclusions: only five targets are used, the reward is similarity to known precursors rather than route validity or executable synthesis quality, and the comparisons are not obviously resource-matched or equally favorable to all methods. There are also methodological concerns in claiming the search is 'sound' in practice when the real setting shows severe dependence on generator quality and when the curated pool condition is substantially easier than online generation.
Novelty (4.0/10)
The paper's most interesting aspect is the explicit decomposition of search quality versus option-generator quality using a controlled grammar plus a small chemistry case study. That framing has some originality as an empirical diagnostic. Still, the core search idea is inherited directly from Flow-of-Options, and the chemistry adaptation appears closer to an application/validation exercise than a fundamentally new method. The related-work positioning is limited, especially given the large retrosynthesis literature on search, template-based planning, one-step precursor ranking, and LLM-driven chemistry. Several novelty claims are somewhat overstated relative to the evidence, particularly the suggestion of architecture-agnostic generality and strong real-world conclusions from a very narrow benchmark.
Clarity (8.0/10)
The manuscript is generally well written, logically organized, and easy to follow. The introduction states the problem, setup, and intended contribution clearly, and the synthetic-to-real transition is presented coherently. Notation is mostly consistent and the methods can be followed by an expert reader without difficulty. The main weakness is presentation support: the paper relies entirely on tables and text, with no figures or diagrams to explain the search process, DAG evolution, or failure modes in the chemistry setting. That omission matters because the work makes structural claims about search behavior that would benefit from clearer visualization.
Significance (7.0/10)
The problem is important: practical synthesis planning depends heavily on the interaction between search and candidate generation, and the paper usefully highlights that strong search cannot rescue poor proposal quality. That diagnosis could motivate better future work on valid and diverse option generation. However, the demonstrated practical impact is limited. The real-chemistry evaluation is too small and too indirect to justify adoption, and the method does not yet establish a robust path to deployment in realistic retrosynthesis systems. As a result, the paper is more significant as a cautionary experimental observation than as a ready-to-use advance.
Overall Verdict (6.5/10)
This paper is thoughtful, clearly written, and strongest when it uses the synthetic benchmark to isolate the effect of lookahead search from option quality. I appreciate the practical message that better search is only useful when upstream proposal generation is valid and diverse. However, the chemistry evidence is too limited and the comparisons too weakly controlled for the paper to support its broader claims about soundness and real-world applicability. My recommendation is borderline reject: promising framing and useful diagnostics, but not yet strong enough in methodological rigor or practical validation for acceptance.
Reviewer E
Tech: 7.0 Nov: 3.0 Clar: 8.0 Sig: 6.0 Overall: 6.0
Technical Quality (7.0/10)
The paper is technically organized and unusually explicit about its formal setup. The grammar benchmark is well specified, the search procedure is defined with notation, and the main design choices are supported by a sensible ablation over beam width and lookahead depth. Reproducibility is relatively strong for a paper of this scope, with concrete hyperparameters and evaluation details reported. However, I do not find the technical case fully reliable at the highest standard. The theoretical bridge is more heuristic than theory, some propositions are asserted as confirmed without proof, and the real-chemistry evaluation has important methodological weaknesses: only five targets are used, the reward is tied to known precursors via Tanimoto similarity rather than route validity, and the comparison between online LLM generation, curated pools, and single-pass baselines is not fully fair in resources or task formulation. These issues do not erase the contribution, but they limit confidence in strong general claims.
Novelty (3.0/10)
I am not persuaded that the paper delivers strong methodological novelty. The central search idea is Flow-of-Options from prior work, and the paper's main addition is a decoupled evaluation framing: a controlled grammar benchmark to isolate search effects and a small chemistry study to argue that option-generator validity is the real bottleneck. That framing is useful, but it is closer to a diagnostic empirical study than a new algorithmic advance. The related-work positioning is also incomplete for a claim space involving retrosynthesis planning, search over action proposals, and LLM-guided chemistry. Comparisons are limited and not comprehensive relative to the state of the art in synthesis planning or molecule-generation validity control. Several claims about architecture-agnosticity and limiting factors therefore feel overstated relative to the evidence presented.
Clarity (8.0/10)
The manuscript is clearly written, well structured, and easy to follow. The introduction states the question, the two-part experimental strategy, and the intended takeaway with minimal ambiguity. Methodological exposition is stronger than average, especially in separating the controlled synthetic setting from the chemistry study and in explaining how the authors interpret the latter through the former. The main weakness is presentation rather than prose: the paper relies almost entirely on text and tables, with no actual figures despite questions of search dynamics and beam behavior that would benefit from visual explanation. Relatedly, some naming is mildly confusing, such as switching between K/D and B/L notation across sections. Still, an expert reader can understand the work without much difficulty, and even adjacent readers could grasp the central claim.
Significance (6.0/10)
The problem is unquestionably important: distinguishing search limitations from proposal-generation limitations in LLM-assisted retrosynthesis is relevant to both reasoning research and AI-for-chemistry. The paper also offers a potentially useful conceptual lesson: better search will not rescue poor option validity, and controlled benchmarks can make that dependence visible. That said, from an impact standpoint I remain unconvinced that the present evidence is strong enough to shift field practice. The chemistry evaluation is extremely small, uses a proxy reward rather than synthesis success, and the strongest positive result comes from a curated option pool that mainly demonstrates an oracle-style upper bound. As a result, the paper identifies an important bottleneck but does not yet show a substantial real-world advance over existing approaches, nor does it demonstrate gains large and robust enough to justify adoption in practical retrosynthesis workflows. Its likely impact is as a cautionary diagnostic framing for future work rather than as a field-changing result in its current form.
Overall Verdict (6.0/10)
This paper asks a meaningful question and provides a clean, instructive decomposition between search quality and option-generator quality. The controlled grammar study is the strongest part of the submission and gives credible evidence that lookahead search can help when the option set is valid and complete. However, the real-chemistry section is too limited to support the broader significance claims: the dataset is tiny, the evaluation is proxy-based, and the comparisons do not establish practical superiority or broad field impact. My recommendation is borderline negative in its current form, though I see value in the benchmark framing and believe a stronger, larger-scale chemistry validation could make this line of work much more influential.
