The submission captures the core ideas in compact Python, and the main synthetic search code is readable with sensible names. However, it is research prototype code rather than well-engineered software: abstraction boundaries are thin, documentation is sparse, style/tooling is unspecified, dependencies are not pinned, and there is little defensive programming, testing, or maintainability infrastructure. The implementation is adequate to communicate the method, but not close to production-grade quality.
Code Reproducibility (2.0/10)
Part I is comparatively reproducible: seeds are given, the grammar is fully included, and the main configuration is explicit. That said, the package/environment is not fully specified, no lockfile or container is provided, and the Part II pipeline depends on external Bedrock access and an LLM service that cannot be reproduced exactly from the materials shown. As provided, one can likely reproduce the synthetic benchmark logic, but not independently and bit-exactly verify all reported quantitative claims.
Code Correctness (4.0/10)
The basic data structures and control flow for the synthetic benchmark are coherent, and several edge cases such as empty candidate sets are handled. Still, there are notable correctness concerns. The code excerpt does not include the full experiment drivers, the Part II implementation is only described rather than fully provided, function signatures are untyped, and the synthetic solver appears to expand rather than contract sequences despite the text framing retrosynthetic contraction. There is also no robust API/error handling for external calls, so correctness across the full claimed domain is not established.
Code-Paper Alignment (10.0/10)
Alignment is the strongest aspect of the submission. The code clearly implements the paper's central constructs: grammar-based synthetic benchmarking, greedy versus FoO beam search with lookahead, the stated hyperparameters, the target molecules, and the reward definition based on RDKit Tanimoto similarity. Function and variable naming largely match the terminology in the writeup, and the provided snippets are directly useful for validating the paper's core methodological claims, especially for Part I.
Overall Verdict (4.2/10)
This is a credible research-code artifact with strong paper-code alignment, especially for the synthetic benchmark, but weak software engineering and limited end-to-end reproducibility. The main contribution is implemented clearly enough to inspect and partially rerun, yet the absence of full Part II code, environment specification, dependency pinning, tests, and robust external-call handling substantially limits reproducibility and confidence in correctness. I would view this as acceptable prototype code for supporting a paper claim, not as a fully reproducible or well-audited release. Recommendation: borderline positive on alignment, but below the bar for code quality and reproducibility excellence.

Extension Review (G)

Reviewer G
Ext: 10.0 Overall: 10.0
Extension Quality (10.0/10)
This submission is a strong and highly coherent extension of Flow-of-Options. It explicitly situates itself as a follow-on to the reference paper, preserves the central FoO idea of option-centric lookahead search over structured decision spaces, and then pushes that framework into a new and meaningful domain: retrosynthesis. The extension is not merely an application paper; it also sharpens the original paper’s implicit claims by decoupling search quality from option-generator quality. The controlled grammar benchmark is especially valuable because it isolates the FoO search mechanism under exact option sets, while the USPTO-50K drug-target study shows where real-world performance breaks down. That design directly addresses a limitation of the reference paper, which demonstrated strong empirical gains but did not cleanly separate the contribution of the search structure from the quality of generated options. The paper also extends the theoretical framing by formalizing FoO-style search, introducing propositions and corollaries about beam width, lookahead, and effective branching factor, and testing those claims empirically. Conceptually, the work remains tightly continuous with the reference paper while adding new methodology, theory, and evidence. Readers of the original FoO paper would likely find this a valuable next step because it both broadens FoO’s scope and clarifies when FoO succeeds or fails.
Overall Verdict (10.0/10)
This is a substantive and well-motivated extension of the reference paper. It maintains clear continuity with Flow-of-Options while contributing a new application domain, a cleaner causal analysis of the search-vs-generator distinction, and a more formal theoretical treatment. The synthetic benchmark is particularly compelling as an extension artifact because it isolates the original mechanism in a way the reference paper did not. I would recommend this as a valuable next-stage FoO paper, especially for readers interested in generalizing the framework beyond AutoML and understanding its true bottlenecks.

Detailed Reviews

Paper Reviews (A-E)

Reviewer A
Tech: 3.0 Nov: 4.0 Clar: 10.0 Sig: 6.0 Overall: 5.8
Technical Quality (3.0/10)
The paper is easy to follow experimentally and does state several assumptions and limitations explicitly, but the technical standard falls well short of rigorous support for its central claims. The strongest issue is methodological: the advertised mix-ratio probe is not actually executed except at alpha=0 and one partial mixed configuration, yet the title and framing imply a broader empirical boundary result. The statistical treatment is also weak. Reporting bootstrap mean and population standard deviation over only eight random sub-samples is not a sound way to characterize uncertainty, and using mean +/- 1.96*std without a justified sampling model is not rigorous. More seriously, the paper conflates pairwise correctness-agreement with stronger notions of dependence/common-mode failure; observing identical correctness on 20 hand-curated items is suggestive but not sufficient to establish the broader conclusion stated. There are also internal tensions: Section 2.2 defines n=8 ensembles drawn from 12 Sonnet and 8 Opus pools, while the reported mixed probe is one Sonnet plus three Opus agents, i.e. effectively n=4 for the rescue demonstration. The use of 'best-of-n oracle' as a reported aggregation strategy is not a fair baseline for practical aggregation, and no substantive ablation validates whether the result is driven by item construction, prompt identity, answer normalization, or model sampling setup. Overall, the evidence supports a narrow descriptive finding on this small benchmark, but not the stronger technical framing.
Novelty (4.0/10)
There is some novelty in isolating aggregation-stage same-model failure via a simple pairwise correctness-agreement probe and in documenting a concrete cross-model rescue case. However, the contribution is largely a narrow empirical observation rather than a substantial methodological or theoretical advance. The relation to prior ensemble-diversity, self-consistency, and multi-agent work is discussed, but not comprehensively enough to justify strong novelty positioning, and the comparisons are limited. Because the paper does not run the full proposed alpha sweep and does not benchmark against broader existing ensemble analyses, the novelty claims feel overstated relative to the actual delivered evidence.
Clarity (10.0/10)
The paper is clearly written, well organized, and unusually explicit about what was and was not measured. The introduction states the problem, experimental focus, and claimed contribution plainly. Methods, notation, and scope boundaries are presented in a way that an expert reader can reconstruct the intended procedure. Figures and tables are referenced appropriately and the prose generally avoids ambiguity. Even where I disagree with the strength of the conclusions, the presentation itself is transparent and accessible.
Significance (6.0/10)
The topic is important: correlated failure in LLM ensembles matters for practical system design. The observed unanimity on a small adversarial set and the demonstration of one cross-model rescue could motivate useful follow-up work. That said, the current study is too small, too task-specific, and too underpowered to support broad changes in how the field should approach ensemble aggregation. The measured improvement is only one item on a 20-question benchmark, and the result may not generalize beyond this curated set. Thus the work has moderate value as a cautionary probe, but limited standalone significance in its current form.
Overall Verdict (5.8/10)
This paper presents a clearly written and potentially interesting cautionary empirical finding: on a small adversarial benchmark, same-model samples exhibited perfect correctness agreement and majority voting offered no gain, while a partial cross-model probe rescued one item. However, from a rigor standpoint the paper overreaches. The core alpha-sweep thesis is only partially executed, the statistics are weak for the claims being made, and the interpretation of perfect correctness agreement as establishing a common-mode-failure ceiling is stronger than the evidence warrants. I would recommend rejection in its current form, but I do think a revised version with a fully run sweep, stronger statistical methodology, and more careful claim calibration could become useful.
Reviewer B
Tech: 4.0 Nov: 2.0 Clar: 10.0 Sig: 5.0 Overall: 5.2
Technical Quality (4.0/10)
The paper is easy to reproduce from the text and appendical claims, and it states assumptions and scope with commendable explicitness. However, the empirical foundation is extremely narrow: only 20 hand-curated items, only two measured alpha points, and a partial cross-model probe based on three Opus samples for the failure cases. Several central claims are therefore under-supported, especially any broader statement about same-model voting, Condorcet-independence failure in practice, or alpha-sweep behavior. I also see methodological weakness in treating a tiny adversarial set as sufficient evidence for a ceiling phenomenon, using bootstrap summaries over such a limited benchmark, and presenting 'best-of-n oracle' as a baseline without deeper validation. The work is more a small diagnostic case study than a robust technical contribution.
Novelty (2.0/10)
From a novelty and literature-positioning perspective, the paper falls well short of a high bar. The core observation—that samples from the same model can exhibit highly correlated failures on adversarial reasoning items, and that another model may rescue some of them—is intuitive and already broadly anticipated by ensemble-diversity, self-consistency, and common-mode-failure discussions. The paper's main contribution is a small empirical probe, not a new method or a new conceptual framework. More importantly, the related-work positioning is too thin for the strength of the novelty claims: there is little engagement with prior work on response correlation, self-consistency failure modes, model diversity in ensembles, or broader LLM ensemble/jury behavior beyond a few canonical citations. The paper gives some credit appropriately, but it overstates originality relative to the modest experiment actually conducted.
Clarity (10.0/10)
This is the strongest aspect of the submission. The problem, measured quantities, scope, and non-claims are stated clearly, and the paper is unusually careful in distinguishing what was actually run from what the infrastructure could run. The structure is clean, notation is simple and consistent, and the figure/table descriptions are understandable. Even readers outside the immediate subarea could follow the argument. I have substantial reservations about the strength of the evidence, but not about the readability of the paper.
Significance (5.0/10)
The topic is important and timely: independence assumptions in LLM ensembles do matter, and explicit probes of correlated failure are useful. The paper could inspire follow-up work on diversity-aware aggregation, benchmarking, and cross-model ensembling. That said, the present evidence is too limited to support broad practical impact. A one-item rescue on a 20-item handcrafted benchmark and a same-model unanimity result on this specific set do not yet justify changing field practice. I therefore view the significance as moderate at best: relevant as a cautionary pilot study, but not a substantial advance.
Overall Verdict (5.2/10)
This paper is clearly written and asks a relevant question, but the empirical basis is too small and underdeveloped to support its broader claims. As a novelty-focused reviewer, I do not find the contribution sufficiently original: the central phenomenon is expected from existing ensemble-diversity reasoning, and the literature coverage does not convincingly establish a gap. The study is best understood as a compact diagnostic note rather than a substantial research advance. I would recommend rejection in its current form, while encouraging the authors to expand the benchmark, run the full alpha sweep, and position the work much more carefully against prior ensemble and error-correlation literature.

Technical Quality (4.0/10)
The paper does a good job defining its main variables and describing the intended protocol, and it is commendably explicit about what was and was not measured. However, the technical evidence is too thin for the strength of the framing. The central empirical claim rests on only 20 hand-curated items, with only two alpha points actually measured and the cross-model condition being a partial probe rather than the preregistered sweep. Several methodological choices are questionable or under-justified, including the bootstrap setup over only eight subsamples, the use of mean +/- 1.96*std as a CI, and the interpretation of pairwise correctness agreement as an operational signature of common-mode failure without stronger controls. Overall, the paper is careful in some respects, but it falls short of the rigor needed to substantiate its broader technical claims.
Novelty (4.0/10)

That angle is somewhat fresh. However, the actual contribution is modest and partly incremental: it demonstrates unanimous failure on a small adversarial set and a single-item cross-model rescue, rather than introducing a broadly new method or substantially extending prior theory. Related work is cited, but the positioning against the wider ensemble/diversity literature is not comprehensive enough, and some novelty claims are too strong relative to the narrow empirical base. In particular, language such as 'direct empirical confirmation' and the broad implications drawn for aggregation assumptions exceed what such a small, curated benchmark can support.

That said, the demonstrated effect size is limited in scope, and the current evidence does not yet justify strong adoption decisions or broad field-level conclusions. Its core message—that same-model voting can collapse under adversarial reasoning and that cross-model mixtures may help—is important and timely, but the empirical foundation is too narrow to support several of the stronger technical and novelty claims. In particular, the small hand-curated benchmark, incomplete alpha sweep, and limited comparative analysis weaken the paper substantially. I would view this as a clear and useful preliminary study rather than a fully convincing technical contribution; my recommendation is borderline, leaning weak reject unless the venue is comfortable with concise empirical probes.

Reviewer F
Tech: 3.0 Repro: 1.0 Correct: 5.0 Align: 10.0 Overall: 4.8
Code Technical Quality (3.0/10)
The submission is reasonably organized for a small experimental script: configuration, scoring, sweep logic, main execution, and self-tests are separated, names are generally clear, and the code is readable. However, it is not production-grade. Documentation is selective rather than comprehensive, there is no linting or dependency management, error handling around external LLM calls is absent, and maintainability is limited to a single script with minimal extension scaffolding. Overall, this is decent research code, but far from enterprise or robust engineering quality.
Code Reproducibility (1.0/10)
Reproducibility is the weakest area. The script has fixed seeds and bundled benchmark items, and it includes deterministic tie-breaking plus lightweight self-tests, which is helpful. But execution still requires external Bedrock credentials and an unspecified external package, dependencies are not pinned, the environment is not containerized, and the claimed paper results are not fully reproducible from the provided instructions because some reported results come from partial probes rather than the full sweep. The code supports rerunning an experiment, but not immediate, exact, self-contained reproduction.
Code Correctness (5.0/10)
The core implementation appears plausible and mostly coherent: the data structures are appropriate, function signatures are sensible, and the main experimental logic broadly matches the stated methodology. Still, there are correctness risks. The bootstrap/CI terminology is somewhat inconsistent with what is actually implemented, the pairwise agreement computation for mixed ensembles is based on a single sampled ensemble per alpha rather than an exhaustive or more stable estimate, and there is no defensive handling of external API failures, malformed responses, or insufficient pool sizes beyond min-truncation. Thus, the code is serviceable research code, but not fully reliable across all realistic inputs and runtime conditions.
Code-Paper Alignment (10.0/10)
Alignment between code and manuscript claims is strong. The script directly implements the described mix-ratio sweep, majority voting with lexicographic tie-breaking, answer normalization, pairwise correctness-agreement, and paired bootstrap comparison across alpha values. The benchmark questions, terminology, and reported infrastructure all match the narrative closely. Even where the paper is explicit that some alpha values were not run, the code clearly provides the corresponding machinery, so it would materially help validate the claimed findings.
Overall Verdict (4.8/10)
This is a solid piece of research prototype code that aligns well with the claimed experiment and captures the essential ideas needed to audit the paper’s code-based contribution. Its main strengths are conceptual clarity, faithful implementation of the reported setup, and inclusion of a few useful self-tests. Its main weaknesses are reproducibility and robustness: the environment is underspecified, external dependencies are unpinned, and the script lacks defensive engineering around API behavior and exact result regeneration. I would view this as acceptable supplementary research code, but not as a fully reproducible or high-assurance implementation.

Extension Review (G)

Reviewer G
Ext: 9.0 Overall: 9.0
Extension Quality (9.0/10)
The submission clearly positions itself relative to Flow-of-Options and maintains strong conceptual continuity by focusing on a specific downstream assumption that FoO-style systems may rely on: that aggregation across agents benefits from diversity rather than collapsing under common-mode failure. This is a meaningful extension in scope, because it interrogates an important boundary condition left largely implicit in the reference work, namely when multiple candidate reasoners are not actually independent. The paper is strongest as a diagnostic and evaluative extension: it introduces a mix-ratio probe, pairwise correctness-agreement analysis, and a cross-model rescue framing that extends the theoretical conversation around option diversity and ensemble usefulness. It also contributes new evidence on adversarial reasoning tasks and provides reproducible infrastructure for further sweeps. The main limitation, from an extension standpoint, is that it does not extend FoO’s actual graph-search machinery, value propagation, or path-construction methodology; by the authors’ own admission, it studies only the aggregation layer assumption. So while this is not a methodological extension of FoO itself, it is still a substantive and valuable continuation that sharpens where FoO-inspired multi-agent voting is likely to help or fail.
Overall Verdict (9.0/10)
This is a strong and credible extension paper. It does not modify the core FoO algorithm, but it meaningfully advances the reference paper’s broader agenda by testing an important hidden assumption behind diversity-based aggregation: whether same-model ensembles actually fail differently. The result is conceptually aligned, empirically useful, and clearly novel enough that readers of the original FoO paper would likely find it informative as a boundary-setting next step. I recommend it as a worthwhile extension, with the caveat that its contribution is diagnostic and analytical rather than a direct methodological augmentation of FoO.

Detailed Reviews

Paper Reviews (A-E)

Reviewer A
Tech: 6.0 Nov: 3.0 Clar: 9.0 Sig: 5.0 Overall: 5.8
Technical Quality (6.0/10)
The paper is formally presented and generally reproducible at the simulator level: notation is explicit, the update rules and warm-start equations are written clearly, and many experimental settings are enumerated. It also contains honest limitation statements and a useful ablation against Bayesian without M2. However, under a strict rigor standard, the methodology has important weaknesses. The per-edge Beta posterior is treated as a Bayesian object despite using sigmoid-weighted fractional updates that are not derived from a coherent Bernoulli generative model, so several probabilistic interpretations are only heuristic. The paper also conflates edge-level uncertainty with path-level reward attribution without analyzing the induced bias from assigning the same walk outcome to every traversed edge in a compositional DAG. Fairness of comparisons is limited in the transfer setting because only the proposed method is given cross-task state, while non-transfer baselines are compared on a regime explicitly designed to reward transfer. There is also no complexity analysis for retrieval or warm-starting, and some statistical reporting is inconsistent or insufficiently justified for the structure of the data. Overall, the work is competently executed but not free of substantial methodological concerns.
Novelty (3.0/10)
The paper itself is fairly honest that most ingredients are standard: Thompson sampling, Beta-Bernoulli posteriors, Jaccard-style topology kernels, and case-based transfer are all known. The contribution is primarily a recombination of these components in the FoO setting. That can still be useful, but the paper does not convincingly establish that the integration rises above a thoughtful engineering variant, especially since the single-task result is null by the authors' own preregistered criterion and the transfer mechanism is evaluated only on a toy DAG. Related-work coverage is also thin for a paper making transfer, structured bandit, and graph-retrieval claims; several adjacent literatures seem under-discussed. I therefore view the novelty as limited and only partially substantiated.
Clarity (9.0/10)
The paper is clearly written, well organized, and easy to follow. The introduction states the modified mechanisms, the equations are readable, and the paper is commendably explicit about what is and is not claimed. The methods and results sections are structured coherently, and the discussion of limitations is unusually candid. The main clarity flaw is presentation consistency around figures and equation numbering: the text refers to equations and figures in ways that are somewhat confusing, and the figure/table apparatus is not fully polished. Still, the exposition is strong enough that an expert reader can reconstruct the intended algorithm and evaluation protocol without much difficulty.
Significance (5.0/10)
Improving transfer in structured option-graph reasoning is a relevant problem, and the paper identifies a plausible failure mode in max-updated edge scores and text-only retrieval. The proposed ideas could matter if they transferred to real FoO benchmarks or broader agentic AutoML systems. However, the current evidence is synthetic-only and narrow, and the strongest gains arise in a benchmark constructed to favor cross-task warm-starting. Because the central single-task claim does not pass the preregistered threshold and no real benchmark validation is presented, it is hard to conclude that the work yet represents a substantial advance likely to alter practice. The paper is potentially useful, but its present demonstrated significance is modest.
Overall Verdict (5.8/10)
This is a well-written and intellectually honest paper with a clear problem framing and a reasonable synthetic experimental protocol, but it falls short of the level of technical rigor needed for a strong endorsement. The main concerns are methodological: the claimed Bayesian semantics are only approximate, edge-level credit assignment is not theoretically justified, and the transfer comparisons are not fully fair in the regime used to claim success. The work is promising as a preliminary systems idea and the authors deserve credit for candid reporting of the failed single-task preregistration, but the present evidence does not yet establish a robust technical advance. My recommendation is borderline negative in its current form, with encouragement to validate on real FoO benchmarks and tighten the probabilistic and comparative methodology.
Reviewer B
Tech: 6.0 Nov: 3.0 Clar: 9.0 Sig: 5.0 Overall: 5.8

However, the technical case remains weakened by substantial methodological concerns. My main clarity reservation is that the figure/table presentation is somewhat uneven: there are figures, but no tables summarizing results compactly, and some figure numbering/cross-referencing is awkward. Still, for an expert reader, the paper is highly readable.

Code Review (F)

Reviewer F
Tech: 2.0 Repro: 3.0 Correct: 6.0 Align: 9.0 Overall: 5.0
Code Technical Quality (2.0/10)
The submission is a reasonably readable single-file research prototype with helpful comments and many docstrings, and naming is generally consistent with the paper terminology. That said, the architecture is monolithic rather than well-factored, several functions rely on mutable global state (notably LATENTS), there is little defensive validation, no tests/CI, and no robust logging or structured error handling. It is suitable as appendix code for a toy simulator, but not close to production-grade or enterprise-maintainable quality.
Code Reproducibility (3.0/10)
Reproducibility is decent for a toy script: it is self-contained, runnable with a single command, uses fixed seeds, and avoids external datasets. However, the environment is not fully specified despite the paper text claiming exact versions outside the code, there is no lockfile/container, no machine-readable config, and no formal validation of expected outputs or numerical tolerances. The script likely reproduces its own toy experiments consistently, but it does not provide enough infrastructure to guarantee full reproduction of all reported quantitative claims.
Code Correctness (6.0/10)
The implementation is mostly coherent and uses sensible data structures and numerically safe patterns in many places, including overflow guards around the sigmoid and safe Beta sampling via gamma draws. Still, there are correctness concerns: the code hardcodes a specific latent toy setup that does not fully match several values described in the paper narrative, uses globals in wrappers, and some stated claims (e.g., exact experimental setup and all comparators) are not fully mirrored. Edge-case handling is limited, and I would not claim correctness for all admissible inputs, but for the intended toy-domain path the code appears broadly functional.
Code-Paper Alignment (9.0/10)
Alignment is the strongest aspect. The code clearly implements the paper's central ideas: FoO-style edge policies, Bayesian Beta/Thompson edge posteriors, sigmoid-weighted updates, topology-kernel similarity, transfer warm-starting, regret computation, sign tests, and calibration plots. Variable names and comments map closely to the manuscript. The main limitation is that the implementation targets only a synthetic toy DAG rather than the real FoO benchmark suite, so it validates the toy-paper claims more than the broader promised setting.
Overall Verdict (5.0/10)
This is a competent toy research prototype that captures the paper's core algorithmic ideas and is readable enough to audit, but it is not a high-quality reproducible software artifact by conference code standards. The biggest strengths are paper-code alignment and conceptual transparency; the biggest weaknesses are monolithic design, limited validation/testing, incomplete environment specification, and partial mismatch between narrative experimental claims and the exact hardcoded setup. I would view the code as sufficient to demonstrate the proposed mechanism on a synthetic benchmark, but insufficient to strongly support broader reproducibility or implementation-quality claims without substantial cleanup and verification.

Extension Review (G)

Reviewer G
Ext: 9.0 Overall: 9.0
Extension Quality (9.0/10)
The submission is a clear and well-scoped extension of Flow-of-Options rather than a detached derivative. It explicitly identifies two concrete FoO components as extension targets—the scalar edge-value update and the text-embedding case-retrieval mechanism—and replaces them with Bayesian edge posteriors plus Thompson sampling, and topology-keyed retrieval with additive Beta warm-starting. This preserves the underlying FoO DAG abstraction, option-walk reasoning structure, and transfer ambition, so continuity with the reference paper is strong. The work also directly engages claimed weaknesses or open issues in the baseline, especially non-stationary max-based edge updates, the inability to distinguish untried from evidence-supported poor edges, and text-similarity-based negative transfer in the case bank. That said, the empirical validation is narrower than the original paper’s full AutoML benchmark suite: the evidence is mainly on toy DAG and synthetic transfer settings, with the real FoO benchmark only promised as future work. Because of that, the paper is more methodological and analytical than applicational, and it does not yet convincingly demonstrate extension to a genuinely new real-world domain beyond the FoO setting itself. Still, as an extension in method, theory, and transfer mechanism, it is substantive and likely valuable to readers of the original FoO paper.
Overall Verdict (9.0/10)
This is a strong, meaningful extension of Flow-of-Options that keeps the original framework intact while upgrading two central mechanisms in a principled way. Its main strength is continuity: the paper clearly starts from FoO’s architecture and advances it with Bayesian uncertainty handling and topology-aware transfer instead of superficial modifications. The main limitation is external validation, since the evidence is still synthetic and does not yet replicate the original paper’s broader benchmark setting. Overall, I judge it a valuable next step for FoO, with the caveat that its claims would be much stronger after confirmation on the full reference-paper task suite.

Paper Reviews (A-E)

Reviewer A
Tech: 5.0 Nov: 4.0 Clar: 8.0 Sig: 5.0 Overall: 5.5
Technical Quality (5.0/10)
The paper is reasonably well specified at the algorithm level: the selector, objectives, normalization, tie-breaking, seeds, and cache behavior are described clearly enough to reproduce the claimed procedure. However, from a rigor standpoint the empirical support is weak. The study is extremely small, heavily dependent on synthetic-mode rows, and uses the same LLM family for generation, execution, and judging, which creates substantial validity concerns. Several headline claims are also much stronger than the evidence warrants: a 0.500 win rate and identical confidence intervals do not establish parity in a robust scientific sense, and the cost reduction is tiny and measured using an ad hoc token proxy rather than actual API cost. I also do not see adequate fairness in the baseline design, especially because the weighted baseline is minimally tuned and the best-first baseline is not a full reproduction of FoO’s exploration behavior. Overall, the methods are described carefully, but the evidentiary standard for correctness and methodological soundness is not met.
Novelty (4.0/10)
The core idea—using Pareto filtering instead of scalarization for multi-objective selection—is sensible but modest. In this setting it appears to be a straightforward transplantation of standard multi-objective optimization machinery, especially NSGA-II-style non-dominated sorting and crowding distance, into a small FoO layer-selection step. The paper positions itself against FoO and weighted scalarization, but the related-work discussion is narrow and likely incomplete for multi-objective decision making, decoding, and reranking in LLM pipelines. Because the empirical evidence is limited and the method is largely a direct adaptation of known tools, the novelty claims should be viewed conservatively.
Clarity (8.0/10)
The manuscript is clearly written and easy to follow. The introduction states the problem, the proposed Pareto-front replacement, and the claimed contribution succinctly. The methods section is organized and concrete, with useful operational details about prompts, scoring signals, fallbacks, and ablations. Results are summarized in a readable way and the paper is commendably explicit about limitations. Clarity is held back mainly by the absence of actual figures/visualizations despite references to them in the checklist dimensions, and by a few places where implementation-specific discussion substitutes for a more formal exposition.
Significance (5.0/10)
The problem addressed—cost-aware reasoning-path selection for LLM systems—is relevant and timely, and the proposal is simple enough that practitioners could try it. That said, the demonstrated gains are marginal: judged quality is tied with best-first, cost savings are very small, and the strongest efficiency result comes from the weighted baseline rather than the proposed method. Given the benchmark scale, synthetic contamination, and lack of evaluation on stronger or more realistic FoO settings, it is difficult to conclude that this work would materially change practice or meaningfully advance the field. The main value is as a lightweight hypothesis or engineering tweak rather than a substantial research contribution.
Overall Verdict (5.5/10)
This paper is clearly written and presents a neat, practically motivated modification to option selection, but the technical evidence is not strong enough for a rigorous research claim. The experimental design is too small, too synthetic, and too entangled with self-judging LLM evaluation to support the stronger conclusions about matching quality while improving cost. I appreciate the authors’ transparency about limitations, yet under a stringent correctness standard the work remains more of a plausible heuristic than a validated advance. My recommendation is rejection in its current form, with encouragement to resubmit after substantially stronger evaluation on larger, fully live benchmarks with better-controlled baselines and independent assessment.

The work does suggest potentially useful follow-up directions around multi-objective option management, but the present evidence supports modest rather than substantial significance.
Overall Verdict (5.0/10)
This paper is clearly written and commendably transparent about its setup, limitations, and operational details, but as a literature- and novelty-focused reviewer I find the contribution too incremental and too weakly situated in prior work to be compelling. The core idea is essentially a standard Pareto-filter/crowding-distance heuristic applied to FoO option selection, and the empirical results are small-scale, mostly tied, and heavily affected by synthetic-mode evaluation. I appreciate the honesty of the reporting, yet the current manuscript does not meet a high bar for originality, comprehensive related-work positioning, or demonstrated impact. My recommendation is reject in its current form, with encouragement to broaden the literature grounding and validate the method on larger, fully live evaluations against stronger baselines.
Reviewer C
Tech: 5.0 Nov: 4.0 Clar: 8.0 Sig: 5.0 Overall: 5.5
Technical Quality (5.0/10)
The paper is reasonably well specified at the algorithmic and experimental levels: the selector is clearly defined, assumptions and fallbacks are mostly explicit, and the ablation structure is concrete enough that an expert could reproduce the study. However, the technical case is weakened by important methodological issues. The evaluation is extremely small, heavily synthetic in the main run, and relies on the same model family for generation and judging. Several headline claims rest on ties or tiny cost differences, and fairness across baselines is not fully convincing because some methods are hand-tuned while others are not, and the budget cap affects modes unevenly. I did not see obvious mathematical mistakes in the Pareto rule itself, but I do see substantial threats to validity, no meaningful complexity analysis, and insufficient evidence for strong technical conclusions.
Novelty (4.0/10)
The core idea—replace scalarized selection with Pareto-front filtering plus a standard crowding-distance tie-break—is sensible but modest. It is a straightforward adaptation of established multi-objective optimization machinery to a very specific point in the FoO pipeline, rather than a fundamentally new reasoning framework. The positioning relative to prior work is serviceable but not comprehensive; the paper discusses FoO and some general LLM reasoning papers, yet it does not seriously engage broader related literature on multi-objective decoding, reranking, or cost-quality tradeoff methods. Claims of contribution are mostly not overstated, but the actual methodological advance appears incremental and the comparison set is too limited to establish stronger novelty.
Clarity (8.0/10)
This is the strongest aspect of the submission. The paper is well organized, the introduction states the problem and proposed fix clearly, and the methodological narrative is easy to follow. Notation is mostly consistent and the prose is crisp without being opaque. Results are summarized with appropriate interpretation, and the limitations section is commendably candid. The main communication weakness is the absence of actual figures despite repeated discussion of visual or tabular insights; as written, there is only one table and no visualizations to help readers inspect fronts, tradeoffs, or live-versus-synthetic discrepancies. Even so, the paper is broadly accessible and significantly clearer than many technically similar submissions.
Significance (5.0/10)
The problem is relevant: cost-aware reasoning-path selection for LLM systems is practically important, and a tuning-free alternative to hand-set scalarization weights could be useful. That said, the demonstrated effect is small, often statistically tied, and shown on a tiny benchmark with substantial synthetic fallback. The work suggests a practical design option rather than a field-shaping change, and the evidence does not support substantial advancement over existing approaches. I do think the paper opens a reasonable follow-up direction on multi-objective option selection in structured reasoning, but the current empirical support is too narrow for a strong significance judgment.
Overall Verdict (5.5/10)
This is a clearly written and well-structured paper proposing a plausible, easy-to-implement modification to Flow-of-Options selection. Its main strengths are communication quality, transparent reporting, and a practical algorithmic idea that avoids manual scalarization weights. However, I am not convinced by the technical evidence: the benchmark is very small, synthetic mode dominates many trials, the judging protocol is weak, and the measured gains are marginal or tied. My recommendation is borderline reject in its current form; with a stronger live evaluation, broader and fairer baselines, and more rigorous evidence for meaningful gains, this could become a solid workshop or applied systems paper.
Reviewer D
Tech: 5.0 Nov: 4.0 Clar: 8.0 Sig: 6.0 Overall: 5.8
Technical Quality (5.0/10)
The paper is reasonably well specified at the algorithm level: the objectives, dominance rule, tie-breaking, prompts, seeds, cache behavior, and ablations are described in enough detail to understand and approximately reproduce the study. I also appreciate the explicit acknowledgement of limitations, especially the dominance of synthetic-mode trials and the small sample size. However, from a feasibility and implementation standpoint, the empirical support is weak for the strength of the practical claims. The benchmark is tiny, the proposed method is evaluated on only depth-2 DAGs with k up to 4, and most main-run trials are synthetic rather than live, which materially limits external validity for deployment. Baseline fairness is also questionable because the strongest practical comparator appears to be best_first rather than the upstream FoO mechanism in its intended setting, and the study does not convincingly establish that the small reported cost difference is meaningful under realistic serving conditions. Complexity and scaling considerations—critical for deployment—are largely asserted rather than analyzed. Overall, the paper is technically coherent, but the evidence for robust, practically realizable gains is not strong enough.
Novelty (4.0/10)
The central idea—replacing a weighted scalar score with Pareto filtering plus crowding-distance tie-breaking over quality, diversity, and cost—is sensible but modest. It is essentially an application of standard multi-objective selection machinery, especially NSGA-II-style concepts, to a specific decision point inside FoO. The paper is appropriately candid about this being an algorithmic drop-in rather than a new reasoning framework, which helps keep the claims mostly restrained. That said, related-work positioning is not comprehensive enough for a strong novelty case: there is limited discussion of broader multi-objective decoding, reranking, or cost-aware inference literature around LLM systems, and the fairness of comparisons to scalarized alternatives is limited because tuning and resource conditions are not deeply explored. In short, the method is a plausible adaptation, but not a substantial conceptual advance.
Clarity (8.0/10)
The paper is clearly written and easy to follow. The introduction states the problem, motivation, and contribution plainly, and the methods section gives a concrete description of the selector, scoring signals, execution protocol, and reproducibility controls. The results are also presented in an interpretable way, with useful distinctions between score, cost proxy, novelty, and win-rate. My main clarity reservations are presentation-related: the paper refers to Figure 2 of FoO rather than providing its own visualizations, and the current submission contains no figures, making some claims about fronts, ties, and selection behavior harder to inspect directly. Still, for a short empirical systems-style paper, the exposition is strong.
Significance (6.0/10)
The problem is relevant: if option-based LLM reasoning is to be deployable, path selection should account for cost and diversity without excessive retuning. The proposed selector is lightweight and could in principle be useful as a practical heuristic in budget-constrained systems. However, the demonstrated gains are very small, mostly amounting to parity in judged quality with a slight reduction in a token-length proxy, while the strongest cost-efficiency result comes from the weighted-sum baseline rather than the proposed method. Because the evaluation is narrow, synthetic-heavy, and not connected to larger or more realistic FoO deployments, it is hard to conclude that this work would materially change practice today. I do see follow-up value, especially in studying multi-objective selection in deeper reasoning graphs, but the present evidence does not support strong impact.
Overall Verdict (5.8/10)
This paper presents a pragmatic and clearly described modification to Flow-of-Options by using Pareto-front filtering over quality, diversity, and cost. The idea is implementable and lightweight, but the current evidence base is too limited to justify strong practical conclusions: the evaluation is very small, dominated by synthetic trials, restricted to shallow graphs, and the reported gains over a simple baseline are marginal. From a deployment realism perspective, the work is promising as an exploratory heuristic rather than a validated improvement. I would view this as borderline at best and in need of substantially stronger live, larger-scale, and more realistic evaluation before acceptance.

Tech: 6.0 Nov: 4.0 Clar: 9.0 Sig: 5.0 Overall: 6.0
Technical Quality (6.0/10)

The core claims are supported by some empirical evidence, and the authors are appropriately candid about limitations. However, the technical case is substantially weakened by the experimental design: the benchmark is extremely small, the live budget cap forces most trials into synthetic mode, and the main claimed gain over best_first is effectively a statistical tie with a very small cost difference. Baseline fairness is also questionable because weighted scalarization is only lightly explored and upstream FoO settings are not meaningfully reproduced. I also did not see any serious complexity analysis, which matters for a method framed as a drop-in selector for broader deployment.
Novelty (4.0/10)
The work is best understood as a modest algorithmic modification rather than a major conceptual advance. Replacing scalarized option selection with Pareto filtering plus NSGA-II-style crowding distance is a sensible adaptation, but it is assembled from standard multi-objective machinery and applied to a narrow selector component of FoO. The positioning relative to FoO is clear, but the related-work discussion around multi-objective search, pruning, and cost-aware LLM inference is not comprehensive enough to support a strong novelty claim. The notation and selector description are easy to follow, and the operational details are unusually explicit. The table is readable and the results are interpreted with useful caution. The only notable clarity weakness is presentation support: there are effectively no substantial visual analyses beyond a single table and pseudocode, so some claims about behavior across settings are harder to inspect than they should be.
Significance (5.0/10)
The paper targets a relevant problem—cost-aware path selection in LLM reasoning systems—and the no-tuning objective is practically attractive. That said, from an impact perspective the evidence for meaningful field-level significance is weak. The reported outcome is essentially parity with best_first on judged quality, a negligible cost reduction, and no compelling demonstration that the method changes deployment decisions or scientific understanding. The study is too small, too dominated by synthetic-mode evaluation, and too narrow in tasks and depth to justify claims of broad influence or substantial advancement. While the idea could inspire follow-up work on multi-objective option selection, the present paper does not yet establish improvements large enough to drive adoption.
Overall Verdict (6.0/10)

However, the empirical evidence does not support a strong impact claim. The method appears to be a modest, standard multi-objective adaptation that achieves near-total parity with a simple baseline while most evaluation occurs under synthetic conditions. I therefore view this as an interesting but presently limited contribution whose practical and field-level significance remains unproven.

Code Review (F)

Reviewer F
Tech: 4.0 Repro: 1.0 Correct: 6.0 Align: 10.0 Overall: 5.2
Code Technical Quality (4.0/10)
The code is reasonably well structured for a research artifact: generation, selection, execution/judging, aggregation, and persistence are separated into coherent functions, names are mostly clear, and type hints are present throughout. It also includes pragmatic safeguards such as cacheing, synthetic fallbacks, atomic cache writes, and assertions around key assumptions. However, it is not production-grade: documentation is uneven, there is no test suite or CI evidence, dependency management is not pinned, some paths are hard-coded to a local filesystem, and style/lint compliance is not demonstrated. Overall, this is a solid experimental script with decent engineering hygiene, but not a polished or fully maintainable software package.
Code Reproducibility (1.0/10)
Reproducibility is the weakest dimension. The script is runnable in principle, but not immediately on a fresh machine because it depends on a local repository path, an external Bedrock-access utility, credentials, and unpinned packages. The authors do document seeds, cacheing, output files, and some fallback behavior, and the CSV/JSON artifacts have clear schemas implied by the dataclasses and writers. But the environment is not fully specified, exact result reproduction is undermined by reliance on live LLM calls and mixed/synthetic modes, and configurations are embedded as Python constants rather than external machine-readable configs. This is better than an unreproducible sketch, but it falls short of strong artifact reproducibility.
Code Correctness (6.0/10)
The implementation appears mostly coherent and internally consistent. Core function signatures are sensible, data structures fit the task, normalization and Pareto-front logic are implemented in a plausible way, and several edge cases are handled, including parse failures, degenerate normalization ranges, and unavailable sklearn/Bedrock. That said, I would not call it fully correct for all intended claims: the selection setup is a simplified per-layer chooser rather than a full FoO graph update procedure, API robustness is limited by lack of timeouts/version checks, and there are correctness risks from hard-coded assumptions, resource exhaustion, and dependence on synthetic fallbacks during failed or capped runs. So the code is broadly functional, but not guaranteed correct across all inputs or execution environments.
Code-Paper Alignment (10.0/10)
Alignment between the described method and the code is strong. The script clearly implements the paper’s central idea of replacing scalar option selection with a Pareto-front selector over quality, diversity, and cost, including non-dominance filtering and NSGA-II-style crowding distance tie-breaking. The benchmark setup, selectors, ablations, metrics, and produced outputs all map closely onto the narrative description. Variable names, function names, and saved artifact structure are also consistent with the method being claimed. As a code artifact for validating the paper’s implementation-level contribution, this is well aligned.
Overall Verdict (5.2/10)
This is a credible research prototype with strong paper-code alignment and acceptable implementation quality for a small experimental artifact, but its reproducibility story is limited. The central Pareto-selection idea is implemented clearly and the experiment pipeline is understandable, yet the package depends on local paths, external infrastructure, and unpinned environments, which prevents easy independent verification. I would view this as a useful accompanying code release rather than a fully reproducible artifact. Recommendation: weak accept on code-paper alignment, with significant concerns about reproducibility and packaging.

Extension Review (G)

Reviewer G
Ext: 9.0 Overall: 9.0
Extension Quality (9.0/10)
This submission is a strong and clearly articulated extension of Flow-of-Options. It explicitly anchors itself in the reference paper, targets a concrete mechanism inside FoO (the scalar top-b harmonization / path-choice routine), and proposes a conceptually coherent replacement based on Pareto-front filtering over quality, diversity, and cost. That is a genuine methodological extension rather than a superficial variant: it preserves FoO’s layered option-graph reasoning setup while modifying the option-selection inductive bias in a way directly motivated by limitations visible in the original framework, especially scalar collapse of competing objectives and deployment cost concerns. The paper also provides additional experiments, ablations, implementation details, and reproducibility infrastructure that go beyond the reference. Its main weakness as an extension is scope: it does not validate on the original FoO benchmark families or a clearly new application domain, instead using a small depth-2 mixed reasoning benchmark with substantial synthetic-mode involvement, so the continuity is stronger than the breadth of demonstrated downstream impact. Still, as a next-step algorithmic refinement of FoO’s selection rule, it is substantive, novel, and well aligned with the reference paper’s goals.
Overall Verdict (9.0/10)
This paper represents a meaningful and well-motivated continuation of Flow-of-Options. Its central contribution—a tuning-free Pareto-based selector replacing scalarized option ranking—fits naturally within the original framework and directly addresses an identifiable weakness in FoO’s decision policy. Although the empirical validation is narrower and less convincing than the reference paper’s main benchmark suite, the conceptual continuity is excellent and the novelty is real. I would view this as a valuable follow-up extension, especially for readers interested in cost-aware and multi-objective reasoning control within FoO-style systems.

Detailed Reviews

Paper Reviews (A-E)

Reviewer A
Tech: 3.0 Nov: 5.0 Clar: 7.0 Sig: 6.0 Overall: 5.2
Technical Quality (3.0/10)
The paper is reasonably detailed procedurally, but the technical case is weak under a strict rigor standard. The method is described mostly operationally rather than formally, with limited notation and no precise objective for the modified search policy. Several core claims are only weakly supported: the empirical study is extremely small, uses only 12 hand-written tasks, only 4 walks per condition, and relies on the same LLM family as both generator and judge, which introduces substantial evaluation bias. The reported improvement is explicitly acknowledged to be statistically indistinguishable from noise, and no ablation isolates the contributions of the four proposed changes. There are also methodological concerns around using judge scores as both evaluation target and search signal, plus limited fairness of comparison because the adaptive method changes the option budget while the baseline remains fixed. On the positive side, assumptions and limitations are stated candidly, and implementation details and hyperparameters are described well enough for approximate reproduction.
Novelty (5.0/10)
The combination of MMR selection, adaptive branching, diversity-aware walk penalties, and uncertainty-aware pruning is plausibly new in the specific FoO setting, and the paper does give proper credit to FoO and MMR. That said, the contribution appears closer to an engineering augmentation than a fundamentally new reasoning framework. Related-work positioning is only partial: the paper cites a few foundational items but does not comprehensively discuss nearby diversity-aware decoding, search, reranking, or uncertainty-based pruning literature. Because comparisons are limited and the empirical support is thin, the novelty claims are acceptable only in a modest sense and should not be interpreted as a strong advance over the state of the art.
Clarity (7.0/10)
The paper is generally easy to read and well organized. The introduction states the problem, the modifications, and the intended evaluation scope clearly, and the methods section provides enough detail for an expert reader to reconstruct the workflow. Results are also interpreted carefully, with appropriate caveats about small sample size and lack of ceiling improvement. However, clarity is reduced by the absence of figures or tables in the paper body despite references to summary files, and by somewhat informal notation and mixed prose/code-style exposition. For a technically oriented paper, the lack of a cleaner formal presentation makes some design choices harder to verify than they should be.
Significance (6.0/10)
The problem of improving diversity and robustness in LLM reasoning search is relevant and timely, and the proposed ideas are potentially applicable beyond the exact benchmark used here. The paper also opens reasonable follow-up directions around adaptive search policies and diversity-aware option lattices. However, the demonstrated effect is small, the best-path ceiling does not improve, and the evaluation setting is too limited to support claims of broad practical impact. As presented, this is more of a diagnostic pilot than a result likely to materially shift practice, though it could still be useful as a stepping stone for stronger future work.
Overall Verdict (5.2/10)
This paper presents a coherent and clearly written extension of Flow-of-Options, but the technical evidence is not strong enough to support a favorable rigor-focused recommendation in its current form. The central empirical gain is very small, statistically inconclusive, and confounded by a judge-based evaluation setup that also supplies the optimization signal. The method bundle is plausible and potentially useful, yet the absence of ablations, formal analysis, and broader evaluation prevents me from concluding that the proposed changes are technically validated. My recommendation is borderline negative: promising pilot ideas, but not sufficiently rigorous for acceptance as a technical contribution.
Reviewer B
Tech: 4.0 Nov: 3.0 Clar: 8.0 Sig: 5.0 Overall: 5.0
Technical Quality (4.0/10)
The paper is reasonably reproducible at the implementation level: it specifies prompts, hyperparameters, walk counts, beam widths, and the exact modifications made to the FoO pipeline. Assumptions and limitations are also stated candidly. However, the technical case is weak by a demanding standard. The method is described mostly procedurally rather than formally, there is no meaningful validation of individual design choices, and the empirical evidence is far too small and underpowered to support strong conclusions. The baseline comparison is limited to an internal reimplementation on 12 hand-written tasks with the same model also serving as judge, which introduces substantial risk of bias and circularity. I also see a major methodological issue in aggregating four distinct heuristics into one bundle without ablation, then attributing observed behavior to the combined design.
Novelty (3.0/10)
From a novelty and literature perspective, this is the paper's weakest dimension. The contribution appears to be a combination of familiar heuristics—MMR reranking, adaptive branching, diversity penalties, and uncertainty-aware pruning—applied to an existing FoO framework. That can still be useful engineering, but the paper does not convincingly establish that this combination rises above incremental modification. More importantly, the related-work positioning is too narrow. The manuscript cites FoO, MMR, ToT, and self-consistency, but does not engage sufficiently with the broader literature on diverse decoding/search, reranking, bandit-style uncertainty handling, verifier/judge-based reasoning evaluation, or adaptive search in LLM planning. As a result, the novelty claims are only partially supported, and the paper is not positioned against the true landscape of prior art at the level expected for a strong originality claim.
Clarity (8.0/10)
The manuscript is easy to follow and generally well written. The introduction clearly explains the original FoO setup, motivates the proposed changes, and previews the evaluation. The methods and results sections are logically organized, and an expert reader can reconstruct the workflow. Notation is light but mostly consistent. The main clarity weakness is presentation support: there are no figures or tables in the paper body despite references to summary files, so some claims are left as prose summaries rather than clearly visualized evidence. Still, as a written artifact, it is substantially clearer than many short systems papers.
Significance (5.0/10)
The problem of improving diversity and robustness in LLM reasoning search is important and timely, and the proposed hooks could be useful for follow-up work. The paper also highlights an interesting distinction between improving average walk quality and improving the best reachable path, which is a meaningful diagnostic angle. That said, the demonstrated gains are extremely modest: a +2.1 percentage-point lift with heavily overlapping confidence intervals and no improvement in best-walk score. On the evidence provided, this does not yet constitute a substantial advance that would justify adoption or change practice. The work is potentially useful as a small exploratory study, but its current empirical scope limits its likely impact.
Overall Verdict (5.0/10)
This submission is clearly written and reasonably reproducible, but as Reviewer B I find the originality and literature positioning insufficient for a strong recommendation. The core idea is a bundle of established heuristics layered onto FoO, and the paper does not adequately situate these choices relative to the broader prior art on diversity-aware search and judge-based reasoning systems. Empirically, the study is too small to support strong claims, and the reported gains are modest and statistically inconclusive. I would view this as a preliminary diagnostic engineering note rather than a convincing research advance in its current form.

Results are also interpreted with appropriate caution. The main clarity weakness is the absence of figures or tables in the paper body despite references to summaries and CSV outputs; this limits communicative effectiveness, especially for a paper centered on search behavior and diversity changes. Still, the prose itself is strong and accessible to adjacent audiences.
Significance (5.0/10)
The problem is relevant: improving robustness of LLM reasoning search under small budgets and without executable rewards is timely and practically meaningful. The proposed ideas are not confined to the exact benchmark and could, in principle, transfer to other multi-option reasoning or planning settings. That said, the demonstrated impact is currently modest. The headline gain is only +2.1 points with overlapping confidence intervals, the ceiling does not improve, and the strongest qualitative case is essentially one task where the method reduces mid-walk errors. From a field-impact perspective, that is not enough to justify adoption or to suggest that this work will materially change practice. The paper is better viewed as a small diagnostic pilot that may enable future research, rather than as a significant advance in how the community should approach LLM reasoning search.
Overall Verdict (5.5/10)
This paper presents a careful and clearly described modification of Flow-of-Options with a sensible emphasis on diversity-aware search, and I appreciate the authors' restraint in acknowledging the small scale and inconclusive statistics. However, the empirical evidence is too limited and the observed gains too modest to support strong claims of advancement or likely field impact. The contribution appears primarily incremental, with several known heuristics combined in one package and evaluated on a very small, judge-dependent benchmark. I would encourage expansion into a substantially broader and more rigorous study with ablations, stronger baselines, and clearer evidence that the method changes outcomes at a meaningful scale before publication.

Code Review (F)

Reviewer F
Tech: 1.0 Repro: 0.0 Correct: 5.0 Align: 10.0 Overall: 4.0
Code Technical Quality (1.0/10)
The script is readable and reasonably organized into helpers for prompting, caching, graph construction, walking, and aggregation, and naming is generally clear. However, it remains a monolithic experiment script rather than a well-factored, maintainable software artifact. Documentation is partial, style/linting evidence is absent, dependency management is unspecified, and there are multiple signs that the code is research-prototype quality rather than production-grade: hard-coded absolute paths, limited validation, broad exception handling, sparse logging, and no tests or CI hooks.
Code Reproducibility (0.0/10)
Reproducibility is the weakest aspect. Although the script fixes Python and NumPy seeds and includes an on-disk cache intended to stabilize repeated LLM calls, execution is not turnkey: it depends on a local repository layout, AWS Bedrock credentials, proprietary model access, and unspecified versions of Python packages and external utilities. The environment is not containerized, resources are not packaged in a permanent repository, configuration is embedded in code rather than external machine-readable configs, and full independent verification of reported numbers is not possible from the submission alone.
Code Correctness (5.0/10)
The implementation appears internally consistent for many core mechanics: graph construction, max-update edge propagation, per-layer option generation, TF-IDF embedding, MMR-style selection, and summary aggregation are coherent. Still, several correctness risks remain. The adaptive variant conditions later option generation on the first option as a placeholder instead of actual walk-conditioned history, pruning logic is a heuristic rather than a faithful uncertainty estimate, API robustness is limited, and the code is not guaranteed free of runtime/environmental failures because it relies on external services, local filesystem assumptions, and unverified helper APIs. Overall, it is plausible as a prototype implementation, but not fully validated or provably correct across the full claimed domain.
Code-Paper Alignment (10.0/10)
Alignment between the described method and the submitted code is strong. The script directly implements the baseline and diversity-adaptive variants described in the submission, including MMR over an expanded pool, adaptive k selection, walk-time diversity penalty, uncertainty-based pruning, Bedrock-backed generation/judging, caching, and the reported task domains and output artifacts. Variable names and outputs closely match the write-up, and the code is clearly useful for validating the implementation-side claims of the submission.
Overall Verdict (4.0/10)
This is a credible research prototype with strong paper-code alignment and a reasonably clear implementation of the claimed extensions, but it falls short on software quality and especially on reproducibility. The main blockers are the hard-coded local paths, dependence on proprietary Bedrock access, lack of pinned environment specifications, and absence of tests or packaging needed for independent reruns. I would view the code as sufficient to demonstrate intent and approximate implementation fidelity, but not sufficient for robust third-party reproduction of the reported results. Recommendation: weak reject from a code/reproducibility standpoint unless the authors provide a self-contained environment and cleaner execution path.

Extension Review (G)

Reviewer G
Ext: 9.0 Overall: 9.0
Extension Quality (9.0/10)
This submission is clearly positioned as an extension of Flow-of-Options and shows strong continuity with the reference paper at the level of problem framing, graph-based option search, traversal, and value propagation. It directly identifies concrete conservative design choices in FoO and proposes a coherent package of modifications to the option-selection layer: MMR-based diversification, adaptive per-step option budgets, diversity-aware walk penalties, and uncertainty-aware pruning. That is a meaningful methodological extension rather than a superficial rebranding. The paper also transfers FoO from execution-scored AutoML workflows to a mixed LLM reasoning benchmark where scoring comes from an LLM judge, which is a legitimate new application domain and a useful stress test of the framework’s portability. The main weakness is that the empirical scope is small and shallow relative to the reference paper, and the work does not substantially deepen FoO’s theoretical foundations; it is more an engineering and evaluation extension than a theoretical one. Still, readers of the original paper would likely regard this as a valuable and conceptually faithful next step, especially as a targeted study of how diversity mechanisms affect FoO behavior outside the original AutoML setting.
Overall Verdict (9.0/10)
This is a strong extension paper. It preserves the central FoO idea of structured multi-option reasoning while introducing nontrivial mechanisms that directly address identifiable limitations in the original design. The evidence is modest in scale, so the claims should remain diagnostic rather than broad, but the continuity with the reference paper is excellent and the novelty is genuine. I would recommend it as a worthwhile next-step extension, with the caveat that larger-scale validation would strengthen its impact.

Paper Reviews (A-E)

Reviewer A
Tech: 5.0 Nov: 1.0 Clar: 9.0 Sig: 4.0 Overall: 4.8
Technical Quality (5.0/10)
The paper is reasonably explicit about the synthetic benchmark construction, utility formula, metrics, ablations, and seed protocol, so the experimental procedure is more reproducible than many short method papers. It also states key assumptions and includes ablation-style analyses and limitations. However, from a rigor standpoint the work remains weak. The algorithm is only semi-formal: several key quantities such as coherence, valid edge checks, claim extraction, and verification are described operationally but not formally specified enough to guarantee unambiguous implementation. More importantly, the paper makes strong methodological claims from a fully synthetic benchmark whose metrics are tightly aligned with the proposed method design; this creates substantial risk of benchmark-method co-design bias. The near-perfect evidence_support result (0.999) and tiny confidence intervals suggest a highly constrained evaluation setting, but the paper does not sufficiently justify why these results should be interpreted as evidence of broader technical effectiveness. The max-update step is asserted rather than theoretically motivated, and no complexity analysis is given despite graph search and verification being central. I did not identify a blatant arithmetic error in the reported utility trends, but the experimental framing has significant validity limitations that prevent a stronger technical assessment.
Novelty (1.0/10)
The claimed novelty is plausible at a high level—integrating evidence into option generation, edge validity, walk selection, and verification within a FoO-style pipeline—but the paper does not establish this novelty convincingly. Related work coverage is thin and the references section is notably problematic, including duplicates and low-signal entries. Comparisons are restricted to internal ablations and a vanilla baseline; there is no serious attempt to compare against broader evidence-grounded planning, retrieval, verification, or structured-generation methods. As written, the work looks more like a benchmark-specific variant and recombination of known ingredients than a clearly demonstrated methodological advance. Novelty claims are therefore not well substantiated.
Clarity (9.0/10)
The paper is clearly organized and generally easy to read. The problem, the FoO gap being targeted, the proposed extension, and the benchmark design are all stated early and revisited consistently. Tables are readable, results are contextualized, and the ablation narrative is straightforward. The algorithm sketch is concise and understandable for an expert reader. The main clarity weakness is that notation and definitions are not consistently formal: some symbols are introduced cleanly, but several core objects and scoring functions remain partly informal, and the duplicated/low-quality references section reduces polish. Still, on presentation alone, the paper is substantially clearer than it is rigorous.
Significance (4.0/10)
The target problem—grounded scientific hypothesis generation/falsification with agentic planning—is important and timely, and the paper does suggest potentially useful follow-up directions in benchmark design and evidence-aware control. However, the demonstrated impact is limited because all evidence comes from a synthetic, tightly controlled setting with custom metrics. There is no persuasive argument that the large reported gains would transfer to realistic scientific writing or hypothesis-generation workflows. The method may be useful as a benchmarked design idea, but the paper does not yet show substantial advancement likely to change practice or strongly influence the field. Its significance is therefore modest rather than high.
Overall Verdict (4.8/10)
This paper is clearly written and comparatively reproducible, but its technical evidence is not strong enough to support the broader methodological claims. The central weakness is external validity: the evaluation is entirely synthetic, the metrics are closely matched to the proposed design, and the baseline set is too narrow to establish meaningful superiority. I appreciate the ablations and explicit limitations, but from a rigor-first perspective the work feels under-validated and under-positioned relative to adjacent prior art. My recommendation is weak reject in its current form, with the main path to improvement being stronger formalization, broader and fairer baselines, and evaluation on more realistic non-synthetic settings.
Reviewer B
Tech: 5.0 Nov: 0.0 Clar: 8.0 Sig: 3.0 Overall: 4.0
Technical Quality (5.0/10)
The paper is reasonably explicit about its synthetic benchmark, utility formula, ablations, and fixed-seed setup, and it does provide enough implementation-level description to broadly reproduce the reported tables. I also appreciate that the limitations of the synthetic setting are acknowledged and that ablations are included. However, the technical foundation remains weak in important ways: the algorithm is only semi-formal, several key objects are underspecified, and there is no real complexity analysis despite graph construction and verification being central. More seriously, the evaluation is methodologically narrow and internally defined: the metrics are bespoke, closely aligned with the proposed method, and the baselines are limited to variants of the same framework rather than broader evidence-grounded agentic or retrieval-based alternatives. The near-perfect evidence_support result and tiny confidence intervals on a templated synthetic benchmark make the claims look more like benchmark engineering than robust technical validation.
Novelty (0.0/10)
From a novelty and literature-positioning perspective, this paper is not convincing. The core idea—injecting evidence or retrieval signals into generation, scoring, selection, and verification loops for agentic reasoning—is a natural combination of already active themes from search-based reasoning, retrieval-grounded generation, and verifier-based filtering. The paper does not establish a sufficiently crisp conceptual or algorithmic distinction from those existing directions, and its related-work coverage is extremely sparse. It cites only a handful of broad papers and then includes duplicated and low-quality reference entries, while overlooking a large body of adjacent work on evidence-grounded scientific writing, citation-grounded generation, retrieval-augmented planning, verifier-guided agents, multi-step hypothesis generation, and scientific discovery assistants. Because the literature review is so incomplete, the novelty claims are overstated and the positioning relative to prior art is inadequate.
Clarity (8.0/10)
The manuscript is generally easy to follow. The introduction states the problem, the claimed FoO gap, and the proposed evidence-guided extension clearly, and the sections progress in a sensible order from related work to method, setup, results, analysis, and limitations. The prose is mostly readable and direct. That said, the notation is thinner and less disciplined than ideal for a method paper, and the figures are not especially informative beyond visually restating the tables; they do not add much analytical insight. Still, an expert reader can understand what was done, how the benchmark is organized, and what conclusions the authors wish to draw.
Significance (3.0/10)
The problem area—scientific hypothesis generation and falsification with grounded evidence—is certainly important and timely. The paper also points toward useful future work directions, especially around moving from synthetic evaluation to expert-annotated real tasks. However, the present contribution has limited demonstrated significance because all results are confined to a synthetic benchmark whose metrics are largely constructed around the method’s own desiderata. There is no evidence yet that the approach would materially affect real scientific writing or discovery workflows, nor that the gains would justify adoption outside this benchmark. As written, the work feels more like an internal benchmark study than a substantial advance likely to shift practice.
Overall Verdict (4.0/10)
This paper is clearly written and reasonably organized, but its main weakness is novelty and scholarly positioning. The proposed method appears to be an intuitive recombination of existing ideas from retrieval-grounded generation, search over reasoning options, and verification, yet the manuscript does not engage the broader literature needed to substantiate a strong originality claim. The synthetic evaluation is reproducible but narrow, with bespoke metrics and insufficiently broad baselines, so the reported gains do not translate into a compelling field-level contribution. I would not recommend acceptance in its current form; the work would need a much stronger related-work treatment, more careful novelty delimitation, and validation on more realistic tasks.

Overall, the paper is technically serviceable as a controlled synthetic study, but not technically convincing as evidence of a robust advance.

Function and variable names largely mirror paper terminology, and the script is clearly designed to regenerate the reported claims.
Overall Verdict (6.8/10)
