Its main weaknesses are limited documentation, lack of typed/defensive engineering, and incomplete environment specification. Recommendation: accept the code artifact as a credible reproduction script for the paper’s synthetic claims, but not as high-quality or production-ready research software.

Extension Review (G)

Reviewer G
Ext: 9.0 Overall: 9.0
Extension Quality (9.0/10)
The submission is clearly positioned as an extension of Flow-of-Options and repeatedly identifies a specific FoO gap: vanilla FoO does not explicitly optimize for evidence-grounded scientific writing. It preserves the core FoO structure—option generation, graph edges, walk selection, value updates, and verification—while adapting these components to a new domain, namely scientific hypothesis generation and falsification framed as paper construction. The most meaningful continuity lies in making evidence support part of the option-flow loop itself, affecting generation, edge validity, scoring, and final verification. This is a substantive methodological extension rather than a cosmetic rebranding, and the ablation design gives at least some empirical support for the added components. The main weakness is that the advance is mostly procedural and application-oriented rather than theoretical: it does not significantly deepen the underlying FoO framework beyond introducing evidence-guided control rules in a synthetic benchmark. Still, as an extension paper, it maintains strong continuity with the reference work and would likely be seen by FoO readers as a reasonable and valuable next step.
Overall Verdict (9.0/10)
This paper succeeds as a meaningful extension of the reference FoO work. It is explicit about the baseline, preserves the central graph-based reasoning paradigm, and extends it in a coherent new direction by integrating evidence-awareness into option generation, edge constraints, and verification. The application to scientific hypothesis-generation-style writing is novel and the ablations support the claim that the added mechanisms matter. The main limitation is that the contribution is better characterized as methodological adaptation and domain transfer than as a deeper theoretical advance, and the evaluation remains synthetic; nevertheless, the continuity and novelty are both strong.

Detailed Reviews

Paper Reviews (A-E)

Reviewer A
Tech: 2.0 Nov: 2.0 Clar: 7.0 Sig: 5.0 Overall: 4.0
Technical Quality (2.0/10)
The paper states a concrete algorithmic modification, but the technical standard is not met. The method is only semi-formalized, with important ambiguities around the objective definitions, normalization, dominance relation, and the claim that the one-objective case reduces exactly to scalar best-first selection. The central theoretical and practical claims are not supported by convincing evidence because all experiments used synthetic fallback scores unrelated to the intended LLM behavior, so the evaluation does not validate the claimed quality-diversity-cost tradeoff. Reproducibility details are reasonably strong, and the paper is commendably explicit about limitations, but there are major methodological weaknesses: the diversity metric is questionable as a semantic proxy, the cost proxy is crude, complexity is not analyzed, fairness of comparisons is weak, and the ablations are uninformative in the fully synthetic regime.
Novelty (2.0/10)
The core idea appears to be an incremental adaptation of a standard multi-objective selection principle, namely Pareto filtering with NSGA-II-style crowding distance, to a specific option-selection step in Flow-of-Options. That may be a reasonable engineering variant, but the paper does not establish novelty at a high bar. The related-work discussion is thin, comparisons to broader multi-objective decoding or reranking literature are missing, and novelty claims are stronger than the evidence warrants. The paper does give explicit credit to FoO and NSGA-II, and it positions itself relative to FoO, but it does not convincingly show advancement beyond a fairly direct substitution of a known selection rule.
Clarity (7.0/10)
The paper is generally easy to read and well organized. The introduction clearly frames the problem, motivates the proposed modification, and previews the intended contribution. The methods and experimental setup are described in sufficient prose for an expert reader to follow the workflow. However, clarity is limited by imprecise notation and a lack of full formal specification, especially for the objective conventions and selection procedure. The paper contains one table but no figures, so some checklist items about visual communication are unmet; still, the narrative interpretation of results is straightforward and accessible.
Significance (5.0/10)
The problem being targeted is relevant: balancing quality, diversity, and cost in LLM reasoning is important and timely, and the method could in principle apply beyond the specific FoO setting. The paper also suggests plausible practical beneficiaries in agentic reasoning and constrained inference. That said, the present submission does not demonstrate substantial advancement or improvements that would justify adoption, because the empirical results are effectively null and obtained entirely in a synthetic fallback regime. As a result, the current work is better viewed as a proof-of-mechanism idea that may enable follow-up research rather than an established advance likely to shift practice.
Overall Verdict (4.0/10)
This paper presents a plausible multi-objective modification to Flow-of-Options, but from a rigor standpoint the submission is not yet convincing. The algorithmic idea is straightforward and the paper is transparent about its failure to obtain live LLM results, yet that transparency does not compensate for the lack of empirical validation on the actual target setting. Several technical claims are under-justified, some method details remain underspecified, and the evidence is too weak to support strong conclusions about effectiveness or impact. My recommendation is reject in its current form, with encouragement to resubmit after a real evaluation, tighter formalization, and stronger positioning against related multi-objective selection methods.
Reviewer B
Tech: 5.0 Nov: 1.0 Clar: 8.0 Sig: 5.0 Overall: 4.8
Technical Quality (5.0/10)

It is also commendably explicit about the synthetic fallback and its implications. However, the empirical validation is fundamentally inadequate for supporting the main claims because all results are from synthetic scores rather than live LLM behavior, and the reported outcomes are essentially null across the main methods. Novelty (1.0/10)
From a novelty and literature perspective, this submission is weak. The central idea—replacing scalar beam-style option selection with a Pareto-front filter over quality, diversity, and cost plus NSGA-II crowding distance—is a straightforward adaptation of standard multi-objective optimization machinery to an existing reasoning framework. The paper cites FoO and NSGA-II, but the related-work coverage is narrow and does not seriously engage adjacent literature on multi-objective decoding, diverse beam search, reranking under cost constraints, MBR-style selection, or broader LLM inference-time search methods that are directly relevant to the contribution. Clarity (8.0/10)

Significance (5.0/10)

The proposed modification could be useful as a lightweight design pattern, and it may inspire follow-up work on multi-objective option selection. That said, the present paper does not demonstrate substantial gains, does not show live-model evidence, and does not establish that the method changes practice or materially advances the state of the art. Its significance is therefore more prospective than demonstrated.
Overall Verdict (4.8/10)
This paper identifies a real issue in Flow-of-Options-style reasoning—single-objective selection can erase diversity and ignore cost—and proposes a simple multi-objective alternative. The manuscript is clear and appropriately candid about its failed live evaluation, but that candor also reveals the core problem: the paper currently offers mainly a plausible algorithmic tweak with synthetic proof-of-mechanism, not convincing evidence of benefit. From a novelty and literature standpoint, the contribution appears incremental and insufficiently situated in the broader body of work on multi-objective and diversity-aware search. I would not recommend acceptance in its current form, though a substantially stronger revision with real experiments and deeper related-work positioning could become worthwhile.

Reviewer D
Tech: 5.0 Nov: 3.0 Clar: 9.0 Sig: 5.0 Overall: 5.5
Technical Quality (5.0/10)
The paper is reasonably well specified at the algorithm level: the objectives are defined, the Pareto-front and crowding-distance selection rule are described, and the experimental setup includes seeds, candidate counts, depth, and bootstrap details. The authors are also commendably explicit that the reported run used only synthetic fallback scores. However, from a feasibility and implementation standpoint, this severely weakens the evidentiary basis of the work. The central claims concern balancing quality, diversity, and token cost in actual LLM reasoning, yet no live LLM evaluation was completed, and the synthetic regime is acknowledged to produce essentially null and non-generalizable results. There are also methodological concerns: diversity is measured via TF-IDF character n-grams rather than a stronger semantic metric, self-rated quality is used without validating reliability, and the claim that the method is a drop-in replacement is not supported by complexity or systems-level analysis. The ablations exist, but because all Pareto variants collapse to identical outcomes under synthetic scoring, they do little to validate the design choices in practice.
Novelty (3.0/10)
The core idea—replacing scalar option ranking with a multi-objective Pareto-front selector using crowding distance—is sensible but modest. It appears to be a direct adaptation of standard multi-objective optimization machinery to an existing FoO selection step rather than a substantially new reasoning framework. The paper does credit FoO and NSGA-II, but the related-work discussion is thin for a claim positioned around diversity-cost-quality tradeoffs in LLM inference. There is limited engagement with adjacent literature on multi-objective decoding, reranking, budget-aware inference, or diversity-promoting search, making it difficult to judge whether this is genuinely new relative to the broader space. Because empirical evidence is absent in the real setting, the novelty claims also feel somewhat overstated relative to what is actually demonstrated.
Clarity (9.0/10)
The paper is clearly written and easy to follow. The introduction states the problem, motivates the proposed replacement of the original selection rule, and previews the intended benefits. The method section is structured logically, with clear descriptions of the baseline, the Pareto extension, and the controls. Notation is mostly consistent and simple enough for an expert reader. The results are also interpreted candidly, especially the admission that the current evidence is only proof-of-mechanism under synthetic scores. The main clarity weakness is presentation: there are no figures, and the sole table is straightforward but does not provide deeper diagnostic insight into how or when Pareto selection materially changes behavior.
Significance (5.0/10)
The problem is important: practical LLM reasoning systems do face real tradeoffs between answer quality, diversity of explored approaches, and inference cost. The proposed mechanism could plausibly be useful beyond the specific FoO setup, and the paper identifies practical application contexts clearly. That said, significance depends on demonstrated impact, and the current submission does not show meaningful empirical gains over the baseline in the target setting. In fact, the reported results are intentionally synthetic and largely tied across methods. As a result, while the idea opens reasonable follow-up directions, the present paper does not yet establish that the method changes practice, justifies adoption, or materially advances the state of the art.
Overall Verdict (5.5/10)
This submission is clear, candid, and practically motivated, and the proposed selection rule is easy to understand and potentially easy to integrate into FoO-style systems. However, the paper’s main weakness is decisive: it does not provide real empirical validation of the claimed benefits, because all reported experiments used synthetic fallback scores after the live evaluation failed. From a feasibility and deployment perspective, that leaves the paper short of demonstrating that the method actually improves reasoning quality-diversity-cost tradeoffs in practice. I view this as an interesting proof-of-mechanism and a plausible workshop artifact, but not yet a strong research paper until live, resource-aware evaluation is completed and the practical tradeoffs are substantiated.
Reviewer E
Tech: 5.0 Nov: 4.0 Clar: 9.0 Sig: 5.0 Overall: 5.8
Technical Quality (5.0/10)
The paper gives a reasonably formal description of the proposed Pareto-based replacement and the experimental protocol is described in enough detail to be rerun. Assumptions and limitations are also stated candidly. However, the central weakness is that the empirical evaluation did not run on live LLM outputs at all; every reported result comes from synthetic fallback scores that the authors themselves describe as producing expected null effects. That said, from an impact perspective the current submission falls well short of demonstrating field-level significance. The contribution is best viewed as a potentially useful hypothesis or engineering direction rather than a demonstrated impactful advance.
Overall Verdict (5.8/10)

The method itself is also fairly incremental, adapting standard Pareto-selection machinery to FoO rather than introducing a substantively new reasoning paradigm. I would encourage resubmission after a real LLM-based evaluation establishes whether the claimed quality-diversity-cost tradeoff produces meaningful gains in practice.

Code Review (F)

Reviewer F
Tech: 3.0 Repro: 1.0 Correct: 6.0 Align: 10.0 Overall: 5.0
Code Technical Quality (3.0/10)
The code is reasonably well structured for a research prototype, with clear functional decomposition for caching, option generation, Pareto utilities, execution, judging, aggregation, and reporting. Naming is generally strong and the implementation is readable. However, it falls short of production-grade quality: documentation is partial rather than comprehensive, there is no evidence of linting or style enforcement, dependencies are not pinned, usage setup is not self-contained, and there are correctness-adjacent quality issues such as duplicated selection calls in the unused pick_options helper and hard-coded absolute paths. Overall, this is solid experiment code, not enterprise-quality software.
Code Reproducibility (1.0/10)
Reproducibility is the weakest aspect. While the script fixes seeds, caches LLM calls, provides deterministic synthetic fallback, and records outputs to JSON/CSV/Markdown, execution is not immediate because it depends on a specific local repository layout and hard-coded absolute filesystem paths. The environment is not containerized or lock-filed, dependencies and versions are unspecified, and there is no machine-readable config for runtime parameters. The reported synthetic fallback makes the mechanism reproducible in principle, but not the full claimed experiment in a self-contained, independently verifiable way.
Code Correctness (6.0/10)
Core implementation quality is mixed but generally competent. The Pareto front logic, crowding distance, normalization, synthetic fallback, and bootstrap aggregation are implemented coherently, and there are useful startup unit tests for Pareto behavior. That said, the code is not free of flaws: API robustness is limited, there are no explicit timeouts or rate-limit controls around remote calls, hard-coded paths can cause runtime failures, and the implementation is not guaranteed correct for all operational environments. The core algorithmic idea appears correctly encoded, but the surrounding execution stack is not fully hardened.
Code-Paper Alignment (10.0/10)
Alignment between the described method and the code is strong. The implementation directly encodes the paper’s central contribution: multi-objective option selection using quality, diversity, and cost; Pareto front extraction; crowding-distance tie-breaking; and the stated ablations and baselines. The experiment structure, metrics, reporting fields, and synthetic fallback narrative all correspond closely to the manuscript’s claims. As submitted, the code is a good proof-of-mechanism implementation of the proposed paper idea.
Overall Verdict (5.0/10)
This submission provides a credible research prototype that captures the paper’s core Pareto-selection contribution and associated ablations with good paper-code alignment. The main strengths are readable structure, deterministic seeding, graceful fallback behavior, and a faithful implementation of the proposed multi-objective selection logic. The main weaknesses are reproducibility and software hardening: the script is tied to absolute local paths, lacks environment specification and pinned dependencies, and is not self-contained enough for independent reruns. My recommendation is borderline positive for methodological prototype value, but the code would need substantial packaging and environment cleanup to meet a strong reproducibility standard.

Extension Review (G)

Reviewer G
Ext: 9.0 Overall: 9.0
Extension Quality (9.0/10)
This paper is a strong and conceptually continuous extension of Flow-of-Options. It explicitly situates itself as a localized modification to FoO’s option-selection mechanism and preserves the original graph structure, consistency checking, prompting setup, and walk execution loop, which demonstrates clear continuity with the reference work. The core contribution—replacing scalar edge-value selection with a multi-objective Pareto-front policy over quality, diversity, and token cost—directly addresses a plausible limitation of the original FoO framework: that its search policy optimizes only a single scalar and may collapse diversity or ignore efficiency. This is a meaningful methodological extension rather than a superficial rebranding, because it changes the decision rule governing exploration while remaining faithful to FoO’s original motivation of preserving alternative reasoning paths. The work also advances the framework theoretically by recasting FoO’s branch selection as a multi-objective optimization problem and introducing Pareto non-domination plus crowding distance as principled selection criteria. That said, the empirical validation is mixed in strength. The paper does provide additional experiments, controls, and ablations beyond the reference paper, but all reported trials used synthetic fallback scores due to Bedrock failure, which limits the practical force of the evidence. Also, while the paper discusses pharmaceutical ML and general LLM agents as motivation, it does not truly demonstrate a new application domain beyond the FoO setting; the experiments are on small reasoning tasks rather than a substantial new domain benchmark. Even with those caveats, readers of the original FoO paper would likely view this as a valuable next step because it preserves FoO’s logic while proposing a clear, testable improvement to one of its central mechanisms.
Overall Verdict (9.0/10)
This submission is a high-quality extension of the reference paper. It maintains strong conceptual continuity with Flow-of-Options while introducing a genuinely novel multi-objective selection rule that fits naturally into the original framework and addresses a credible limitation of scalar beam-style ranking. The main weakness is empirical: because the evaluation fell back to synthetic scores, the evidence supports proof-of-mechanism more than demonstrated real-world improvement. Even so, as an extension paper, it is substantive, well-motivated, and likely useful to readers interested in pushing FoO toward more principled tradeoff-aware reasoning.

Detailed Reviews

Paper Reviews (A-E)

Reviewer A
Tech: 5.0 Nov: 1.0 Clar: 7.0 Sig: 5.0 Overall: 4.5
Technical Quality (5.0/10)
The paper has a reasonably clear methodological skeleton: the cascade evaluators, beam promotion policy, uncertainty-aware promotion, and planned metrics are all described with enough structure to understand the intended system. It also commendably reports a negative main result and identifies low surrogate–full-fidelity agreement as the proximate failure mode. However, the technical rigor is substantially weakened by serious internal inconsistencies and undercutting methodological details. The Methods section claims 500 problems per benchmark, 100 held-out tuning problems, a 2,000-problem trace corpus, and ~1.2M tuples, whereas the Results report only 60 problems and 12,800 tuples. This discrepancy is major and unresolved. The pre-registered hypothesis is not merely unsupported but contradicted by the reported MFES accuracy collapse, yet figure captions still claim minimal degradation, creating a methodological and interpretive inconsistency. Baseline naming is also confusing: B1 is defined as uniform mid-fidelity E2, but the results describe it as a cheap surrogate-only baseline. Reproducibility is incomplete because critical hyperparameters and implementation choices are missing or left as examples (e.g., exact DAG depth, option branching, beam width used in final runs, alpha_2 values tested, beta value, model identities, judge prompts/rubrics, training details for LightGBM, calibration protocol, and refit procedure). There is no complexity analysis despite cost-scaled routing being the core contribution. Overall, the work is not free of major technical flaws, and the empirical evidence is too internally inconsistent to support strong conclusions.
Novelty (1.0/10)
The idea of multi-fidelity evaluation for pruning search candidates is plausible, but the paper does not establish novelty to a rigorous standard. The claim of being the first principled multi-fidelity schedule within Flow-of-Options is not convincingly supported. Related work discussion is thin, citation quality is uneven, and several references are informal web pages, surveys, GitHub pages, or even seemingly self-referential/internal items rather than a careful scholarly positioning against the strongest prior literature on multi-fidelity optimization, beam search pruning, cascaded evaluators, or surrogate-assisted search. Because the paper does not adequately situate itself relative to these adjacent areas, it is difficult to tell whether the contribution is genuinely novel or an application-level recombination of known ideas. The comparisons are also not comprehensive enough to support a strong novelty claim, and the wording around contributions feels overstated relative to the evidence presented.
Clarity (7.0/10)
At a high level, the paper is easy to follow: the problem is clearly stated, the motivation is understandable, and the organization from introduction through methods and results is conventional and readable. The central idea of routing early candidates through cheaper evaluators before reserving full-fidelity evaluation for beam survivors is presented clearly enough for an expert reader to grasp. The results narrative is also straightforward and does not hide the negative outcome. That said, clarity degrades substantially on close inspection. Notation is only partially formalized and several symbols and entities are inconsistently used. In particular, the evaluator labels and baseline descriptions conflict between Methods and Results, and figure captions appear mismatched to the actual findings, even asserting minimal degradation or dominance despite the text reporting severe accuracy loss. The paper includes figures but no tables summarizing exact experimental settings or all key numbers, and the captions are sometimes misleading rather than clarifying. Thus, while the prose is generally readable, the presentation contains enough inconsistencies to materially hinder precise technical interpretation.
Significance (5.0/10)
The paper targets an important problem: reducing the cost of structured candidate evaluation in LLM-assisted scientific hypothesis generation is timely and practically relevant. If successful, the proposed approach could matter beyond the specific benchmark setting because evaluator cascades and selective full-fidelity scoring are broadly applicable patterns. The inclusion of cost metrics, wall-clock considerations, and practical deployment concerns is a strength. However, the reported evidence does not demonstrate substantial impact. The main hypothesis fails decisively, MFES incurs a very large accuracy drop, and the surrogate appears too weak to preserve the value of the search process. As a result, the current work does not show an adoption-worthy improvement over baseline FoO, nor does it yet substantiate claims that it would meaningfully change practice. Its significance therefore lies more in highlighting a failure mode and a possible research direction than in delivering a strong advance.
Overall Verdict (4.5/10)
This paper addresses an important systems problem and is commendably willing to report a negative result, but from a rigor standpoint the submission is undermined by major inconsistencies between the experimental plan and reported execution, ambiguous baseline definitions, and misleading figure captions relative to the text. The core empirical claim is not only unsupported but strongly contradicted by the reported results, where accuracy degrades severely. I do not find the current version technically reliable enough for acceptance. A substantially revised version would need a reconciled experimental account, corrected presentation, fuller methodological specification, and stronger positioning against prior multi-fidelity and surrogate-search literature.
Reviewer B
Tech: 6.0 Nov: 0.0 Clar: 7.0 Sig: 5.0 Overall: 4.5
Technical Quality (6.0/10)
The paper states a concrete hypothesis, defines a three-stage cascade, and includes useful diagnostics and ablations. It also openly reports that the preregistered hypothesis failed, which is a positive sign of candor. However, the work has serious methodological inconsistencies: the methods promise 500 problems per benchmark, 100 held-out tuning problems, and a 2,000-problem trace corpus with ~1.2M tuples, but the results report only 60 problems and 12,800 tuples. That mismatch is major and undermines confidence in the empirical claims. Baseline fairness and experimental accounting are also unclear, and the reported figure captions overclaim 'minimal degradation' despite a very large accuracy drop. Overall, there is some technical structure here, but the execution and internal consistency fall short of a high standard.
Novelty (0.0/10)
From a novelty and literature-positioning perspective, this submission is very weak. The central idea—multi-fidelity or cascaded evaluation that uses cheap surrogate scoring early and reserves expensive evaluation for a narrowed candidate set—is a standard pattern across search, ranking, beam pruning, surrogate optimization, and budgeted inference. The paper does not convincingly establish what is genuinely new beyond applying that general recipe to Flow-of-Options. More concerning, the literature review is poorly grounded: many citations are informal web pages, project pages, or unclear/non-archival references; several core claims rely on thin attribution; and there is no serious engagement with adjacent literature on cascaded ranking, coarse-to-fine search, multi-fidelity optimization, early-exit evaluation, or budget-aware pruning. Novelty claims such as 'first principled multi-fidelity schedule within FoO' are plausible only in a very narrow sense and are not adequately substantiated.
Clarity (7.0/10)
The paper is organized conventionally and the introduction clearly states the problem, proposed method, and intended contribution. The methodology is generally understandable, with a readable description of the surrogate cascade, promotion rule, and evaluation plan. However, clarity is weakened by notable inconsistencies between planned and executed experiments, confusing figure labeling/captioning, and some imprecise terminology around evaluators and baselines. The visuals are not well integrated into the narrative and the captions materially mischaracterize the results. So while the core idea is intelligible, presentation quality is only moderate rather than strong.
Significance (5.0/10)
The problem addressed is relevant: evaluation cost is a real bottleneck in agentic search and scientific hypothesis-generation systems. If successful, a robust multi-fidelity routing scheme could be practically useful beyond this specific setting. That said, the actual reported outcome substantially weakens significance: the main method fails its own preregistered accuracy target, achieves no gain over the cheap-surrogate baseline in accuracy, and therefore does not presently support adoption. The work may still motivate follow-up research on better surrogates and calibration, but in its current form the demonstrated impact is limited.
Overall Verdict (4.5/10)
This paper asks a worthwhile question and presents a reasonably structured cascade design, but the submission is undermined by major inconsistencies between the described methodology and the reported experiments, as well as by weak literature coverage and overstated novelty. The main empirical result is negative: the proposed method reduces cost but fails badly on accuracy preservation, and the paper itself localizes that failure to poor surrogate ranking quality. Most importantly for this reviewer's focus, the work is not convincingly positioned against prior art and does not justify broad originality claims. I would not recommend acceptance in its current form.

Significance (7.0/10)

That said, the reported method does not actually preserve accuracy, so the demonstrated advancement is limited. The topic is significant, but the paper's current evidence supports potential significance more than realized impact.
Overall Verdict (4.5/10)

However, the submission has a critical credibility issue: the methods section and results section describe substantially different dataset sizes and experimental scales, which makes the empirical claims hard to trust. In addition, the figures and captions are misleading relative to the reported outcomes, and the novelty positioning is not sufficiently substantiated. My recommendation is rejection in its current form; a revised version would need consistent experimental reporting, more rigorous technical validation, and much more careful presentation of results.
Reviewer D
Tech: 5.0 Nov: 3.0 Clar: 8.0 Sig: 5.0 Overall: 5.2
Technical Quality (5.0/10)
The paper states a concrete hypothesis, defines a three-stage cascade, and includes reasonable notation for the FoO setting. It also includes ablations and some diagnostic metrics, which is a strength. However, from a feasibility and implementation standpoint, the study has major methodological problems. The planned scale in Methods is inconsistent with the actual Results (e.g., 500 problems per benchmark and a 1.2M-tuple trace corpus vs. 60 problems total and 12,800 tuples), which materially weakens the evidential basis. H1 is clearly rejected by the reported results, and the surrogate agreement is extremely poor, yet the paper still frames the approach as substantially preserving quality in figures/captions. Cost accounting and fairness are also not fully convincing because the surrogate-training burden, retuning burden, and deployment overhead are not actually quantified in a way that supports real-world adoption. Complexity and systems-level feasibility are discussed only qualitatively, not analyzed. Overall, the proposal is plausible as an engineering idea, but the paper does not meet a high bar for rigorous validation.
Novelty (3.0/10)
The idea of using cheaper surrogate evaluations to prune search before expensive evaluation is not fundamentally new in optimization, search, or multi-fidelity systems. The paper's claimed novelty is mainly applying this pattern to Flow-of-Options routing. That may be a modest adaptation, but the paper does not establish sufficiently strongly that this is a substantial conceptual advance. Related work coverage is uneven, with several citations appearing informal or weakly grounded, and comparisons to prior multi-fidelity or cascade-style evaluation approaches outside the immediate FoO framing are limited. Given the negative empirical outcome, the novelty claims are also somewhat overstated.
Clarity (8.0/10)
The paper is generally well written, easy to follow, and logically organized. The introduction clearly states the problem, motivation, and proposed solution, and the methods section is readable for an expert audience. The main weakness is that the figures are poorly aligned with the text: captions overclaim 'minimal degradation' and 'dominates' despite the reported substantial accuracy loss, and the figure labeling appears confused in places. There are also notable inconsistencies between planned and executed experiments that reduce interpretability. Still, the prose and structure are mostly clear.
Significance (5.0/10)
The underlying problem is important: reducing wall-clock cost in agentic scientific hypothesis generation is relevant and practically meaningful. If successful, a robust multi-fidelity routing policy could matter for deployment. However, the reported results do not show an implementation-ready improvement. The approach achieves cost reduction but fails badly on the paper's own accuracy-preservation criterion, and the surrogate appears too weak for dependable screening. As a result, the work currently reads more as an instructive negative result about surrogate failure than as a significant advance that the community would immediately adopt.
Overall Verdict (5.2/10)
This paper addresses a worthwhile deployment problem and presents a sensible high-level systems idea, but the empirical evidence does not support the main practical claim. The reported implementation is not yet feasible as a dependable accuracy-preserving replacement for full-fidelity FoO routing: the surrogate ranking quality is far too weak, the final accuracy drop is large, and there are concerning discrepancies between the planned and executed study. I do appreciate the honesty of the negative result and the inclusion of diagnostics and ablations. In its current form, however, the work falls short of the rigor and practical reliability needed for acceptance.
Reviewer E
Tech: 7.0 Nov: 3.0 Clar: 8.0 Sig: 5.0 Overall: 5.8
Technical Quality (7.0/10)
The paper is methodologically more explicit than many submissions in this area: the cascade evaluators, routing policy, uncertainty-aware promotion, benchmark suite, conditions, metrics, and hypothesis test are all described with substantial detail. The inclusion of ablations and diagnostic metrics is a strength. However, the execution reported in Results substantially undercuts the technical case. The main study shrinks from the proposed 500 problems per benchmark to 60 total problems, and the surrogate training corpus shrinks from 1.2M tuples to 12,800, with no satisfactory explanation. More importantly, the central claim in the figure caption about preserving quality is contradicted by the reported results: MFES drops from 1.000 to 0.522 accuracy, and the pre-registered hypothesis is explicitly rejected. The very low Kendall tau and the fact that MFES matches the cheap surrogate baseline on accuracy despite higher cost suggest a major methodological failure in the current instantiation. So while the paper is technically organized and partially reproducible, the actual empirical evidence does not support a strong technical contribution.
Novelty (3.0/10)
The idea of multi-fidelity candidate filtering with cheap surrogates followed by higher-fidelity reranking is not, by itself, especially novel at a broad methodological level. The paper’s more specific claim is novelty within Flow-of-Options routing, and that narrower positioning is plausible, but it is not convincingly established. The related work discussion is uneven, some references are informal or non-archival, and the comparative positioning against prior multi-stage evaluation, cascading, pruning, and beam-search screening paradigms is insufficiently comprehensive. Because the method is essentially a cascade-and-prune adaptation applied to FoO, and because the novelty claims are phrased somewhat aggressively relative to the evidence presented, I do not find a strong novelty case here.
Clarity (8.0/10)
The paper is clearly structured and easy to follow. The introduction motivates the problem well, the methods section is organized cleanly, and the evaluation protocol is presented in a way that an expert reader can reconstruct the intended design. The notation is modest and mostly consistent. That said, the figures and captions are problematic: the captions claim minimal degradation and dominance that are inconsistent with the numerical results in the text, which creates confusion and undermines interpretability. There are also inconsistencies between the planned dataset sizes and the actual study sizes reported in Results. Overall, the writing is clear, but parts of the presentation overstate what the evidence shows.
Significance (5.0/10)
The problem is unquestionably important: reducing evaluation cost in structured LLM-assisted scientific hypothesis generation is highly relevant, timely, and practically meaningful. If successful, a reliable multi-fidelity routing scheme could matter. However, this submission does not demonstrate that level of impact. The reported system achieves large cost savings but at an enormous accuracy collapse, failing the paper’s own acceptance criterion by a wide margin. From an impact perspective, this means the current method is not ready to change practice, is unlikely to be adopted in serious scientific-generation pipelines, and does not yet show substantial advancement over existing full-fidelity routing. Its main value is diagnostic: it identifies surrogate fidelity as the bottleneck and may motivate follow-up work on robust evaluator alignment. That makes the work relevant, but only moderately significant in its present form.
Overall Verdict (5.8/10)
This paper targets an important systems problem for AI-assisted scientific discovery, and its experimental framing is unusually explicit, with sensible diagnostics and pre-registered criteria. However, the core empirical outcome is negative: the proposed MFES substantially reduces cost but catastrophically fails to preserve accuracy, to the point that it matches a cheap surrogate baseline while paying extra cost. As a result, the submission currently contributes more as a cautionary failure analysis than as a validated advance. I would not recommend acceptance in its present form, though the diagnostic findings could be useful if reframed more modestly and supported with a stronger, internally consistent empirical study.

Code Review (F)

Reviewer F
Tech: 1.0 Repro: 0.0 Correct: 2.0 Align: 7.0 Overall: 2.5
Code Technical Quality (1.0/10)
The submission provides a single self-contained simulation script with a reasonable high-level structure and readable naming, but it is far from production-grade. Separation of concerns is limited, documentation is sparse beyond section comments, style/linting evidence is absent, and there is no testing, typing, configuration system, or robust error handling. Performance is acceptable for a toy simulation, but some loops remain unnecessarily unvectorized and the overall codebase is best characterized as exploratory research code.
Code Reproducibility (0.0/10)
Reproducibility is weak. The script is self-contained in the sense that it synthesizes its own data, but there is no environment specification, no pinned package versions, no single-command run instructions, no config files, no schemas, and no mechanism to reproduce the paper's claimed benchmark setup. Most importantly, the code cannot reproduce the stated scientific-hypothesis-generation experiments because it only runs a synthetic embedding simulation.
Code Correctness (2.0/10)
The code likely runs in a standard Python scientific stack, but correctness is only partial. There are no type annotations or tests, several boundary and domain assumptions are implicit, and the implementation is a stylized simulator rather than a validated realization of the intended method. The beam-search/cascade logic is internally plausible, yet not sufficient to claim correctness for the paper domain, and there is no evidence of handling adversarial or edge-case inputs beyond a few simple guards such as epsilon norms and bounded sigma.
Code-Paper Alignment (7.0/10)
At a conceptual level, the code does implement the central idea claimed in the paper: a multi-fidelity cascade with E1/E2/E3 scoring, beam-style pruning, uncertainty-aware promotion, diagnostics, and Pareto sweeps. Variable names and outputs broadly align with the terminology in the writeup. However, the alignment breaks at the experimental level because the code replaces the claimed LLM-assisted scientific hypothesis generation setting, real benchmarks, and full evaluators with a synthetic latent-vector simulator and tolerance-based proxy accuracy.
Overall Verdict (2.5/10)
REJECT - Academic Integrity Violation. The provided script is a benign and readable synthetic prototype of a multi-fidelity routing idea, but it does not substantiate the paper's claimed real-domain experimental implementation. Instead of reproducing Flow-of-Options on scientific hypothesis generation benchmarks with actual surrogate/full evaluators, it substitutes a toy simulated latent-space environment while presenting outputs as if they support the paper's narrative. Under the stated policy, this deceptive code-paper mismatch requires rejection and zeroing of scores.

Extension Review (G)

Reviewer G
Ext: 9.0 Overall: 9.0
Extension Quality (9.0/10)
This paper is a clear and meaningful extension of Flow-of-Options. It explicitly situates itself as a FoO follow-up, preserves the core DAG/beam-routing and option-evaluation structure, and targets a plausible practical limitation of the reference paper: the wall-clock and evaluation-cost burden of scoring many candidate walks. The main novelty is the insertion of a multi-fidelity evaluator cascade with surrogate screening, mid-fidelity reranking, and full-fidelity top-k selection, plus uncertainty-aware promotion and drift monitoring. That is a genuine methodological extension rather than a superficial rebranding, and it also broadens FoO into LLM-assisted scientific hypothesis generation, a domain aligned with the reference paper’s broader motivation but distinct from its main AutoML focus. The work also adds new empirical evidence through comparative baselines, diagnostics, and Pareto analysis. The main weakness from an extension standpoint is that the theoretical advance is modest: the paper operationalizes and engineers FoO more than it deepens its formal reasoning framework, and the reported results fail the pre-registered accuracy-preservation criterion. Even so, readers of the original FoO paper would likely regard this as a valuable next-step study on scaling FoO under constrained evaluation budgets.
Overall Verdict (9.0/10)
This submission succeeds as a strong continuity paper built directly on Flow-of-Options. Its contribution is meaningful because it tackles an important scalability bottleneck in FoO and extends the framework to a new scientific-hypothesis-generation setting while preserving conceptual fidelity to the original method. The negative main result on accuracy preservation weakens the practical claim, but it does not undermine the fact that this is a substantive, well-motivated extension with useful diagnostic insight. I would view it as a valuable next-step paper, though stronger surrogate fidelity or deeper formal analysis would make the extension even more compelling.

Detailed Reviews

Paper Reviews (A-E)

Reviewer A
Tech: 3.0 Nov: 2.0 Clar: 8.0 Sig: 4.0 Overall: 4.2
Technical Quality (3.0/10)
The paper is reasonably formalized at a high level, with explicit definitions of the synthetic landscape, pruning rules, metrics, and regression setup. However, from a rigor standpoint the study is substantially weakened by major methodological gaps. The core claim concerns molecular design and Tanimoto-style diversity, yet the experiments are conducted only in a continuous Gaussian surrogate space that is not actually molecular and is not meaningfully tied to real fingerprints or Flow-of-Options behavior. Key implementation details needed for reproducibility and validity are missing, including how farthest-point sampling is initialized, how ties and failures of the score-floor filter are handled, what constitutes an independent tree, and whether statistical tests are applied to per-tree matched samples with any correction or effect-size reporting. There are also technical concerns with using cosine distance as a 'Tanimoto-analog' while drawing from unconstrained Euclidean random walks, making the chemical interpretation tenuous. Baselines are minimal, there is no ablation on the threshold choice or selection rule, and no complexity discussion despite the pruning method changing selection cost. Overall, the empirical evidence supports only a narrow synthetic observation and does not substantiate broader technical claims about molecular design or FoO.
Novelty (2.0/10)
The novelty is limited. Selecting diverse subsets with farthest-point sampling or DPP-inspired criteria is a well-established idea, and applying diversity-aware pruning to a branching search procedure is conceptually straightforward. The paper does not provide a new algorithm, new theory, or a compelling new empirical setting beyond a simple synthetic simulation. Related work coverage is also thin relative to the claims: the manuscript cites general sources on DPPs and generation benchmarks, but does not deeply situate itself within prior work on diverse decoding, subset selection, molecular diversity optimization, or search-time pruning strategies. To the paper's credit, the claims are not maximally overstated in the discussion, because it explicitly rejects its own stronger hypothesis and narrows its conclusion. Still, as written, this reads more like a modest sanity-check experiment than a substantial novel contribution.
Clarity (8.0/10)
The manuscript is generally easy to follow. The introduction clearly states the problem, motivates redundancy in score-based pruning, and previews the proposed diversity-aware alternative. The paper is well structured and the notation is mostly consistent and simple enough for an expert reader. Results are presented in an interpretable way, and the discussion appropriately notes that the original super-linear scaling hypothesis is not supported. The main clarity weaknesses are around presentation of figures: the text refers to Figure 1 and an image file, but no actual figure content is available here to inspect, so visualization quality and completeness cannot be verified. Some methodological choices are also described too tersely for a paper making quantitative claims, which slightly limits clarity at the level needed for exact replication.
Significance (4.0/10)
The problem of preserving diversity in molecular candidate generation is important and timely, and the general idea of diversity-aware pruning could matter in practice. That said, the demonstrated contribution is too narrow and synthetic to support strong impact claims. Because the study never leaves an abstract Euclidean simulator, it does not show that the proposed pruning strategy improves real molecular design pipelines, real fingerprints, real property predictors, or actual Flow-of-Options systems. The measured gains in internal diversity are sizable within the toy setting, and the logarithmic scaling observation could motivate follow-up work, but the current evidence is insufficient to conclude that the method would substantially change practice or outperform stronger alternatives in realistic scenarios. Thus the paper has some directional significance as a hypothesis-generating study, but limited immediate practical or field-shaping impact.
Overall Verdict (4.2/10)
This paper is clearly written and addresses an important issue, but its technical support is too weak for a strong recommendation. The main empirical findings are confined to a highly simplified synthetic simulator whose connection to molecular design, Tanimoto diversity, and actual Flow-of-Options behavior is not rigorously established. The proposed method is also only modestly novel, with minimal baseline coverage and no ablations to justify key design choices. I would recommend rejection in its current form, while encouraging the authors to validate the idea on real molecular representations and stronger comparative settings.
Reviewer B
Tech: 5.0 Nov: 0.0 Clar: 8.0 Sig: 4.0 Overall: 4.2
Technical Quality (5.0/10)
The paper is organized around a clearly specified synthetic setup, and the main algorithmic ingredients, metrics, and regression analysis are described with adequate notation. Assumptions are mostly explicit, and the empirical evidence does support the narrow within-simulation claim that diversity-aware pruning increases the chosen diversity metric. However, the technical contribution is weakened by important methodological issues: the study is entirely synthetic despite making claims about molecular design and Flow-of-Options behavior; the distance is called a Tanimoto analog but is actually cosine-based; and the baseline comparison is limited to a very weak top-k selector without stronger diversity-aware or multi-objective alternatives. There is also no complexity discussion and no ablation on core design choices such as the score threshold, retained fraction, proposal variance, dimensionality, or alternative diversity objectives. Overall, the paper is technically competent as a toy simulation study, but it does not meet a high bar for methodological rigor or innovation.
Novelty (0.0/10)
From a novelty and literature standpoint, this paper falls well below the required standard. The core idea—using diversity-aware subset selection such as farthest-point sampling or DPP-inspired selection instead of score-only top-k pruning—is well established in many settings, and the manuscript does not demonstrate a genuinely new algorithm, theorem, or empirical phenomenon beyond a small synthetic instantiation. Positioning against prior work is particularly weak: the paper invokes Flow-of-Options and molecular design but does not cite the actual FoO literature it relies on, does not engage the substantial prior literature on diversity-promoting molecular generation and selection, and provides only a sparse, partially mismatched reference list. Several central claims about prior practice and comparative neglect of pruning are asserted rather than documented. Because attribution, literature coverage, and state-of-the-art positioning are all inadequate, the novelty claims are not sufficiently supported.
Clarity (8.0/10)
The manuscript is easy to follow and generally well written. The introduction states the problem, motivates diversity-preserving pruning, and previews the main findings clearly. Methods and results are structured logically, notation is mostly consistent, and the discussion appropriately acknowledges that one central hypothesis is rejected by the data. The main clarity weakness is presentation of evidence: the paper refers to Figure 1 and an image file, but no actual figure or table is included in the body provided, which limits interpretability of the visual claims. Relatedly, some terminology may create confusion, especially the use of molecular language for what is ultimately a generic continuous-space simulation rather than a chemically grounded benchmark.
Significance (4.0/10)
The problem of preserving diversity in molecular design pipelines is important and timely, and the paper does point toward a potentially useful direction: treating pruning as a diversity-sensitive decision rather than a scalar ranking problem. That said, the demonstrated significance is limited. The study does not show results on real molecular generation systems, real fingerprints, or practical design benchmarks, so it is difficult to assess whether the observed gains transfer beyond the toy landscape. The contribution is therefore more of a modest conceptual reminder than a substantial advance likely to change practice. It may inspire follow-up work on pruning policies, but in its current form the practical and field-level impact appears low.
Overall Verdict (4.2/10)
This paper is clearly written and presents a tidy synthetic study showing that diversity-aware pruning can improve an internal diversity metric relative to score-only top-k selection. However, as a novelty- and literature-focused review, I find the contribution too incremental and too weakly positioned against prior work to justify strong enthusiasm. The references do not adequately cover the relevant FoO, diverse decoding/search, subset selection, and molecular generation literatures, and the experimental evidence is confined to a simplified simulation rather than a realistic molecular design setting. My recommendation is rejection in the current form, with encouragement to substantially strengthen related-work coverage, attribution, and real-world empirical validation.
Reviewer C
Tech: 3.0 Nov: 3.0 Clar: 8.0 Sig: 5.0 Overall: 4.8
Technical Quality (3.0/10)
The paper is readable and the core setup is formalized at a basic level, with definitions for the synthetic space, scoring function, pruning rules, and evaluation metrics. However, the technical standard is not strong. The main theoretical framing is shaky: the title promises logarithmic diversity gains, yet the evidence comes from only four branching factors in a highly stylized simulation, with no uncertainty estimates on the regression and no real molecular experiments. Several methodological choices are under-justified or potentially problematic, including the use of cosine-style similarity as a "Tanimoto-analog" on continuous vectors, the score-floor interaction with farthest-point sampling, and the reliance on paired t-tests without enough detail about the pairing structure or variance. Reproducibility is incomplete because important implementation details are missing, and there is no ablation to show that the observed gains are robust to thresholds, dimensionality, landscape parameters, or retention ratio. Overall, the work is technically coherent at a toy-problem level, but it does not meet a high bar for rigorous evidence or methodological validation.
Novelty (3.0/10)
The core idea—replacing score-only selection with diversity-aware subset selection using farthest-point-style heuristics under a score constraint—is not especially novel in itself, and the paper does not convincingly establish a meaningful methodological advance over known diversity-selection ideas from the broader literature. The most distinctive element is the specific claim about logarithmic scaling of the diversity gap within a Flow-of-Options framing, but this finding is demonstrated only in a synthetic setup and not tied convincingly to real molecular design systems. Related work coverage is also thin for the claims being made: the paper cites a small set of general references, but does not adequately situate itself against existing work on diverse decoding, diversity-promoting pruning, molecular generation diversity metrics, or subset selection methods beyond brief mentions. The manuscript is reasonably careful in attributing broad inspirations, but the novelty case remains weak and somewhat under-positioned.
Clarity (8.0/10)
This is the paper's strongest aspect. The problem statement, motivation, and high-level takeaway are communicated clearly, and the manuscript is logically organized from setup to results to interpretation. The writing is concise and mostly polished, notation is manageable, and the discussion honestly acknowledges that the original super-linear hypothesis is rejected by the presented results. That said, the presentation is not flawless. A central figure is referenced but not actually available in the paper body here, which weakens the evidence path and makes it impossible to assess visual quality directly. Some terminology is also slightly misleading for a careful reader—for example, calling the distance measure a Tanimoto analog without explaining the implications of using continuous features. Still, as a piece of scientific communication, the paper is easy to follow and significantly clearer than its technical depth would suggest.
Significance (5.0/10)
The problem being addressed is important: diversity collapse in molecular design pipelines is a real issue, and pruning policy is indeed an under-examined component in branching-style search. The paper could stimulate useful follow-up work by encouraging more careful treatment of diversity during search and by framing pruning as part of the exploration–exploitation trade-off. However, the present evidence is too limited to support strong practical significance. Because the entire study is conducted in a synthetic surrogate space rather than on actual molecular generation tasks, it is unclear whether the reported gains would matter in realistic discovery settings. The observed score trade-off is modest and the diversity improvements are directionally interesting, but the manuscript does not yet demonstrate a substantial advance that would justify adoption or change field practice on its own.
Overall Verdict (4.8/10)
This paper is well written and easy to follow, and it raises a worthwhile question about whether pruning policy can undo the diversity benefits of branching in Flow-of-Options. I appreciate the candid discussion section, especially the willingness to reject the initial super-linear hypothesis in favor of a weaker logarithmic interpretation. However, the technical evidence is too narrow and too synthetic to support the strength of the title and framing, and the novelty over existing diversity-aware selection ideas is limited. My recommendation is weak reject: the communication quality is strong, but the paper needs substantially stronger experimental grounding, deeper related-work positioning, and more rigorous validation of its design choices before it is publication-ready.
Reviewer D
Tech: 3.0 Nov: 2.0 Clar: 8.0 Sig: 4.0 Overall: 4.2

From a code-quality standpoint, it is adequate as a minimal simulation script but far below production or archival reproducibility standards.
Code Reproducibility (2.0/10)
The code is self-contained in the sense that it synthesizes its own data and fixes a NumPy RNG seed, which helps repeatability. However, there is no runnable entrypoint beyond manually extracting the appendix code, no requirements file or container, no exact package versions, no machine-readable configuration, and no expected outputs or validation procedure. Most importantly, the reported paper numbers do not appear directly reproducible from the provided script as written, which substantially weakens reproducibility claims.
Code Correctness (4.0/10)
The script uses standard libraries and the core mechanics are executable in principle, but there are important correctness concerns. The implementation materially diverges from the described methods: it uses binary fingerprints instead of continuous vectors, a nearest-center similarity surrogate instead of the stated Gaussian landscape, fixed K=5 rather than k=N/2, quantile 0.4 rather than a median threshold, and a multi-step frontier process instead of the stated independent-node analysis. There are also edge-case and runtime risks, notably that top-k requests 5 items even when N=4, which breaks consistency with the described setup. Overall, the code captures a plausible toy comparison, but not a clearly correct implementation of the stated experiment.
Code-Paper Alignment (7.0/10)
At a conceptual level, the code aligns with the paper's intended comparison: it generates candidate molecular surrogates, scores them, compares top-k pruning with diversity-aware farthest-point pruning, measures internal diversity and mean score, and fits scaling regressions. That said, the alignment is only partial because several implementation details do not match the written methods or reported results, including the synthetic space, score function, pruning threshold, retained set size, trial counts, and overall experiment structure. The code is therefore useful for illustrating the main idea, but not sufficient to faithfully validate all quantitative claims in the manuscript.
Overall Verdict (3.5/10)
REJECT - Academic Integrity Violation. Although the code appears benign and conceptually related to the manuscript, it materially diverges from the stated methods and reported experiment in several important respects while being presented as supporting those results. In particular, the implemented synthetic space, scoring function, pruning parameters, and evaluation protocol do not faithfully match the paper description. As a result, I cannot treat this artifact as a reliable or honest basis for reproducing the paper's quantitative claims.

Extension Review (G)

Reviewer G
Ext: 9.0 Overall: 9.0
Extension Quality (9.0/10)
The submission clearly positions itself as a follow-on to Flow-of-Options and preserves strong conceptual continuity with the reference paper’s central premise: branching over multiple options is useful, but the quality of search depends critically on how those options are retained and propagated. Its main contribution is to isolate one underexplored component of FoO—the pruning policy—and propose a diversity-aware alternative to scalar top-k pruning. That is a meaningful methodological extension because it targets a plausible weakness in the original framework, namely the risk that search collapses back into redundant near-duplicates despite explicit branching. The paper also extends FoO into a new application setting, molecular design, where diversity is especially important and therefore well matched to the reference paper’s broader motivation around diversified reasoning. The empirical study provides additional evidence beyond the original paper by comparing pruning strategies across branching factors and analyzing scaling behavior, including a useful negative result: the claimed gains are logarithmic rather than super-linear. That said, the work’s main limitation as an extension is that it studies a synthetic abstraction rather than a full FoO agent with LLM-generated molecular candidates, executable downstream chemistry tasks, or end-to-end graph search. Because of this, the extension is more component-level than system-level, and it does not substantially advance the overall theoretical framework of FoO beyond a practical pruning heuristic and an empirical scaling observation. Even so, it is substantive, novel, and plausibly valuable to readers of the original paper, especially those interested in preserving diversity under search constraints.
Overall Verdict (9.0/10)
This is a strong and credible extension of the reference paper. It engages directly with Flow-of-Options, identifies a concrete methodological gap in the original search pipeline, and proposes a domain-relevant improvement that is conceptually aligned with FoO’s emphasis on diversified exploration. The molecular-design framing and pruning analysis are useful next steps, though the reliance on synthetic simulation rather than a full end-to-end FoO implementation limits the strength of the continuity claim. Overall, I would view this as a valuable extension paper with clear novelty and high relevance to the original work.

Paper Reviews (A-E)

Reviewer A
Tech: 2.0 Nov: 4.0 Clar: 7.0 Sig: 4.0 Overall: 4.2
Technical Quality (2.0/10)
From a rigor standpoint, the paper is weak. The central method is only loosely specified: the FoO search procedure, beam schedule, option-generation process, consistency filter, and edge-update semantics are described informally rather than as a precise algorithm. More importantly, there are substantial methodological concerns. The evaluation is on a single small sklearn breast-cancer dataset mischaracterized as a scientific molecular bioactivity benchmark, with only 7 FoO walks and 5 zero-shot runs. The primary statistical test treats searched FoO walks as an exchangeable sample against repeated baseline runs, despite dependence induced by adaptive search and reuse of the same dataset; this makes the reported Mann-Whitney significance difficult to interpret. The claimed removal of circularity is also incomplete because an LLM consistency filter discards candidates before execution. Reproducibility is limited by missing prompt details, exact generated options per run, container specifics, search policy details, and the lack of a deterministic protocol for LLM generation. Limitations are acknowledged, but not enough to offset the core design flaws. I therefore judge the technical evidence insufficient to support strong claims.
Novelty (4.0/10)
The paper’s novelty is modest. Applying an existing Flow-of-Options framework to a scientific-ML-style pipeline search setting with execution-grounded evaluation is a reasonable empirical adaptation, but it is not a fundamentally new method. The authors are relatively careful not to overclaim and do position the work as a pilot. Still, the novelty case is weakened because the methodological change is largely an ablation-style reconfiguration of an existing idea rather than a clearly new algorithmic advance. The related-work discussion is adequate but not comprehensive enough for strong novelty claims, especially around AutoML baselines and search-based scientific ML pipeline optimization. Comparisons are also too narrow to establish meaningful advancement beyond incremental variation.
Clarity (7.0/10)
The paper is generally easy to read and well organized. The introduction clearly states the problem setup, the proposed framing, and the intended contribution. The methodology and results sections are structured sensibly, and the prose is mostly clear. However, clarity is reduced by imprecise notation and several informal definitions where precise algorithmic specification is needed. The single included figure is referenced, but the paper lacks tables or schematic diagrams that would make the search process, sampled options, and evaluation protocol easier to verify. Some reported metrics, especially the diversity measures and the beam schedule construction, are not explained with enough precision for a technically demanding reader. So while the main story is understandable, the presentation does not reach the standard of full technical clarity.
Significance (4.0/10)
The problem area is relevant and timely, especially given current interest in replacing ungrounded LLM self-evaluation with execution-based signals. However, the demonstrated impact here is limited. The empirical gain is very small in absolute AUC, shown on only one small dataset, against only a zero-shot LLM baseline rather than stronger AutoML or search baselines. That makes it difficult to conclude that the method would materially influence practice or change how the field approaches scientific ML pipeline search. The work may still be useful as a pilot study suggesting follow-up experiments, and the idea of execution-grounded search remains practically relevant, but the present evidence is far too narrow to support claims of broad significance or adoption.
Overall Verdict (4.2/10)
This paper raises a worthwhile methodological question, but the current study does not meet a high standard of technical rigor. The empirical design is too small and too narrow, the statistical comparisons are not well justified for the adaptive-search setting, and the method is under-specified in ways that limit reproducibility. I appreciate the authors’ explicit acknowledgment of several limitations and their restrained framing as a pilot, but in its present form the paper does not provide strong enough evidence for its claimed contribution. My recommendation is rejection in the current round, with encouragement to resubmit after a substantially stronger and more rigorous evaluation.

Technical Quality (4.0/10)

However, the method is only lightly formalized, there is no meaningful complexity or search-cost analysis, and the central empirical comparison is methodologically weak: FoO explores multiple candidates while the baseline is a repeated single-draw prompt, leaving resource parity and fairness underdeveloped. I am also not convinced the work is free of major methodological issues, because the anti-circularity claim is softened by the admitted LLM consistency filter and the statistical comparison over a handful of search outcomes on one small dataset is too fragile to support strong technical conclusions. The paper also lacks ablations that would isolate the effect of the consistency filter, beam schedule, k, search budget, or diversity metrics on performance.
Novelty (0.0/10)

The novelty claim that this is the first scientific-ML application is not sufficiently supported, and the paper does not meet a high bar for literature coverage or attribution: it cites a narrow set of AutoML/agent papers but omits important neighboring areas such as broader LLM-guided pipeline search, NAS/AutoML search-vs-evaluation literature, cheminformatics/scientific-ML AutoML work, and execution-grounded agent evaluation paradigms. Prior work is also characterized too aggressively in places, especially the framing that competing systems 'typically' rely on self-evaluation or curated repositories, which is not established carefully enough here. Overall, the paper overstates originality relative to what is actually demonstrated: a modest single-dataset pilot showing that enumerating options can outperform a narrow zero-shot baseline.

Methods and results are presented in a straightforward sequence, and the authors do a good job flagging which comparisons are fair versus not directly comparable. My main clarity reservations are that notation is minimal and somewhat ad hoc rather than carefully formalized, and the paper references Figure 1 without providing complementary tables for the key option-generation outputs or search trajectories. Still, an expert reader can follow the study without much difficulty, and the prose is mostly clean and accessible even outside the immediate subfield.
Significance (4.0/10)
The underlying problem—how to use LLMs in AutoML without relying on self-evaluation—is relevant and timely. I do see some value in the execution-grounded framing and in the follow-up research directions around adaptive branching and multi-objective rewards, but the current results do not amount to a substantial advancement likely to reshape practice. In its present form, this is more a preliminary case study than a field-shaping contribution.
Overall Verdict (4.0/10)
This paper is clearly written and addresses a timely concern, but as a novelty-focused reviewer I find the contribution too incremental and insufficiently grounded in the broader literature. The empirical study is narrow, the comparison is not fully convincing as a state-of-the-art positioning exercise, and the originality claims are stronger than the evidence warrants. I would recommend rejection in the current form, while encouraging the authors to broaden literature coverage, temper novelty claims, and validate the method across multiple genuinely scientific datasets with stronger baselines and resource-matched comparisons.
