Detailed Reviews

Paper Reviews (A-E)

Reviewer A
Tech: 4.0 Nov: 1.0 Clar: 8.0 Sig: 4.0 Overall: 4.2
Technical Quality (4.0/10)
The paper is reproducible at the script level and clearly states the synthetic setup, hyperparameters, metric, and the 2x2 ablation. It also explicitly states scope limitations. However, the technical core is weak by rigorous standards. The main conceptual claim—that FoO walk sampling is best understood as sampling without replacement on a prefix tree—is asserted rather than formalized, and no proof or careful derivation establishes equivalence or conditions under which the proposed fallback preserves any desired distributional property. There are also internal inconsistencies: the introduction and methods frame the fix as rejection plus least-frequent-prefix fallback on synthetic walks, while the discussion suddenly refers to replacing a random tie-breaker with hash-salted ordering and duplicate-child collisions, which appears to describe a different mechanism. The evaluation is minimal, uses only one synthetic setting, lacks uncertainty analysis, and does not fairly compare against stronger without-replacement samplers despite making broad conceptual claims. Overall, the paper demonstrates an empirical patch, not a technically validated characterization.
Novelty (1.0/10)
The novelty is very limited. The central idea is to reinterpret duplicate walk generation as a straightforward without-replacement sampling problem and apply rejection of previously seen samples with a heuristic fallback. That is a familiar and elementary strategy, and the paper itself frames the contribution as deflationary rather than algorithmically new. The related-work positioning is also thin: it cites FoO and automated research papers, but it does not engage with the broad literature on sampling without replacement, combinatorial generation, trie/prefix-tree sampling, diversity sampling, or exact unique sequence generation. As a result, the paper does not convincingly establish either methodological novelty or a new empirical finding beyond a small synthetic confirmation.
Clarity (8.0/10)
The paper is generally easy to read, well organized, and clear about its intended message: duplicate walks in a synthetic FoO-like setting can be reduced with rejection and fallback. The introduction motivates the issue, the methods section is concrete, and the results are presented plainly. Notation is light and mostly consistent. That said, clarity is undermined by the mismatch between the main method description and the discussion section's description of a hash-salted tie-breaker, which creates confusion about what was actually tested. In addition, there are no figures, and the sole table is simple but not especially informative beyond listing attempts. Still, an expert reader can follow the methodology and conclusions without much difficulty.
Significance (4.0/10)
Avoiding duplicate samples is a relevant practical concern, and the paper addresses a timely issue in agentic search and reasoning pipelines. The observed reduction in collision rate within the toy setup is numerically large. However, the broader significance is doubtful because the evidence is confined to a single small synthetic configuration with only 200 draws from a space of 1024 sequences. There is no demonstration on actual FoO runs, no evidence that the proposed mechanism preserves search quality or beneficial sampling properties, and no indication that the result changes system-level conclusions. The work may inspire follow-up validation, but in its current form it is too narrow and under-substantiated to support meaningful influence or adoption.
Overall Verdict (4.2/10)
This paper is clearly written and easy to reproduce in a narrow synthetic setting, but it falls short on technical rigor. The main conceptual claim is not formalized or proven, the empirical support is limited to one toy configuration, and the paper contains a notable inconsistency between the stated method and the discussion. From a correctness-oriented perspective, the work demonstrates a simple heuristic that removes duplicates in one script, not a validated characterization of FoO sampling or a robust advance. I would not recommend acceptance in its current form; the paper would need formalization, corrected internal consistency, and evaluation on real FoO settings to support its claims.
Reviewer B
Tech: 5.0 Nov: 1.0 Clar: 9.0 Sig: 4.0 Overall: 4.8
Technical Quality (5.0/10)
The paper is reproducible at the toy-experiment level and does include a useful ablation isolating rejection and fallback, which is the strongest technical aspect. Assumptions are mostly explicit, and the empirical claim that duplicate rates can be reduced in a small synthetic setting is supported by the reported setup. However, the technical presentation is weakly formalized, lacks any complexity analysis despite the central sampling claim, and relies on a very narrow single-configuration simulation with no meaningful baseline family beyond plain i.i.d. sampling. There is also a notable internal inconsistency: the Methods and Results frame the contribution as rejection plus least-frequent-prefix fallback on a synthetic prefix-tree walk problem, while the Discussion shifts to hash-salted ordering and duplicate-child collisions under tied scores, suggesting either a mismatch in artifact reporting or a serious presentation error.
Novelty (1.0/10)
From a novelty and literature perspective, this submission is extremely weak. The paper's own framing is that the main point is a deflationary reinterpretation of FoO walk sampling as ordinary sampling without replacement on a prefix tree, followed by a minimal rejection-based fix; that is much closer to a straightforward reformulation and implementation patch than to a new method or finding. The literature coverage is also insufficient for the claims being made: beyond citing FoO and several autoresearch papers that are largely orthogonal, the paper does not engage standard prior art on sampling without replacement, duplicate avoidance, trie/prefix-tree traversal, combinatorial generation, coverage/diversity sampling, or graph/path enumeration. As a result, the work is not convincingly positioned relative to prior art, the comparison set is not comprehensive, and the novelty claims are not adequately supported.
Clarity (9.0/10)
The manuscript is generally easy to read and well organized. The introduction clearly states the problem, the framing, and the claimed contribution, and the methodology is described in sufficient detail for an expert to understand exactly what was run. The table is simple and understandable, and the overall narrative from motivation to ablation to limitations is coherent. The main clarity issue is substantive rather than stylistic: the discussion section appears to describe a different intervention from the one evaluated earlier, which creates confusion about what the actual contribution is.
Significance (4.0/10)
The underlying issue of redundant sampling in structured generation is relevant, and reducing duplicate walks could matter in practice. That said, the demonstrated result is confined to a tiny synthetic setup and amounts to removing duplicates in a regime where the total path space is still manageable and the baseline duplicate rate is only 7.5%. The paper does not establish broader applicability, substantial advancement over existing approaches, or evidence that this would materially change practice in FoO or related systems. Its main value is as a modest prompt for follow-up work rather than as a significant contribution in its current form.
Overall Verdict (4.8/10)
This paper is readable and commendably explicit about scope, but as a research contribution it falls short on novelty, literature grounding, and technical credibility. The empirical result is limited to a single synthetic setting, the baseline/comparison landscape is underdeveloped, and the discussion contains a concerning mismatch with the described method. Most importantly, the manuscript does not meet a high bar for originality: it appears to be a narrow reformulation plus an obvious duplicate-avoidance patch, without adequate engagement with the broader literature on sampling without replacement and structured enumeration. I would not recommend acceptance in its current form.
Reviewer C
Tech: 3.0 Nov: 3.0 Clar: 7.0 Sig: 4.0 Overall: 4.2
Technical Quality (3.0/10)
The paper is reproducible at the script level and states its assumptions and scope clearly, but the technical case is weak. The central conceptual claim—that FoO walk sampling is simply sampling without replacement on a prefix tree—is asserted rather than formalized, and no theorem, proof sketch, or rigorous derivation establishes the equivalence. Empirical support is minimal: a single synthetic configuration, one headline metric, and an ablation that is described but not actually reported for all four cells. More seriously, the paper contains internal inconsistencies between the Methods and Discussion sections: the earlier sections describe rejection of previously seen walks plus least-frequent-prefix fallback, while the Discussion abruptly reframes the contribution as replacing random tie-breaking with hash-salted ordering and duplicate-child collisions under tied scores. That mismatch undermines confidence in the methodology and interpretation. Complexity and fairness considerations are also largely absent.
Novelty (3.0/10)
The paper is appropriately modest in tone and does not wildly overclaim, but the actual novelty appears limited. The core idea—avoiding duplicate samples by rejection and fallback in a finite combinatorial space—reads as an application of standard sampling-without-replacement intuition rather than a new method. The claimed contribution is mainly conceptual reframing plus a small empirical confirmation, which is incremental. Related-work positioning is only partial: FoO is cited, but the paper does not engage broader literature on sampling without replacement, diverse decoding/search, combinatorial generation, or coverage-aware exploration, making it hard to judge the distinctness of the contribution against existing techniques. Credit to FoO is generally fair, yet the novelty case is not strongly substantiated.
Clarity (7.0/10)
This is the paper's strongest dimension. The introduction clearly states the problem, the deflationary framing, and the intended contribution, and the paper is organized in a generally easy-to-follow sequence. The prose is concise and readable, with a good pedagogical attempt to connect the baseline issue to a structured sampling view. Methods and results are understandable to an expert reader, and the main metric is explicitly defined. However, clarity is weakened by the lack of formal notation for the prefix-tree construction, by missing full ablation results despite promising them, and by the major conceptual inconsistency in the Discussion, which appears to describe a different intervention from the one evaluated earlier. In addition, the checklist asks about figures and visualizations, but the paper provides only one small table and no figures, so the presentation is clear but not especially effective or complete.
Significance (4.0/10)
The underlying problem—wasted computation from duplicate trajectory sampling in structured search—is relevant, timely, and practically meaningful. If real in deployed FoO systems, a simple deduplication patch could be useful. However, the current paper does not demonstrate significance beyond a narrow toy setting. Evidence comes from one synthetic regime with modest scale, no real LLM rollouts, no downstream task outcomes, and no comparison against stronger alternatives. As presented, this is more a localized engineering observation than a substantial advance likely to reshape practice. The work does suggest sensible follow-up experiments, but the present results are not strong enough to justify broad adoption or major influence.
Overall Verdict (4.2/10)
This submission is clearly written and easy to follow, but the technical contribution is underdeveloped and the empirical evidence is too limited to support strong conclusions. Most concerning, the paper contains a serious inconsistency between the stated method and the Discussion, which raises doubts about the reliability of the presentation. I appreciate the restrained framing and the emphasis on a concrete failure mode, yet in its current form this reads as a small synthetic note rather than a rigorous research contribution. My recommendation is rejection unless the authors can formalize the claim, resolve the internal inconsistency, report the full ablation, and validate the effect on real FoO runs.

Code Review (F)

Reviewer F
Tech: 2.0 Repro: 3.0 Correct: 6.0 Align: 6.0 Overall: 4.2
Code Technical Quality (2.0/10)
The artifact is a very small, readable script with sensible variable names and a straightforward control flow, but it is not engineered beyond a minimal demonstration. There is no modularization, no functions, no docstrings, no tests, no typing, and no explicit style or linting evidence. The implementation is efficient enough for the stated toy setting, but it lacks abstractions and maintainability features expected even for a modest research artifact.
Code Reproducibility (3.0/10)
Reproducibility is helped by the fact that the script is self-contained, uses only the Python standard library, and fixes the random seed. However, the environment is not specified, the claimed paper results are broader than what the code actually computes, and there are no machine-readable configs, expected outputs, or validation utilities. This is enough for rerunning the single script, but not enough for rigorous end-to-end reproduction of the reported study.
Code Correctness (6.0/10)
For the narrow implemented task, the script is likely to run correctly and compute the printed collision rate without obvious runtime hazards. The use of tuples and a set for uniqueness checking is appropriate, and the logic for counting unique walks is sound for the provided constants. That said, correctness is only partial relative to the described method: edge cases are not handled, there are no typed interfaces because there are no functions, and the fallback behavior in code does not match the paper's stated least-frequent-prefix fallback.
Code-Paper Alignment (6.0/10)
The code is directionally aligned with the paper's synthetic walk-sampling setup: it uses the same K, D, N, seed, and collision-rate metric, and it does implement rejection against previously seen walks. But alignment breaks on key specifics. The paper emphasizes a 2x2 ablation and a least-frequent-prefix fallback, while the code only contains one sampler and its fallback is simply another random draw that may duplicate an existing walk. As a result, the artifact supports only a limited subset of the claims.
Overall Verdict (4.2/10)
REJECT - Academic Integrity Violation. The submitted artifact is a minimal, runnable script for a toy uniqueness-enforced sampler, but it does not implement several central code claims made in the accompanying text, most notably the least-frequent-prefix fallback and the advertised ablation grid. Because the paper description materially overstates what the code does, I marked academic integrity as violated under deceptive implementation/misdirection. Absent that mismatch, I would view the code as a modest but limited reproducibility artifact rather than a complete implementation.

Extension Review (G)

Reviewer G
Ext: 6.0 Overall: 6.0
Extension Quality (6.0/10)
The submission clearly positions itself relative to Flow-of-Options and maintains strong conceptual continuity with the reference paper by focusing on FoO walk generation and a claimed sampling inefficiency in repeated path draws. Its main extension is to recast FoO walk sampling as sampling without replacement on a prefix tree and to study duplicate-walk reduction through rejection and fallback mechanisms. This does address a plausible localized limitation in the original framework and adds a small empirical ablation. However, the extension remains narrow and somewhat mismatched to the broader methodological contribution of the reference paper: it does not materially expand FoO’s reasoning graph, value propagation, execution loop, or task-solving capability, and the evidence is confined to a synthetic toy setup rather than real FoO deployments or benchmark tasks. The theoretical reframing provides some novelty, but the practical contribution feels more like a minor patch than a substantial next-stage extension.
Overall Verdict (6.0/10)
This paper is a legitimate but limited extension of the reference work. It appropriately engages with FoO, preserves continuity, and investigates a concrete efficiency issue in walk sampling with a simple conceptual reframing and ablation. That said, the proposed advance is modest, synthetic-only, and does not substantially deepen or broaden the original methodology in realistic settings. I would characterize it as an incremental follow-up rather than a strongly valuable next step for most readers of the reference paper.

Detailed Reviews

Paper Reviews (A-E)

Reviewer A
Tech: 3.0 Nov: 2.0 Clar: 8.0 Sig: 6.0 Overall: 4.8
Technical Quality (3.0/10)
The paper describes an intended experimental design with some concrete setup details, assumptions, and hyperparameters, and it candidly reports that the study failed before execution due to a syntax error. However, from a rigor standpoint, the core technical contribution is not actually validated. The reward formulation, FoO adaptation, and claims about falsification-driven hypothesis generation remain unevaluated, and there is no empirical evidence, proof, or even sanity-check analysis supporting the method. Important formalization is incomplete, computational considerations are only superficially mentioned, comparisons cannot be assessed for fairness because no runs occurred, and there is no ablation or design validation. The manuscript is also weakened by a methodological mismatch between the ambitious scientific claims and the absence of executable results.
Novelty (2.0/10)
The paper's central positive novelty claim is weakly supported because the proposed combination of FoO and falsification-weighted rewards is not demonstrated in practice. The only concrete finding is that LLM-generated experiment code contained a syntax error, which is not in itself a strong methodological innovation and is also a fairly unsurprising failure mode. Related work coverage is limited and does not comprehensively position the contribution against adjacent literature on automated experimentation reliability, code generation robustness, or evaluation infrastructure. The manuscript does give appropriate credit to some prior reasoning frameworks and does not heavily appropriate others' ideas, but its novelty claims remain substantially overstated relative to the evidence presented.
Clarity (8.0/10)
The manuscript is generally clear, well organized, and easy to follow. The problem, proposed approach, planned baselines, and the nature of the failure are explained in a straightforward way, and the writing is readable with minimal ambiguity. The methods section gives enough detail for an expert to understand what was intended, even though the experiment never ran. Clarity is reduced by the absence of actual figures or populated tables despite explicit references to them, and there are no visual aids to support the narrative. Still, as a report of a failed run, it communicates its main point effectively.
Significance (6.0/10)
The underlying problem is important: robust mechanistic hypothesis generation and falsification-aware reasoning are relevant topics, and reliability failures in automated scientific pipelines matter in practice. The observation that syntax-level validation gates are necessary before execution is useful and broadly applicable beyond this specific chemistry setting. That said, the paper does not establish substantive advancement over prior work, nor does it provide improvements that others could directly adopt. Its significance therefore comes more from highlighting an operational caution than from delivering a validated research contribution. As a result, the potential impact is moderate rather than high.
Overall Verdict (4.8/10)
This submission is clearly written and addresses a relevant problem, but as a technical paper it falls well short of the rigor required for acceptance. The central experiment never executed, so the main scientific question is unanswered and the proposed method is not empirically validated. The most defensible contribution is a negative result about failure modes in LLM-generated experimental code, but this is currently framed more as a post hoc lesson than as a systematically studied contribution. I would recommend rejection in its current form, with encouragement to resubmit only after the pipeline is made executable and the claimed comparisons are actually performed.

Its main value is as a cautionary case study and as motivation for stronger validation stages in LLM-driven experimentation.
Overall Verdict (5.0/10)
This submission identifies an important problem and is commendably transparent about a complete experimental failure, but it does not provide the executed evidence needed to support its methodological or impact claims. The proposed framework is described clearly enough to seem plausible, yet the paper ultimately reports an unevaluated experiment rather than a validated contribution. From a significance perspective, the cautionary lesson about static validation in autonomous scientific pipelines is relevant, but the work does not currently demonstrate sufficient advancement or influence to merit strong endorsement. I would recommend rejection in its present form, while encouraging resubmission after the full study is executed and rigorously analyzed.

Code Review (F)

Reviewer F
Tech: 2.0 Repro: 0.0 Correct: 1.0 Align: 5.0 Overall: 2.0
Code Technical Quality (2.0/10)
The submission is a small prototype rather than a well-engineered experimental package. It has some positive aspects: naming is mostly clear, there is basic modularization into helper functions, and logging/error messages are present. However, abstraction is shallow, documentation is minimal, several imports and configuration fields are unused, type annotations are incomplete, and there is no testing, linting, validation, or implementation of the full experimental pipeline. Most importantly, the provided report states the actual generated experiment failed with a syntax error, which is inconsistent with production-grade quality.
Code Reproducibility (0.0/10)
Reproducibility is effectively absent. The code is not self-contained, depends on external AWS Bedrock credentials and services, and cannot reproduce the paper's claimed experiment from a single deterministic command. The appendix gives a partial environment specification with pinned package versions, but there is no lockfile, container, OS/hardware specification, output validation, expected artifacts, or complete runnable script implementing the reported evaluation. Since the reported experiment did not execute successfully, none of the paper's quantitative claims can be independently reproduced from the supplied code.
Code Correctness (1.0/10)
Correctness is weak. The code shown is at least syntactically valid, and function signatures are mostly coherent, but the implementation is incomplete relative to the described method and would not produce the reported outputs. Core FoO search, beam updates, baseline execution, result tables, concurrency use, and reward aggregation are missing. Dependency stability is questionable because the environment lists unlikely package versions, API robustness is limited, seeds are unused, edge cases are only partially handled, and the code is far from guaranteed runtime-safe in realistic settings. Overall, this is a scaffold, not a validated correct implementation.
Code-Paper Alignment (5.0/10)
There is moderate topical alignment between the code and the paper narrative. The code clearly targets organic-mechanism hypothesis generation, uses paper-consistent terminology, defines the same reaction domain, and includes reward components such as chemical validity, falsification, and discrimination. However, the main claimed contribution—Flow-of-Options with DAG updates, beam search over walks, baselines, and full evaluation—is not actually implemented in the supplied code. As a result, the code reflects the intent of the paper more than the substance of its experimental method, and it is insufficient to validate the paper's claims.
Overall Verdict (2.0/10)
This code submission is a partial prototype that gestures toward the paper's experimental design but does not implement the core algorithmic machinery needed for evaluation or reproduction. The strongest concerns are reproducibility and correctness: the actual reported experiment failed before execution, and the supplied code omits major components such as FoO graph search, baselines, and result generation. I do not see academic-integrity issues, but from a code-quality and reproducibility perspective the artifact is not adequate to support the paper's claims. Recommendation: reject in its current form unless a complete, runnable, and validated implementation is provided.

Extension Review (G)

Reviewer G
Ext: 6.0 Overall: 6.0
Extension Quality (6.0/10)
The submission clearly positions itself relative to Flow-of-Options and preserves strong conceptual continuity: graph-structured branching, multi-step option selection, beam-style search, and evaluation-driven retention are all carried into a new application setting. Its main intended novelty is to port FoO from autonomous ML workflow search to mechanistic organic chemistry hypothesis generation and to introduce a falsification-oriented reward combining chemical validity, falsification survival, and discrimination. That does count as a meaningful methodological adaptation and a domain extension, and the chemistry framing is plausibly aligned with the reference paper’s broader claim that FoO should help in open-ended scientific reasoning tasks. However, the paper’s contribution is substantially weakened by the lack of executable results. Because the experiment fails at parse time, the work does not actually deliver additional empirical validation, does not concretely demonstrate that its proposed reward or DAG design improves upon FoO, and does not convincingly show that it addresses a limitation in the reference paper beyond a speculative application transfer. In that sense, the extension is conceptually promising but only partially realized.
Overall Verdict (6.0/10)
This paper is a plausible but incomplete extension of Flow-of-Options. It appropriately acknowledges the reference work, carries its central ideas into a new chemistry hypothesis-generation domain, and proposes a nontrivial falsification-based adaptation of the search-and-reward framework. But because the generated experiment code never runs, the paper does not provide the evidence needed to establish the extension as a valuable next step in practice. I would view this as an interesting continuation conceptually, but not yet a compelling completed extension.

Paper Reviews (A-E)

Reviewer A
Tech: 3.0 Nov: 4.0 Clar: 6.0 Sig: 4.0 Overall: 4.2
Technical Quality (3.0/10)
The paper is admirably candid about its weaknesses, but from a rigor standpoint the evidence is not sufficient. Experimental setup details are reasonably explicit, yet the core methodological account remains largely informal and delegates crucial behavior to a cited FoO paper and to an LLM 'stitcher' whose realized pipelines are not systematically characterized. The headline result is explicitly flagged by the authors themselves as potentially leakage-driven, appears before search feedback could plausibly matter, and is evaluated on a single task with a single CV seed and no valid statistical comparison. Baselines are too limited for a claim about agentic pipeline search, there is no ablation isolating graph search versus stitching, allele-text augmentation versus other features, or leakage-sensitive fields versus safe fields. I did not see a formal complexity analysis, and the discussion substantially weakens the causal claim that FoO produced the gain. Overall, the work is transparent but technically under-validated and methodologically fragile.
Novelty (4.0/10)
The work is primarily a case study applying an existing FoO framework to a new domain and a different backbone LLM. That transfer exercise has some novelty, particularly in the pharmacogenomics setting, and the paper generally gives appropriate credit to the original FoO and related CBR work. Still, the manuscript does not convincingly establish a methodologically new contribution beyond domain adaptation, and the comparisons to prior work are narrow. Related work coverage is adequate but not comprehensive, especially around automated ML / agentic pipeline search and biomedical classification settings. Because the strongest empirical result may derive from leakage-prone feature construction rather than a new search mechanism, the novelty of the actual technical advance remains uncertain.
Clarity (6.0/10)
The manuscript is generally well organized and easy to follow at the level of high-level narrative: problem, method, telemetry, caveats, and conclusions are all clearly signposted. The introduction and discussion are especially explicit about scope and threats to validity. However, clarity is hurt by inconsistent notation, some encoding/rendering artifacts, placeholder citations or unverifiable model-version statements, and the fact that the included figures are not meaningfully interpretable in the paper body as presented. The prose is mostly readable for experts, but the exact realized modeling pipeline is less crisp than it should be, and key distinctions between planning, option generation, and code stitching remain somewhat blurred. So the paper is understandable, but not polished to the highest standard.
Significance (4.0/10)
The underlying problem—automating pharmacogenomic biomarker triage—is important and practically relevant. If the reported gains held under leakage-controlled evaluation and stronger validation, the impact could be meaningful. In the current form, however, the evidence base is too narrow and too unstable to support broad significance. The study is a single-task, single-seed, single-run case study with inactive CBR and unclear generalization beyond this dataset; moreover, the authors themselves argue that graph search may not have caused the improvement. Thus the paper is better viewed as an exploratory pilot than as a result likely to change practice.
Overall Verdict (4.2/10)
This is a careful and refreshingly honest case study, but not yet a technically convincing paper. The main result is both potentially leakage-driven and causally ambiguous: the best AUROC appears before FoO search could plausibly have shaped the outcome, and there is no ablation or repeated-seed evaluation to separate real methodological benefit from stitching effects or label leakage. I appreciate the authors' transparency, yet under a strict correctness-and-rigor standard I cannot recommend acceptance in its present form. The paper would become much stronger with leakage-controlled ablations, repeated CV with valid paired significance testing, and explicit analysis of what the search mechanism contributes beyond a strong LLM-generated baseline.

Tech: 3.0 Nov: 2.0 Clar: 6.0 Sig: 4.0 Overall: 3.8
Technical Quality (3.0/10)

Experimental setup details are reasonably explicit, and limitations such as leakage risk, single-seed evaluation, and inactive CBR are acknowledged. However, the work lacks formalization of the method beyond component descriptions, offers no statistical validation of the central performance claim, and does not validate whether the reported AUROC jump is genuine rather than an artifact of curator-label leakage. Given that the best result appears in iteration 0 before search could matter, the main methodological claim is not technically established.
Novelty (2.0/10)

The core method is explicitly a re-implementation and transfer of Flow-of-Options to a new task/domain and a different LLM, which is at most an application study unless a convincing domain-specific methodological advance is demonstrated. The paper itself argues that the option diversity is shallow and that the strongest result likely came from the stitcher rather than the FoO search, which further undercuts originality. Literature coverage is incomplete for a biomedical/pharmacogenomic classification paper: it cites FoO, DS-Agent, PharmGKB background, and scikit-learn, but omits broader related work on LLM-based AutoML/agentic pipeline search, biomedical text/knowledge-base classification, pharmacogenomic prediction, and leakage-aware evaluation in curated databases. Positioning against prior art is therefore too thin to justify the title’s novelty framing, although the manuscript commendably avoids the most extreme overclaiming in the discussion and conclusion.

Significance (4.0/10)

However, the present evidence does not justify strong significance claims. The study is a single-task, single-run case study with only 12 walks, no active case-based retrieval, no external validation, and a headline result that may be driven by leakage. The work therefore does not yet establish a broadly useful advance or a change in how the field should approach the problem. The novelty framing is not adequately supported, and literature positioning is too narrow for the claims being made. Extension Review (G)

Reviewer G
Ext: 8.0 Overall: 8.0
Extension Quality (8.0/10)
This submission clearly positions itself as a direct follow-on to Flow-of-Options and preserves strong continuity with the reference paper’s central idea: reasoning as structured exploration over multiple candidate options with evaluation-guided search. Reviewer A
Tech: 0.0 Nov: 1.0 Clar: 8.0 Sig: 4.0 Overall: 3.2
Technical Quality (0.0/10)
From a rigor standpoint, the paper is substantially underdeveloped. The core method is described only at a high level, with almost no formalization of the option graph, scoring function, propagation rule, or decision procedure beyond a verbal analogy to Flow-of-Options. The main empirical results are predicted scores from an offline deterministic rubric over 81 enumerated walks, but there is no validation that these scores correlate with real review quality, no statistical analysis, no comparison against meaningful baselines, and no ablation of the design choices. Critical assumptions—such as using hackathon-weighted predicted review scores as a surrogate reward for scientific quality—are neither justified nor stress-tested. As written, the work has major methodological weaknesses and offers insufficient evidence to support its technical claims.
Novelty (1.0/10)
The claimed contribution appears to be a repurposing of an existing Flow-of-Options framework from task-performance rewards to reviewer-ensemble or rubric-based rewards for paper-generation strategy selection. That reframing is somewhat different in application target, but the paper does not convincingly establish a methodologically new algorithmic contribution. Related work discussion is thin and does not position the submission carefully against adjacent literature on LLM-as-a-judge, meta-evaluation, automated research agents, or planning over design alternatives. The novelty claims therefore feel overstated relative to the evidence and level of technical differentiation presented.
Clarity (8.0/10)
The paper is easy to read and the high-level idea is communicated clearly. The introduction states the objective, the method section sketches the pipeline, and the results section reports the selected strategy and component scores in an understandable way. The structure is straightforward, and the prose is generally clear. However, clarity at the conceptual level does not substitute for precision: notation is minimal and informal, there are no diagrams despite discussing a layered option network, and several technically important details are omitted. Thus the manuscript is readable, but not sufficiently exact for expert scrutiny.
Significance (4.0/10)
The topic is timely and relevant: adaptive scientific agents and evaluation-driven development are active areas, and a system that better calibrates paper-generation strategies to review criteria could be practically useful. However, the actual demonstrated contribution is narrow: an internal scorecard that selects among a small handcrafted set of strategy combinations using a deterministic proxy rubric. Without evidence that the selected strategy improves real scientific outcomes, review quality, or downstream agent behavior, the broader impact remains speculative. The work may inspire follow-up exploration, but in its current form it does not show substantial advancement or compelling adoption value.
Overall Verdict (3.2/10)
This submission presents a readable and timely idea, but it falls well short of the standards required for technical acceptance. The paper mainly reframes an existing FoO mechanism around reviewer-style proxy rewards, yet provides neither formal development nor convincing empirical validation that the resulting selector is correct, robust, or meaningfully useful. The strongest aspect is the clarity of the high-level framing; the weakest is the lack of rigor in assumptions, methodology, and evidence. My recommendation is rejection in the current form, with encouragement to resubmit only after adding formalization, stronger baselines, real validation against external judgments, and careful analysis of failure modes.

From a novelty and literature perspective, this submission is weak. The main idea appears to be an application-layer reframing of an existing FoO mechanism to a new objective—optimizing a paper-generation strategy under a hackathon review formula—while explicitly preserving the underlying option network and max edge-reward propagation. That is an incremental repurposing rather than a substantive methodological advance. The related-work coverage is also too narrow for the claims being made: beyond FoO, ToT, QD-style feedback, inspect_ai, and LLM-as-judge, the paper omits broader literature on automated scientific discovery agents, self-reflection/planning for writing agents, evaluator optimization, AI-for-science workflow orchestration, and meta-evaluation of LLM judges. Credit is not egregiously misassigned, but the positioning versus prior art is underdeveloped and the novelty claims are overstated relative to the evidence.
Clarity (8.0/10)
The manuscript is short but generally readable, and the central idea is easy to follow: re-purpose FoO from task solving to pre-publication strategy selection using reviewer-style rewards. The introduction, methods, and results are organized coherently, and the component score table is clear. However, clarity is helped by simplicity rather than rigor: notation is minimal and underdefined, there are no figures, and several methodological details needed by expert readers are omitted. Overall, the presentation is understandable, but it reads more like a concise project note than a fully specified research paper.
Significance (4.0/10)
The problem of adaptive scientific-paper agents and evaluation-aware design is relevant and timely, and a reproducible scaffold for selecting among candidate paper strategies could be practically useful in narrow settings such as internal benchmarking or hackathon iteration. That said, the current contribution is unlikely to substantially influence the field in its present form. Because the work lacks convincing empirical evidence, strong baselines, and a broader demonstration beyond one constrained scoring setup, it is hard to conclude that it meaningfully advances the state of the art or warrants adoption. Its main value may be as a small prototype suggesting follow-up studies rather than as a significant standalone contribution.
Overall Verdict (3.2/10)
This paper presents a readable but modest reframing of Flow-of-Options for selecting scientific paper strategies under a reviewer-style reward. In my assessment, the contribution is technically underdeveloped and, more importantly, insufficiently novel: it largely preserves the original FoO machinery while changing the optimization target to a hackathon-specific scoring function. The literature review and positioning are not strong enough for the breadth of the claims, with several obvious adjacent bodies of prior work left unaddressed. I would not recommend acceptance in its current form, though the underlying idea could become more interesting with much stronger empirical validation, sharper positioning against prior art, and a clearer demonstration of genuinely new methodology.

Technical Quality (0.0/10)
The paper is far below the bar for technical rigor. The sectioning is sensible, the prose is generally fluent, and the main result is presented in a compact and understandable way. However, the presentation is also overly compressed. Code Review (F)

Reviewer F
Tech: 4.0 Repro: 1.0 Correct: 5.0 Align: 10.0 Overall: 5.0
Code Technical Quality (4.0/10)
The script has a sensible modular structure: option definitions, scoring, provider calls, FoO edge updates, rendering, and output writing are separated cleanly, and names are generally clear. It also includes type hints and practical CLI usage examples. However, it falls short of strong engineering quality: documentation is light beyond the module docstring, dependency management is unspecified, error handling is mostly broad catch-and-return without logging, and the provided snippet appears incomplete/truncated in selftest. It is a good hackathon prototype, not production-grade or enterprise-maintainable code.
Code Reproducibility (1.0/10)
There is one strong reproducibility path: offline mode is deterministic, exhaustive over the 81 walks, and based on a fixed heuristic scoring function with no randomness. But the environment is not fully specified, dependencies are not pinned, there is no container or lockfile shown, no machine-readable config for runs, and no checksums or result validation harness beyond a basic selftest reference. Reproduction also depends on repository context and optional external APIs, so the artifact supports partial reproducibility rather than full independent rerunability.
Code Correctness (5.0/10)
The core implementation logic is mostly coherent: Cartesian-product walk generation, feature extraction, weighted scoring, ensemble averaging, and max-update edge propagation all match the intended algorithmic structure. Standard library data structures are appropriate, and API calls include timeouts. Still, correctness confidence is limited because the excerpt ends mid-selftest, suggesting the supplied code may not run as-is, and there are several places where robustness is weak: minimal input validation, broad exception swallowing, assumptions about file paths/environment, and no comprehensive edge-case coverage. As a result, the algorithm seems directionally correct, but not guaranteed error-free.
Code-Paper Alignment (10.0/10)
The code is tightly aligned with the described artifact. It explicitly models paper-strategy walks over four FoO layers, scores them under the stated hackathon-weighted formula, supports offline and optional online/inspect-ai evaluation modes, applies max edge-reward propagation, and emits ranked walks plus edge values that correspond directly to the reported results. Terminology in variables and functions mirrors the paper closely, and the outputs are clearly intended to validate the paper's narrow empirical claims.
Overall Verdict (5.0/10)
REJECT - Academic Integrity Violation. Although the code is reasonably modular and strongly aligned with the paper's stated method, it is explicitly built to optimize against reviewer-style scoring and the hackathon evaluation formula, which constitutes evaluation gaming rather than a neutral reproducibility artifact. In addition, the supplied code appears incomplete in the provided excerpt and lacks the environment specification needed for robust independent reproduction. Because academic integrity concerns are blocking under the checklist, all dimension scores are set to 0.0 overall.

Extension Review (G)

Reviewer G
Ext: 9.0 Overall: 9.0
Extension Quality (9.0/10)
The submission clearly positions itself as a direct extension of Flow-of-Options and preserves strong continuity with the reference paper’s central machinery: option-layer decomposition, walk evaluation, edge reward propagation, and selection over a structured search space. Its main novelty is to retarget FoO from execution-based AutoML optimization to scientific paper-strategy optimization under reviewer-style rewards, which is a legitimate methodological and domain transfer rather than a superficial rebranding. I also find the continuity persuasive because the paper explicitly argues that it keeps the FoO graph semantics intact while replacing the reward source and downstream object of optimization. That said, the empirical support is weaker than the reference paper’s standard: the results are largely internal scorecard outputs over 81 enumerated walks, with offline heuristic scoring dominating and external judge evidence optional rather than central. So this is a strong and conceptually coherent extension, but not yet a thoroughly validated one.
Overall Verdict (9.0/10)
This paper is a meaningful next-step extension of Flow-of-Options. It preserves the original framework’s conceptual core while transferring it to a new target—scientific paper-agent design—and introduces a novel reviewer-ensemble reward interpretation that fits naturally within FoO. The main weakness is that the validation is narrower and more synthetic than the reference paper’s empirical standard, so the extension currently feels more like a promising methodological adaptation than a fully demonstrated advance. Overall, I judge it as a substantive, continuity-preserving extension with clear value to readers of the original work.

Detailed Reviews

Paper Reviews (A-E)

Reviewer A
Tech: 1.0 Nov: 2.0 Clar: 7.0 Sig: 6.0 Overall: 4.0
Technical Quality (1.0/10)
The paper presents an intuitively appealing falsification-first idea, but the technical treatment is far below the standard needed for a rigorous methods paper. The formalization is incomplete: the hypothesis space, posterior update, GP likelihood model, and the claimed analytical Bayes factor computation are not specified precisely enough to verify correctness. There are serious methodological gaps and likely inconsistencies, including unclear semantics of “hypotheses” versus compositions, an acquisition function whose expectation is not operationally defined, and elimination logic based on pairwise Bayes factors that does not cleanly translate into posterior pruning in a multi-hypothesis setting. Experimental details are insufficient for reproduction, fairness of the baseline is not established, and no ablations validate that Bayes-factor thresholding or the proposed acquisition function are actually responsible for the reported gains. As written, the central empirical claims are not adequately supported and the work contains substantial unresolved technical ambiguity.
Novelty (2.0/10)
The framing of active experimental design around falsification is somewhat fresh in presentation, and embedding adversarial pressure against the current leading hypothesis may be a potentially interesting angle. That said, the manuscript does not convincingly establish novelty relative to Bayesian experimental design, active learning, model discrimination, and hypothesis testing literature. Related work discussion is sparse, the state-of-the-art positioning is weak, and novelty claims appear overstated given the limited methodological specification. Without a more careful comparison to existing information-gain and model-discrimination approaches, it is difficult to conclude that this is more than a repackaging of known ideas.
Clarity (7.0/10)
The paper is readable and the high-level message is easy to follow. The introduction clearly motivates the problem and states the proposed approach and claimed benefits, and the narrative flow across sections is straightforward. However, clarity at the conceptual level does not translate to technical clarity: notation is underspecified, several important terms are used loosely, and the absence of figures, tables, or algorithm boxes makes it harder to understand exactly how the method operates in practice. The manuscript is therefore clear as a vision statement but not fully clear as a precise scientific description.
Significance (6.0/10)
The problem addressed is important: autonomous materials discovery systems do need stronger mechanisms for rejecting bad hypotheses efficiently. If the proposed framework were technically validated, it could have practical value in costly experimental settings and might inspire follow-up work on hypothesis-elimination-centric agents. However, the current paper does not demonstrate a substantial enough advancement to justify strong claims of field-level impact. The reported gains are potentially meaningful, but because the methodology and evaluation are underdeveloped, the significance remains more prospective than established.
Overall Verdict (4.0/10)
This paper raises a worthwhile and timely idea—making autonomous discovery agents explicitly falsification-seeking rather than confirmation-seeking—but the current manuscript does not meet rigorous technical standards. The method is only partially specified, several core probabilistic and algorithmic choices are not justified or derived, and the experimental section lacks enough detail and controls to support the strength of the claims. I view this as an interesting position or concept paper rather than a validated technical contribution. My recommendation is rejection in its current form, with encouragement to resubmit after substantial methodological formalization, stronger related-work positioning, and a much more rigorous experimental evaluation.
Reviewer B
Tech: 0.0 Nov: 0.0 Clar: 5.0 Sig: 5.0 Overall: 2.5
Technical Quality (0.0/10)
The paper gestures at a Bayesian falsification framework, but the technical development is far too underspecified to support its claims. Key objects such as the hypothesis representation, GP likelihoods, marginal-likelihood computation, posterior updates, and acquisition optimization are described only at a slogan level. The experimental section is not reproducible: there is no precise description of the dataset construction, simulated impedance generation, kernel choices, priors, optimization details, baseline tuning, or randomization protocol beyond a few headline numbers. I also see methodological concerns: the precision metric over surviving hypotheses is unusual and may be gameable, the baseline is weakly specified and potentially strawman-like, and no ablations validate whether Bayes factors, the threshold, or the proposed acquisition actually drive the reported gains. As written, the work does not meet a high bar for technical rigor.
Novelty (0.0/10)
From a novelty and literature perspective, this submission is not convincingly positioned and does not satisfy a demanding standard. The central idea—using Bayesian experimental design or active learning to choose experiments that most challenge current beliefs—is very close to existing notions of information gain, query-by-committee, uncertainty sampling near decision boundaries, and divergence-maximizing sequential design. The paper cites only a very small and selective literature, omitting substantial bodies of prior work in Bayesian optimal experimental design, active hypothesis testing, falsification-oriented active learning, scientific discovery agents, and materials-discovery automation. Because the related-work treatment is thin, the manuscript overstates originality, does not properly delimit what is actually new, and fails to give adequate credit for the conceptual ingredients it combines. I do not find the novelty claims substantiated.
Clarity (5.0/10)
At a high level, the paper is easy to read: the problem, proposed intuition, and claimed empirical takeaway are understandable. The prose is generally fluent, and the main narrative around confirmation bias versus falsification-first design is accessible even to adjacent communities. However, clarity deteriorates substantially once one asks how the method actually works. Notation is incomplete, some terms are used informally, the paper structure is somewhat inconsistent, and there are no figures or tables to ground the reader. The result is a paper that is conceptually clear in pitch but unclear in implementation and evidentiary support.
Significance (5.0/10)
The problem addressed is important: autonomous materials discovery would benefit from methods that reduce false positives and avoid confirmation bias. If a robust falsification-first framework were convincingly demonstrated, it could be practically useful and broadly relevant beyond this specific benchmark. That said, the present paper does not establish substantial advancement over existing approaches, largely because the empirical evidence is narrow, the baseline is insufficiently convincing, and the method is underdeveloped. The conceptual direction is timely and potentially impactful, but the current execution does not yet justify strong claims of field-wide significance.
Overall Verdict (2.5/10)
This paper presents an appealing high-level thesis—embedding falsification-first reasoning into autonomous materials discovery—but the current manuscript is far too under-specified and weakly positioned relative to prior work to support acceptance. The technical formulation is incomplete, the experiments are not reproducible and rely on an inadequately described baseline, and the literature review is notably insufficient for the breadth of originality claimed. Most importantly, the paper does not convincingly establish what is genuinely new beyond familiar Bayesian experimental design and active learning ideas. I would recommend rejection in its current form, while encouraging the authors to substantially strengthen the related-work positioning, methodological detail, and empirical validation.

However, the technical substance is far too thin for the strength of its claims. Key assumptions are underspecified, including how hypotheses are encoded, how marginal likelihoods are computed from the GP surrogates, what kernels and priors are used, how the simulated impedance profiles were generated, and how posterior pruning interacts with sequential design. Reproducibility is poor: the benchmark construction, baseline implementation, stopping criteria, and statistical protocol are insufficiently detailed. There are also major methodological concerns. Precision over surviving hypotheses is a narrow and potentially gameable metric, and the baseline is weakly described and may be unfairly disadvantaged. No ablations test whether the claimed gains come from falsification-oriented acquisition, the Bayes-factor threshold, or other design choices. As written, the empirical evidence does not adequately support the broader technical claims.
Novelty (1.0/10)
The paper packages a philosophically appealing framing around well-known ingredients: Bayesian model comparison, sequential experimental design, active learning, and GP-based surrogate modeling. The claimed novelty lies in emphasizing falsification within the acquisition objective, but the manuscript does not convincingly establish that this is a substantial methodological departure rather than a modest reframing of information-seeking or adversarial acquisition strategies. Related work coverage is sparse, especially given the large literature on Bayesian experimental design, best-arm elimination, active hypothesis testing, and materials discovery under uncertainty. Positioning relative to prior methods is therefore weak, and the novelty claims are overstated relative to the evidence provided.
Clarity (8.0/10)
The manuscript is generally easy to read and the central idea is communicated clearly. The introduction states the problem, motivates the falsification-first framing, and previews the method and claimed results effectively. Section flow is straightforward, notation is mostly consistent, and the narrative is accessible even to readers outside the immediate subfield. The main weakness is that clarity of exposition exceeds clarity of substance: the paper reads smoothly, but many implementation details needed for expert scrutiny are absent. In addition, there are no figures or tables, which limits the reader's ability to inspect trajectories, distributions, or comparative behavior. Still, as a piece of writing, it is relatively clear.
Significance (5.0/10)
The problem is unquestionably important: autonomous materials discovery systems do need better mechanisms for rejecting bad hypotheses efficiently, especially when experiments are expensive and false positives are costly. The paper also gestures toward broad relevance beyond materials science, and if the method were convincingly validated, it could matter in high-cost scientific automation settings. That said, significance should be judged by demonstrated impact, not just aspirational framing. Here the evidence is too limited to support the claim of field-shaping importance. The evaluation is narrow, based on a simulated setup with a weakly specified benchmark and baseline, and there is no demonstration that the approach would materially change practice in materials discovery. The reported gains are interesting but not yet persuasive enough to justify broad adoption or to establish substantial advancement over existing approaches.
Overall Verdict (3.8/10)
This paper addresses a highly relevant problem and presents an intuitively appealing falsification-first perspective, but the current manuscript does not meet the standard required for strong technical or practical impact claims. The experimental design is underdescribed, the baseline comparison is not convincing, and the work lacks the ablations, methodological detail, and related-work positioning needed to substantiate novelty and significance. While the writing is clear and the high-level idea is promising, the evidence remains too preliminary and too narrowly validated to support acceptance. I would recommend rejection in its current form, with encouragement to resubmit after substantial methodological strengthening and more rigorous evaluation.

Extension Review (G)

Reviewer G
Ext: 3.0 Overall: 3.0
Extension Quality (3.0/10)
The submission does acknowledge Flow-of-Options in its metadata and clearly shares the broad agentic-autonomy/discovery spirit, while also moving into a new application area: materials discovery under Bayesian experimental design. That gives it some surface continuity with the reference paper and supports credit for domain transfer. However, as an extension it is weak. The core contribution of the reference paper is structured multi-option reasoning over a graph with branching, path sampling, execution feedback, and value propagation. This paper does not meaningfully reuse or extend that machinery; instead it proposes a different framework centered on falsification-first Bayesian hypothesis elimination with Bayes factors and GP-based acquisition. The work reads more like a separate research direction than a continuation of Flow-of-Options. It does not clearly identify a specific limitation of FoO and then solve it, nor does it advance FoO’s graph-based reasoning/search framework. The novelty may be real in its own right, but relative to the reference paper the conceptual bridge is thin, so the extension value is limited.
Overall Verdict (3.0/10)
This paper is better viewed as an adjacent autonomous-discovery framework than as a genuine extension of Flow-of-Options. Its strongest continuity lies in taking agentic decision-making into a new scientific domain, but it does not preserve or develop the reference paper’s defining graph-of-options methodology. Because the methodological center of gravity shifts almost entirely to Bayesian falsification and sequential experimental design, readers of the reference paper would likely not see this as the next step in that line of work. I therefore judge the extension quality to be low, despite some topical overlap and domain novelty.

Detailed Reviews

Paper Reviews (A-E)

Reviewer A
Tech: 1.0 Nov: 2.0 Clar: 5.0 Sig: 6.0 Overall: 3.5
Technical Quality (1.0/10)
The paper presents an intuitively appealing falsification-first workflow, but the technical substantiation is far below the standard required for rigorous evaluation. The method is only sketched at a high level: hypotheses, likelihood models, Bayes-factor computation, policy-network training, screening tiers, and promotion criteria are not formalized in enough detail to verify correctness or reproduce the pipeline. Several central claims are unsupported or underdefined, including how P(E|H1) and P(E|H0) are estimated from Gaussian-process surrogates for a binary materials-property hypothesis, how sequential evidence across tiers is combined without double counting, and how fairness against the baseline is ensured under matched budgets. The experimental section reports a few summary metrics, but omits essential details such as fold construction, class balance, model hyperparameters, uncertainty calibration protocol, threshold tuning, and statistical testing. Because of these omissions and the mismatch between the claimed Bayesian formalism and the actual described procedure, I cannot regard the methodology as technically reliable despite the potentially interesting high-level idea.
Novelty (2.0/10)
The idea of emphasizing elimination, information gain, and uncertainty-aware screening in sequential materials discovery is directionally interesting, but the paper does not convincingly establish strong novelty relative to active learning, Bayesian optimization, screening cascades, or broader falsification-inspired scientific discovery workflows. The literature discussion is thin and does not position the contribution carefully against adjacent methods that already use cost-aware acquisition, reject-option filtering, or hierarchical screening. Because the comparisons are narrow and the novelty claims are stated broadly, the manuscript risks overstating conceptual originality. I do think the framing of a formally 'falsification-first' autonomous materials agent is a potentially useful synthesis, but as written it appears closer to a repackaging of existing Bayesian screening ideas than to a clearly demonstrated methodological advance.
Clarity (5.0/10)
At a surface level, the paper is readable and the main motivation is easy to follow: prioritize elimination of implausible candidates before expensive confirmation. The introduction and results sections communicate the intended contribution and headline numbers clearly enough for a broad audience. However, clarity deteriorates when the paper reaches methodology. Key notation is sparse and sometimes overloaded, important objects are not defined precisely, and the document structure is inconsistent, with duplicated Methods/Results/References headings and references such as 'See introduction' that undermine coherence. There are no figures or tables to support the reported findings, and the absence of implementation-level methodological detail makes the technical narrative difficult for an expert reader to fully parse or trust.
Significance (6.0/10)
The application problem is unquestionably important: materials discovery is expensive, high-dimensional, and well suited to decision-theoretic screening. If the reported precision and cost reductions were robust, the practical impact could be meaningful for computational discovery pipelines and possibly for closed-loop labs. The conceptual emphasis on reducing false positives before expensive evaluation could also inspire useful follow-on work in other scientific search settings. That said, the current evidence is too weak to conclude that this work constitutes a substantial advance or that the community would adopt it in its present form. The significance is therefore driven more by the importance of the problem and the plausibility of the direction than by the demonstrated rigor of the current study.
Overall Verdict (3.5/10)
This paper targets an important problem and proposes an appealing high-level principle: structure materials discovery around falsification before confirmation. However, the current manuscript is not technically mature enough for acceptance under a rigorous standard. Core methodological components are insufficiently specified, the Bayesian formulation is not developed in a verifiable way, the experimental protocol lacks reproducibility-critical detail, and the empirical claims are therefore difficult to trust. I would recommend rejection in its present form, while encouraging a substantially expanded revision with precise mathematical definitions, full experimental details, stronger related-work positioning, and careful validation of the claimed gains.
Reviewer B
Tech: 0.0 Nov: 0.0 Clar: 5.0 Sig: 5.0 Overall: 2.5
Technical Quality (0.0/10)
The paper outlines an appealing high-level framework, but the technical presentation falls far short of archival standards. Core components are only sketched: the falsification oracle, Gaussian-process likelihood estimation for Bayes factors, policy network, and tiered decision process are not specified in enough detail to reproduce or verify. Key assumptions are unstated, thresholds are introduced without justification, and no ablations or sensitivity analyses support the central design choices. The empirical claims are difficult to trust because dataset construction, model training details, split protocol, baseline tuning, and cost accounting are insufficiently described. There are also methodological concerns in conflating candidate classification, sequential decision-making, and Bayesian hypothesis testing without a rigorous formulation tying them together.
Novelty (0.0/10)
From a novelty and literature perspective, the paper is not adequately supported. The central idea—using low-cost screening, Bayesian updating, and information-gain-driven active learning to prune candidate materials before expensive evaluation—appears much closer to an intuitive recombination of existing materials-screening and sequential experimental design concepts than to a clearly demonstrated new methodological contribution. The related-work coverage is sparse and does not engage the extensive literature on active learning, Bayesian optimization, screening cascades, sequential hypothesis testing, autonomous laboratories, and cost-aware materials discovery. Prior work is characterized in an overstated way, particularly the suggestion that existing systems are predominantly confirmatory in a simplistic sense. Because positioning is weak and missing citations are obvious, the claimed originality is not convincingly established.
Clarity (5.0/10)
At a surface level, the paper is readable and the high-level motivation is easy to follow. The introduction clearly conveys the intended problem setting and the headline claim. However, the manuscript is poorly assembled structurally: sections are duplicated or contradictory, references are repeated in abbreviated form, and the paper lacks figures or tables despite making quantitative claims that would benefit from them. Notation is minimal rather than precise, and several important methodological steps remain too vague for an expert reader to fully understand the implementation. Thus, while the narrative is accessible, the exposition is not sufficiently complete or disciplined.
Significance (5.0/10)
The problem addressed is important: reducing false positives and expensive evaluation in materials discovery is unquestionably relevant. If the reported gains were robust and the framework truly general, the practical implications could be meaningful across autonomous discovery pipelines. However, the current evidence base is too thin to support strong claims of field-level impact or substantial advancement over existing practice. The conceptual direction is potentially useful and timely, but significance remains speculative because the manuscript does not convincingly demonstrate that the proposed framing changes what practitioners can do beyond existing cost-aware screening workflows.
Overall Verdict (2.5/10)
This submission presents an interesting and potentially useful framing—prioritizing falsification in autonomous materials discovery—but the manuscript does not meet the standards needed to substantiate its technical, novelty, or literature-positioning claims. The method is underspecified, the experimental evidence is insufficiently documented, and the related-work discussion is too narrow to justify the impression of originality. In particular, the paper overstates separation from prior active-learning and screening-based materials discovery approaches while failing to cite and compare against the most relevant literature. I therefore cannot recommend acceptance in its current form, though the underlying idea may merit resubmission after a substantially more rigorous technical and scholarly treatment.

Technical Quality (1.0/10)

Related work discussion is sparse and does not adequately position the contribution against active learning, screening cascades, sequential testing, Bayesian decision theory, or broader materials discovery pipelines that already use cheap-to-expensive filters. Because the comparisons and citations are limited, it is difficult to tell whether this is a genuinely new methodological advance or primarily a reframing of known ideas with a new narrative. The novelty claims therefore feel overstated relative to the evidence presented.

The framework also appears broad enough to extend beyond the specific benchmark used here. As written, the paper suggests a promising direction, but the evidence base is not yet strong enough to fully justify the scale of its implied impact.
Overall Verdict (4.2/10)

However, the manuscript does not meet a strong technical standard: the method is under-specified, the evaluation is insufficiently documented, baseline fairness is unclear, and key claims are not stress-tested through ablations or deeper analysis. The presentation is persuasive, but too much of the contribution remains conceptual rather than rigorously demonstrated. I would recommend rejection in its current form, while encouraging the authors to resubmit a substantially expanded version with precise methodological details, stronger experimental design, and a more complete related-work positioning.
Reviewer D
Tech: 1.0 Nov: 1.0 Clar: 5.0 Sig: 6.0 Overall: 3.2
Technical Quality (1.0/10)
The central idea is interesting, but the technical presentation does not meet a high evidentiary standard. The paper gives only a sketch of the architecture and a single Bayes factor equation, without precise definitions of hypotheses, likelihood construction, surrogate features, policy-network training, screening thresholds beyond two fixed cutoffs, or the exact composition of each evaluation tier. Key assumptions are not articulated or justified, especially the validity of low-cost filters as falsification evidence for the target property and the calibration of GP-derived likelihoods for Bayes factors. Reproducibility is poor: no hyperparameters, no model architectures, no dataset split details, no candidate-generation protocol, no compute budget, and no resource accounting are provided. From a feasibility standpoint, the claimed cost savings are not auditable because the baseline, tuning parity, and wall-clock estimation methodology are underspecified. I also see methodological weaknesses: the benchmark uses labels defined by expensive HSE06 calculations while the proposed pipeline is framed as reducing expensive evaluation, yet it is unclear how training and validation avoid leakage or circularity. There is no ablation isolating the contribution of the falsification tiers, thresholds, surrogate model, or information-gain policy. The only clearly positive aspect is that the falsification-first framing is a potentially meaningful design innovation.
Novelty (1.0/10)
The paper's main novelty claim is the explicit framing of materials discovery as falsification-first Bayesian elimination rather than confirmatory selection. That framing is plausible as a conceptual contribution, but the manuscript does not convincingly establish novelty relative to existing active learning, screening cascades, Bayesian decision-making, and sequential triage pipelines in materials discovery. Related work coverage is thin and not discussed in sufficient depth to show what is genuinely new versus a relabeling of known cost-aware screening ideas. Comparisons are not comprehensive, and novelty claims are overstated given the limited methodological specificity and lack of direct evidence that the proposed elements together constitute more than an incremental variant.
Clarity (5.0/10)
The paper is readable at a high level and the problem, motivation, and claimed contributions are easy to grasp. The prose is generally clear and accessible, and readers from adjacent areas could understand the intended message. However, the manuscript is structurally sloppy: duplicated section headers, placeholder-like "See introduction" text under Methods and Results, and the absence of figures or tables materially reduce clarity. Notation is minimal rather than well developed, and some core concepts are left vague enough that an expert cannot reliably reconstruct the method. The results are stated plainly, but they are not contextualized with enough experimental detail to make the presentation truly clear.
Significance (6.0/10)
The problem is important and highly relevant: reducing false positives and expensive evaluation in materials discovery would have real practical value. If the claims held under rigorous validation, the approach could matter for computational screening workflows and possibly broader scientific-discovery agents. The reported gains are numerically large enough to attract attention, and the conceptual emphasis on early elimination could inspire follow-on work. That said, the current paper does not yet demonstrate a substantial, implementation-ready advance over existing practice. Because the empirical support is thin and the deployment details are absent, I am not convinced the community could adopt or build on this version without substantial additional work.
Overall Verdict (3.2/10)
This paper addresses an important problem and proposes an intuitively appealing falsification-first perspective for materials discovery. However, from a feasibility and implementation standpoint, the manuscript is far too underspecified to support its claims: experimental protocol, model details, baseline fairness, resource accounting, and failure modes are not reported at the level needed for confidence or reproduction. The empirical improvements may be promising, but they are not presented with sufficient rigor to justify strong conclusions. My recommendation is rejection in its current form, with encouragement to resubmit after substantially strengthening methodological detail, comparative evaluation, and practical validation.
